Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Zelman Cowen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, one of Her Majesty's Counsel learned in the law, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: SECOND PERIOD)
Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.7 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Advisory Councilfor Inter-government Relations Act1976.
Airlines Agreement Act 1981 (Act No. 75 of 1981 )An Act to approve the execution of an agreement relating to air transport, and for purposes connected therewith.
Airlines Equipment Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 77 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Airlines Equipment Act 1958.
Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Act 1981 (Act No. 38 of 1981). An Act relating to guarantees of certain borrowings by a domestic airline.
Antarctic Marine Living Resources Conservation Act 1981 (Act -No. 30 of 1981 ) An Act relating to the conservation of marine living resources of the Antarctic and its surrounding seas.
Apple and Pear Export Underwriting Act 1981 (Act No. 14of 1981 )An Act relating to the underwriting of returns from the export from Australia of apples and pears.
Apple and Pear Stabilization Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 15 of 1981)An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Stabilization Act 1971, to repeal certain related Acts, and for other purposes.
Appropriation Act (No.3) 1980-81 (Act No. 55 of 1981 )An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No. I) 1980-81, for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1981.
Appropriation Act (No.4) 1980-81 (Act No. 56 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund, additional to the sum appropriated by the Appropriation Act (No.2) 1980-81 ,for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1961.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 16 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Gaming and Liquor Authority Act 1981 (Act No. 116of 1981)An Act to establish a Gaming and Liquor Authority for the Australian Capital Territory and for related purposes.
Australian Federal Police Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 22 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Federal Police Act1979.
Australian National Airlines Repeal Act 1981 (Act No. 78 of 1981)An Act to repeal the Australian National Airlines Actl945 and for related purposes.
Australian National University Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 106 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian National University Act 1946.
Australian Tourist Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 27 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Australian Tourist Commission Act 1967.
Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 49 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Beef Industry (Incentive Payments) Act 1977.
Bounty (Non-adjustable Wrenches) Act 1981 (Act No. 18 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain wrenches.
Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 17 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Bounty (Polyester-Cotton Yarn) Act 1978, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Printed Fabrics) Act 1981 (Act No. 102of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain printed fabrics.
Bounty (Textile Yarns) Act 1981 (Act No. 103 of 1981)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain textile yarns.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 113 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 198) (Act No. 114 of 1981 )An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964 to extend it to certain licences.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 48 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905.
Christmas Island Agreement Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 107 of 1981)- An Act relating to the Christmas Island Agreement.
Coal Excise Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 19 of 1981)An Act to amend the Coal Excise Act 1949, and for other purposes.
Commonwealth Banks Act 1981 (Act No. 29of 1981).An Act to amend the Commonwealth Banks Act 1959, and for related purposes.
Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 26 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Employees (Redeployment and Retirement) Act 1979.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued

Commonwealth Functions (Statutes Review) Act 1981 (Act No. 74 ofl981 ) An Act to implement certain changes in Commonwealth functions.

Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 62 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Legal Aid Commission Act1977.

Commonwealth Teaching Service Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.5 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commonwealth Teaching Service Act 1972, and for purposes connected therewith.

Companies Act 1981 (Act No. 89 of 1981 ) An Act to make provision for the government of the Australian Capital Territory in relation to the formation of companies, the regulation of companies formed in that Territory, the registration in that Territory of certain other bodies and certain other matters.
Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No.2 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 94 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares) Act 1980.

Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 95 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Companies (Acquisition of Shares-Fees) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 4 of 1981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies and Securities (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Amendment Act (No. 2) of 1981 (Act No. 98ofl981)An Act to amend the Companies and Securit-ies (Interpretation and Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1980.

Companies (Fees) Actl981 (Act No. 90of 1981 ) An Act relating to fees payable for the purposes of the Companies Actl98l.

Companies (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 92 of 1981 ) -

.

An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981 and for other

purposes.

Companies (Transitional Provisions) Act 1981 (Act No. 91 of 1981 )An Act to enact transitional provisions consequent upon the enactment of the Companies Act 1981.

Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act 1981 (Act No. 21 of 1981)An Act relating to complaints made in respect of members of the Australian Federal Police, and for related purposes.

Conciliation and Arbitration Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 71 of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.

Crown Debts (Priority) Act 1981 (Act No. 93 of 1981)- An Act relating to the priority of Crown debts.

Currency Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. II of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Currency Act 1965.

Customs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 64of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901.

Customs Amendment (Securities) Act 1981 (Act No. 67 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Customs Actl901 in relation to securities.

Customs Amendment (Tenders) Act 1981 (Act No. 45 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 to provide for tenders for rights to import goods at concessional rates of duly, and for related purposes.

Customs Securities (Penalties) Act 1981 (A.:t No. 46 of 1981 )An Act to provide for penalties for refusal or failure to give certain securities with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Customs Tariff Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 68 of 1981 ) - An Act to amend the Customs Tariff Act1966.

Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 66of 1981 )- An Act to amend the Customs Tari.ff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975.

Customs TarilfValidation Act 1981 (Act No. 87 of 1981 ) An Act to provide for the validation of certain collections of duty of Customs.

Customs Undertakings (Penalties) Act 1981 (Act No. 47 of 1981)An Act to provide for penalties for breaches of undertakings with respect to the importation into Australia of dutiable goods.

Dairying Research Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 12 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Dairying Research Act 1972, and for related purposes.

Designs Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 42 of 1981 ) An Act to amend the Designs Act 1906, and for related purposes.

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1509

Thursday, 9 April 1981

by Mr Bradfield. Petition received.

Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 10.30 a.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS
The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Diabetics: Insulin
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled, the petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That in view of the recent standardisation of insulin and insulin syringes, having the effect of increasing the cost of both items to the diabetic in general, and as every insulindependent diabetic must use the same, daily or twice daily according to medication.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that syringes be made available on the N.H.S. and that insulin prescriptions revert to 10 bottles as previously supplied.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Beazley, Mr Campbell, Dr Cass, Mr Cunningham, Mr Dawkins, Mr Duffy, Mr Hawke, Mr Howe, Mr Jacobi, Dr Jenkins, Mr Barry Jones, Mr Mildren and Mr Milton.
Petitions received.
National Women's Advisory Council
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled: The Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the National Women's Advisory Council has not been democratically elected by the women of Australia;
That the National Women's Advisory Council is not representative of the women of Australia;
That the National Women's Advisory Council is a discriminatory and sexist imposition on Australian women as Australian men do not have a National Men's Advisory Council imposed on them.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the National Women's Advisory Council be abolished to ensure that Australian women have equal opportunity with Australian men of having issues of concern to them considered, debated and voted on by their Parliamentary representatives without intervention and interference by an unrepresentative "Advisory Council".
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Donald Cameron and Mr Millar.
Petitions received.
National Day of Prayer and Fasting
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives, in Parliament Assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that: In view of the Spiritual, and Moral decline of our Nation we your Petitioners therefore. humbly pray that Parliament will call a day of National Prayer and Fasting in 1981.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Medical Records: Confidentiality
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled the petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That we are gravely concerned by the invasion of privacy caused by Government agents seizing patients' medical records.
Your petitioners most humbly pray that the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled, should:
Legislate to protect the private and confidential nature of medical records from scrutiny except on the express and informed consent of the patient or an order from a presiding judge.
And-your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Cadman.
Petition received.
Metric System
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That the plan to obliterate the traditional weights and measures of this country does not have the support of the people;
That the change is causing and will continue to cause, widespread, serious and costly problems;
That the compulsory tactics being used to force the change are a violation of all democratic principles.
Your petitioners therefore pray:
That the Metric Conversion Act be repealed to ensure that the people are free to utilize whichever system they prefer and so enable the return to imperial weights and measures wherever the people so desire;
That weather reporting be as it was prior to the passing of the Metric Conversion Act;
That the Australian Government take urgent steps to cause the traditional mile units to be restored to our highways;
That the Australian Government request the State Governments to procure that the imperial and metric systems be taught together in schools.
And your petitioners, as in duty bound, will ever pray.
by Mr Chapman.
Petition received.
Toxic Shock Syndrome
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned showeth:
That your petitioners are gravely concerned by the threats of women's health and lives posed by Toxic Shock Syndrome, and urge the following steps to be taken as a matter of urgency:
(1) Tampoons, sanitary napkins, vaginal deodorants and similar sanitary products to be re-classified under Therapeutic Agents (not under "Cosmetics" as at present), in order to bring them under the scrutiny of the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee. We believe manufacture must be under the strictest hygienic conditions.

1510 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Petitions

(2) After re-classification, we urge an immediate government-funded inquiry into all aspects of manufacture and content of these products. This must lead to: (i) contents of product and instructions for correct usage to be shown on packaging; (ii) removal of synthetic fibres, chemicals or ingredients that are potentially or actually harmful to users.
(3) Allocation of extra funding for ongoing research by the National Health and Medical Research Council into these products, results of this research to be made public to ensure all health workers and potential users are fully informed. This campaign of public education would necessitate multilingual distribution of the information where appropriate.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take immediate and positive action to avert possible threats posed to women's health by implementation of the above measures.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child.
Petition received.

Service Pensions

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of

men and fifty-five years of age for women regardless of where they served during the 1939-45 war.

Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled -should give urgent
consideration to a favourable decision to the granting of the Service Pension to all Ex-Service Personnel who served their country in time of need and who did not serve overseas through no fault of their own. The Service Pension is
presently only granted to Returned Ex-Service Personnel.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

by Mrs Child.

Service Pensions
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
(1) That merchant seamen have in the past served in - theatres of war; they have been decorated for valour following
war action; they have been prisoners of war and should therefore be entitled to similar pension rights and medical benefits as regular servicemen.
(2) That by being denied the entitlements, such as pension and medical benefits, which other war veterans receive, they are thereby being discriminated against.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government amend the Repatriation Act (1920) Section 100 (d) (iv) to take account of war-related service by merchant seamen during World War 2.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.

Moreton Island: Sand Mining
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
1. That sandmining on Moreton Island poses a threat to the natural ecology or-environment.
2. That Moreton Island is included on the interim list of the Australian Heritage Commission, and the Federal Government is urged and indeed it must, under the Australian Heritage Commission Act 1975, take into consideration the protection of the island.
3. That the Queensland Government has recently renewed a further mining lease against the advice of conservationists.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House will request the Government to refuse requests for export permits covering mineral sand products.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.

Australian Broadcasting Commission: Melbourne Showband
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the Australian Parliament in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Victoria respectfully showeth that:
As regular viewers and listeners of the ABC Melbourne Showband we were disappointed to learn of the dismissal of seventeen members of this outstanding orchestra.
We have had a great deal of pleasure over the past eight years viewing and listening to this barid's fine music. It is the only full-time light orchestra in Australia and we are disgusted to think that such talented musicians should be dismissed in this manner.
We therefore call on the Federal Government to rescind this decision and make more money available for the continuance of the full Showband.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Hawke and Mr Hodgman.

~d.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
RUNDLE SHALE OIL PROJECT
Mr KEATING-I ask the Prime Minister whether, to support claims by the Government that it knew of the present state of the Rundle investigation on 18 February of this year, he will table the letter that Senator Carrick said he had addressed to the Prime Minister expressing disquiet at the progress of Rundle. Will he also table the advice of the Attorney-General's Department, which Senator Carrick claims urged the Government not to take the initiative and publicise the current status of the Rundle venture? If the Prime Minister is not prepared to table the letters, can he tell the House precisely why?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-There were discussions involving Senator Carrick, myself, the Treasurer and I think one or two other Ministers, in relation to this particular matter and also in relation to the appropriate course that the Government should take in these circumstances-where obviously there are circumstances of interest to the stock exchange and to the wider public community. It was at a time when the Rundle partners themselves were still seeking to sort out the facts to see to what extent they had a common view of the circumstance, and the changed circumstance, of the Rundle operation. Mr Speaker, could I take the slightly unusual course of asking the Treasurer to add to this answer, because he had, more directly, conversations with the persons involved than I did and also wrote communications which are more directly relevant. I know that the Treasurer is willing to table those particular letters, which would express' quite clearly the Government's concerns and interests.
Mr SPEAKER-I call the Treasurer.
Mr HOWARD-It is true, as Senator Carrick has already indicated, that arising out of a visit that he paid to Gladstone in, I think, about the third week of February, there was a discussion between Esso Australia Ltd and the Prime Minister and senior Ministers. Arising out of that discussion, I addressed a letter dated 27 February to Mr McFarlane, the chairman and managing director of Southern Pacific Petroleum.

Questions Without Notice

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1513

I seek leave to have the letter incorporated in Hansard.
Leave granted.
The document read as follows-
TREASURER
PARLIAMENT HOUSE CANBERRA 2600
27 Feb. 1981
Mr I. McFarlane Chairman and Managing Director Southern Pacific Petroleum NL 143 Macquarie Street, SYDNEY, NSW 2000
Dear Mr McFarlane
It has been brought to the notice of the Government that ongoing investigatory work in relation to the Rundle shale oil project has thrown up certain problems.
It is understood that those problems extend to both the mining and processing sides of the proposed venture and that they have implications for the timetable and scale of operations of the project-and, perhaps, for its overall economic viability. I believe that your technical people are currently examining the additionai information.
The Government is naturally concerned that any significant new information bearing upon the future of the Rundle project be brought immediately to the attention of the relevant stock exchanges in accordance with normal listing requirements. Without in any way inferring that you would act differently, I should like you to be aware of the Government's continuing interest in Rundle and of its concern that investors on the stock exchanges have access to all relevant information on the project as it becomes available.
Yours sincerely,
JOHN HOWARD
Copy to:
The Rt Hon. J. M. Fraser, C.H., M.P. The Prime Minister Parliament House CANBERRA, ACT 2600
The Rt Hon. J. D; Anthony, M.P. Senator the Hon. J. L. Carrick
Mr HOWARD-The thrust of that letter was that the Government had been made aware of the views of one of the partners that the prospects for the project were significantly different from what was then the general public conception. It was our view that, whatever disclosure should be made, should be made promptly, in the interests of keeping the investing public fully informed. I quote from that letter; it states:
The Government is naturally concerned that any significant new information bearing upon the future of the Rundle project be brought immediately to the attention of the relevant stock exchanges in accordance with normal listing requirements.
That is the letter I addressed to the Southern Pacific Petroleu;m company on 27 February. I set out very much the desire of the Government, immediately on being made aware of concerns felt by one of the Rundle partners, for the public to be kept fully informed. Subsequent to that, there was

a response to that letter and a discussion between myself and an officer of my Department and the Minister for National Development and Energy and an officer of his Department. Arising from that, at my request, advice from the Crown Solicitor's Office was obtained. Subsequent to that, I had a personal discussion with the Solicitor General and oral advice was conveyed to me by him. The effect of that advice-if I report it incorrectly I will so advise the House-was that there was no legal obligation upon the Government at that stage to convey unilaterally to the stock exchanges what it had been told by Esso Australia Ltd, and that the proper course of action was to urge the parties to resolve their differences as quickly as possible with a view to making a full and complete disclosure. As a consequence of that discussion and further advice that I obtained, I wrote again to Southern Pacific Petroleum and also to Mr Kirk, the managing director of Esso, on 20 March. I seek leave to have those two letters incorporated in Hansard.
Leave granted.
The documents read as follows-
TREASURER PARLIAMENT HOUSE
CANBERRA 2600
20 March, 1981
Dear Mr McFarlane,
In the light of recent separate meetings between Commonwealth Ministers and the Joint Venturers in the Rundle Oil Project, it is clear that there are considerable differences between the Joint Venturers both. on estimates of capital costs and on certain technical aspects of the operation.
I understand that arrangements have been made for the respective parties' engineers and other technical experts to consult and sort out the facts and their implications. I wish to reiterate the Government's concern to ensure that the disagreements on costs and technical aspects are resolved at the earliest opportunity so that investors on the stock exchanges can be informed of all relevant information..
In the intervening period, however, I suggest that the stock exchanges be informed of the differences between the Joint Venturers and also that these matters are being examined with a view to making a public announcement on their resolution.
In view of the obvious importance attached by the Government to this matter, I should appreciate your letting me know as soon as possible what you propose to do in response to the suggestions made in this letter.
I have written in similar terms to Mr Kirk.
Yours sincerely,
JOHN HOWARD
Mr I. McFarlane, Chairman and Managing Director, Southern Pacific Petroleum NL, GPO Box 4024, SYDNEY, NSW 2000

UNION DEMARCATION DISPUTES Mr BOURCHIER-Can the Minister for Housing and Construction advise the House whether major Commonwealth projects are being affected by the current high incidence of union demarcation disputes? If so, will he provide the House with details of these disputes?
Mr McVEIGH-The short answer to the honourable member's question is yes. I would like to take a few minutes of the time of the House to indicate to honourable members some of the problems that have occurred through the raising of senseless demarcation issues. They are denying jobs to Australian workers. They are also denying services to Australian consumers as well as adding significantly to the costs associated with building. My Department has in progress at present jobs costing in excess of $500m. Four of those projects-namely, a $34m project at the Exhibition telephone exchange in Melbourne, a $9m project at the telephone exchange in North Sydney, a $6.8m service information centre at North Sydney and a $4.5m stores complex at Kingswood, Sydney-are being held up by demarcation issues. That is to be deplored.
Additionally, I would remind the House that a dispute at Bonegilla held up defence installation works costing $34m. It is pleasing for me to advise the House that, following solid and determined leadership by the Minister for Industrial Relations, work at the Omega site was resumed. This project is to cost $9.7m. I am delighted to be able to advise interested people that at 9 o'clock this morning the height of the tower at Omega was 190 metres and that at 10 o'clock it was 199.56 metres, indicating that Australian workers can do the job even at those heights. I am hopeful that the complete tower of 427 metres will be finished in time to allow that navigational aid to be functional by next year. Finally, I make the point that it is about time those who sit opposite supported the efforts of the Minister for Industrial Relations to give more jobs to Australian people and to allow costs to be contained. Why do they deny jobsto their fellow workers?
RUNDLE SHALE OIL PROJECT Mr KEATING-I ask the Treasurer to supply us with another answer in relation to the Rundle project. Why was it that the Government felt compelled to be so involved in commercial arrangements with two Australian companies and an international company in Rundle, that is Central Pacific Minerals and Southern Pacific Petroleum and Esso Australia Ltd, that it had the

Prime Minister, the Minister for National Development and Energy, the Treasurer, the Attorney General and the Solicitor-General involved in what was, after all, a feasibility study-one among a number of feasibilities studies currently proceeding-for the development of a resource? Is the Treasurer aware that there was enormous trading in Rundle shares on 19 February? Is he also aware that by his own statement he did not advise the companies until nine days after the advice from Senator Carrick? He might tell us also why Senator Carrick actually had to go to the site to get that advice. Finally, why will the Government not table Senator Carrick's letter to the Prime Minister or the advice to the Treasurer from the Attorney-General?
Mr HOWARD-I would not have thought that it was a matter of critical comment on this Government that a number of senior Ministers as well as the Prime. Minister should be concerned about a project of such potentially great significance to the energy future of this country. To suggest that there is something odd about the Prime Minister and my colleagues, the Minister for Trade and Resources, the Minister for National Development and Energy and myself being concerned about the project trivialises the whole issue. Our actions at every stage were the actions of a government having been made aware, through the visit of Senator Carrick to Queensland in February, of the possibility that the original projections and projections on which investment decisions were being based on Australian stock exchanges could in fact be different. We took every reasonable step, with proper ex- . pedition, to get the companies to resolve their differences and to advise the current situation which they did by the public statement last wee~.
Mr Keating-Is this normal practice?
Mr HOWARD-The honourable member for Blaxland asks me--
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member for Blaxland will remain silent.
Mr Keating-Let him tell us where else he has done this.
Mr SPEAKER~The honourable member for Blaxland will remain silent.
Mr HOWARD-The honourable member for Blaxland asks me whether I will table an opinion of the Attorney-General or the Solicitor-General. I obtained oral advice from the Solicitor-General. I said that in my earlier answer. The sequence of events was that having written the initial letter that I tabled and having had a discussion with Southern Pacific Petroleum NLin response to

1516 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Questions Without Notice

that letter, I sought, through my own Department, the advice of the Crown Solicitor's Division or the Attorney-General's Department. I forget which of those two bodies it was but I will find out if the honourable gentleman is interested and let him know so that he cannot base some other question on my inability to answer that part of his question.
Mr Leo McLeay-What about Senator Carrick's letter?
Mr HOWARD-I will come to that in a moment. Having had that discussion we then obtained the advice, through my Department, of the Crown Solicitor as to whether any further action might be necessary on the part of the Government in the light of information that it had been given. That is not the action of a government trying to cover up something. It is the action of a government doing everything that it ought to do to make sure that the public is fully informed of the facts. As a consequence of that, at the request of the Attorney-General's Department the Solicitor-General contacted me by telephone in Sydney. His advice to me was that the action that I had taken was proper and that he did not believe that there was any legal obligation on me unilaterally to notify the stock exchanges of the position. Indeed, his advice was that in advance of the companies' being given a reasonable opportunity to resolve their differences it might well be a point of criticism of the Government if it had unilaterally conveyed that information to the stock exchanges.
It subsequently transpired that the concerns expressed by Esso to us were misplaced in certain material respects. We would then have been open to the very legitimate criticism that in fact we had over-reacted to the situation. I believe that in all the circumstances they were the proper actions and responses of a government not trying to cover something up but a government determined by its own actions to make the information available and to see that it was available promptly. So far as the letter from Senator Carrick to the Prime Minister is concerned, that letter contained a report from Senator Carrick in very technical terms of his discussions with the Esso people in Gladstone.
Mr Keating-Let us have it.
Mr HOWARD-As the honourable gentleman knows, it is the long-standing practice not to table communications between Ministers. That letter merely reported on the technical aspects of the matter. It was arising out of that letter that the discussion took place between Mr Kirk, the Prime Minister and other senior Ministers of the Government. The letters that I have already

tabled, which clearly indicate the position of the Government despite the attempts by the Opposition to indicate otherwise, also arose from that letter and that discussion. There can be absolutely no reflection on the actions of the Government.
CONCILIATION AND ARBITRATION ~CT AND REGULATIONS
Mr WHITE-My question is directed to the Minister for Industrial Relations. On 4 December the Minister informed this House that his Department would initiate an examination of the Conciliation and Arbitration Act and regulations with a view to simplifying them. Can the Minister inform the House of the progress of that review?
Mr PEACOCK-I did indicate on that occasion the need for such a review and the Department of Industrial Relations did commence a review of the Conciliation and Arbitration Act. I have also asked' Mr Richard Searby of the Victorian Bar and former Conciliation and Arbitration Commission Deputy President Taylor to act as consultants to the Department in the exercise. They will, I am sure, be of considerable assistance to the Department. I have asked the Department, and the consultants in particular, to try to complete their examination by approximately the end of July. That will be a difficult assignment because of the state of the Act at present.
I envisage that the review will encompass such questions as whether some provisions of the Act and regulations are any longer necessary, whether procedures can be streamlined and whether certain provisions can be better grouped. These are just examples of aspects to be covered by the review. Certainly, a simplified Act will assist in the conduct of industrial relations and, I hope, enhance, the public's understanding of the processes involved. Unquestionably the review is long overdue. Practitioners in the field have long complained about the state of the Act. Throughout the field of industrial relations I think this review will be welcomed. People have expressed their concern about the number of amendments over the years that have resulted in certainly a complicated and cumbersome Act. I once described it as an Act that is like a can of worms. One !Jewspaper said that I did not go far enough and that it should be described as a bag of snakes. I would hope that if the review is completed by the end of July, I can inform the House of the progress made in reporting appropriately to peak employer and union groups through the National Labour Consultative Council.

Mr NEWMAN-The honourable gentleman has asked an obviously very important question. He is asking for details of our contingency planning and our operational ability to respond to terrorism. Mr Spender-I did not ask for details; just whether the Minister was satisfied.
Mr NEWMAN-I can assure the honourable member and the House that there is a national anti-terrorist plan which is regularly examined, up-dated and tested by regular exercises. The plan does encompass hijacking incidents and involves other Ministers responding in addition to the Minister for Transport.

TAX DEDUCTIONS: ART DONATIONS
Mr WILLIS~I address a question to the Treasurer. The question refers to reports regarding tax deductions for art donations in which it is alleged that a syndicate of Melbourne businessmen and stockbrokers who donated a collection of pre-Colombian sculptures, ceramics and stoneware to the National Gallery of Victoria has obtained a tax deduction of $3.7m and a collective tax benefit of $2.2m for an outlay of only $1.2m. I

ask: In view of the fact that a world authority on pre-Colombian art has ridiculed the valuations on which the tax deduction was based, is the Treasurer satisfied with the system of valuation of art works for the purposes of section 78 of the Income Tax Assessment Act? In particular, does he consider it proper that in this case the person apparently charged with the sole responsibility ~o find and recommend valuers for the particular donation was a director of the Gallery which stood to benefit from the donation? Have the tax authorities investigated the valuation process in this case to see what commissions, if any, were payable to the valuers and, if not, will they do so? In view of the apparent complete misuse of the tax. incentives for the arts scheme, does the Treasurer intend to take action to rectify this situation?
Mr HOWARD-I was concerned, as I am sure the honourable member for Gellibrand was concerned, on learning of these reports. I have already discussed the matter with the Commissioner of Taxation. Naturally the secrecy provisions of the Act preclude my giving certain advice from Mr O'Reilly to me, but I can assure the House that I have asked the Taxation Office to examine what changesmight be necessary in terms of both the legislation and the way in which it is administered, in particular the way in which valuations are done, to prevent the type of situation described by the honourable member from occurring in the future. I make it plain that it is quite unacceptable that it should. I fully share the honourable member's concern on the matter. Action will be taken to stop this type of thing happening again.
AFGHANISTAN
Mr JARMAN-I ask the Minister for Foreign Affairs whether he is aware of today's report that the Soviet Union has increased its forces in Afghanistan by up to 20,000 additional troops. Is this report correct? If so, what implications has this for a peaceful settlement of the Afghan crisis?
Mr STREET-I am aware of reports that the Soviet Union may have substantially added to the 85,000 troops it already has in Afghanistan. I am advised also that the Soviet Union has an estimated 30,000 additional troops just across the border from Afghanistan. At this stage I cannot confirm those reports. Clearly, any further move~ ment of Soviet troops into Afghanistan would increase the level of fighting and the loss of life and lessen whatever opportunity there may be of reaching a peaceful settlement of this problem. We have consistently condemned the Soviet military invasion and occupation of Aghanistan. We

Questions Without Notice

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1519

have supported calls in the United Nations and other forums for the complete and unconditional withdrawal of those troops so that the Afghan people can determine their own future.
It is worth noting that groups of patriots within Afghanistan are resisting, by force of arms, the invasion of their country and their bravery and tenacity has aroused the admiration of the world. Australia has given consistent support to Pakistan which has had to bear the burden of some 1.7 million refugees in its territory. Concern about the possibility of Soviet intervention in Poland should not be allowed to obscure the reality of the Soviet occupation of Afghanistan by force. This must not be allowed to slip from the world's memory.
TAXATION
Mr JACOBI-My question, which is addressed to the Treasurer, refers to his statement two weeks ago regarding the $16.5m tax-free profit to the Bell group of companies for its sale of shares in Elder Smith Goldsbrough Mort Ltd when he said that he would consider whether this transaction revealed a weakness in section 26AAA of the taxation Act. Is he aware that in November 1979 the Bell group made a similar tax-free profit of $11m by selling a subsidiary company, Bell Air Charter, which had purchased shares in Ansett Transport Industries Ltd for the Bell group during a takeover attempt? Is he also aware that in August last year the Bond group used a similar device to make a tax free profit of $32m by selling a subsidiary, Narla prospecting company, which had shortly before purchased shares in the White industries organisation? Was the Treasurer made aware of these blatant manipulations of the taxation Act to avoid taxation? If so, why has he not acted sooner to stop such blatant tax avoidance and what action does he intend to take?
Mr HOWARD-As a result of the transaction to which the honourable member referred and which was the subject of a question asked by the honourable member for Chifley a couple of weeks ago, I sought the advice of the Commissioner of Taxation. For reasons which I know the House will understand, I cannot precisely affirm or deny a particular tax position of an individual tax payee. The circumstances of that matter have brought to light what in my view is a weakness in the operation of section 26AAA. I am in the process of putting together certain recommendations to the Government as to what ought to be done. It is not completely free from complexity and I hope to put those recommendations to the Government shortly.
Mr Armitage-Could you make it retrospective?

Mr HOWARD-I think the Government's position on that point is understood.
AUSTRALIAN BROADCASTING COMMISSION: AUSTRALIAN LABOR
PARTY ELECTION CAMPAIGN
Mr IAN ROBINSON-I direct a question to the Minister for Communications. Is it a fact that a senior reporter with the Australian Broadcasting Commission maintained a virtual open line contact with a senior Australian Labor Party campaign official throughout the 1980 Federal election campaign and that the Australian Labor Party used this link to try to apply pressure on ABC producers? Is it further a fact that the Australian Labor Party was being given advance information about the content of ABC current affairs programs and that the Australian Labor Party through this contact attempted to influence the coverage given to the Government side? Were program producers threatened by the Australian Labor Party? Did they find it necessary to complain to the management? Finally, can the Minister inform the House whether at the end of the election campaign the reporter concerned was a guest at a 'thank you' party put on by the Australian Labor Party?
Mr SINCLAIR-There seems to be a fashion to reveal moles around the place. The honourable gentleman's question certainly suggests that some such person exists within the Australian Broadcasting Commission. I understand, as we all do, that within the Australian community there are opportunities for the expression of a political attitude within the polling box. That is the fit and proper place where a member of the Public Service or someone attached to one of the statutory authorities should express that point of view. I do not believe that those who work within the constraints of one of the public service areas or one of the statutory authorities has the right to exercise the licence that the honourable gentleman's question suggests has been exercised in this instance. I do not know of the illustration, nor do I know the individual. However, I will ask for a report from the general manager of the ABC. I shall respond to the honourable gentleman and to this House when I have received that report.

over recent months, in the absence of clear directions on the future form of the wage system, have created widespread uncertainty. Therefore, it is natural in that situation of uncertainty that businessmen will not employ so many people as they would otherwise. EMPLOYMENT OPPORTUNITIES ARISING FROM WAGE FIXING PRINCIPLES
Mr CHAPMAN-I ask the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs: What are the implications for employment of the wage determination principles adopted by the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission?
Mr VINER-There are important implications for employment in the decision handed down by the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission on national wage fixing principles. I believe that if those principles are observed by the union movement they will strengthen the foundations on which employment is already growing in the community. Pressure for wage increases

Mr VINER-To conclude, fundamentally we require in Australia, while economic growth is occurring, the avoidance of costly industrial disputation. Above all, we cannot afford to see the introduction of a 35-hour week with the destructive effects it would have on the continuing growth in employment. Flowing from the wage determination decision of the Conciliation and Arbitration Commission we ought to see a return to greater stability in the wage determination processes, giving the community an opportunity to build on the gains already made.
AUSTRALIAN PARLIAMENTARY CHRISTIAN FELLOWSHIP
Mr CHAPMAN (Kingston)-by leave-On Tuesday, 24 February 1981, the Thirteenth

Australian Parliamentary Christian Fellowship 9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1521

Annual Service of Worship, arranged by the Australian Parliamentary Christian Fellowship to mark the opening of the 1981 parliamentary year, was held. The Parliamentary Reporting Staff has prepared a transcript of that service. I seek leave to have the transcript incorporated in Hansard.
Leave granted.
The document read as follows-
THE PARLIAMENT OF THE COMMONWEALTH OF AUSTRALIA
THIRTEENTH ANNUAL SERVICE OF WORSHIP
TO MARK THE OPENING OF THE 1981 PARLIAMENTARY YEAR
Held on Tuesday, 24 February 1981,
at Wesley Uniting Church, Forrest,
at9a.m.
This service was arranged by the President and Executive of the Australian Parliamentary Christian Fellowship
The congregation stood as His Excellency the Governor General and the officiating clergy entered the church and remained standing for the first six bars of 'God. Save The Queen'. The congregation was then seated.
Senator DAVIDSON-On this the first sitting day of the 1981 parliamentary year, the Parliamentary Christian Fellowship takes pleasure in welcoming its members and guests to this annual service of worship. This is the thirteenth such occasion and we are mindful of the witness which this yearly observance has made. We are privileged to be identified in this way with the institution of Parliament and we are grateful for all the co-operation which has been extended.
We are especially pleased to have with us this morning His Excellency the Governor-General, the Prime Minister and Mrs Fraser, the Deputy Prime Minister and Mrs Anthony and the Leader of the Opposition representative, Mrs Kelly. The Presiding Officers, members of the Senate and members of the House of Representatives are joined this morning by members of the judiciary, Government representatives, High Commissioners and Ambassadors, Canberra citizens and others who one way or another share in the round of this nation's public affairs. We are encouraged by your presence. We are glad to be back in Wesley Church again. We offer our thanks for hospitality and for so many excellent arrangements that have been made. Similarly we record our gratitude to the group of clergy, known to us as the Canberra Committee, who so willingly and efficiently attend to the many details relating to this occasion.
The relationship between the worlds of Parliament and the worlds of religion are complex and not easily defined. But to people who are called to lead and who influence opinion in Australia it is a relationship which must be constantly examined and appropriately developed. It is here, in this hour, that we take time to look again at the spiritual values which give meaning to our public life. We do this so that this service does not become a meaningless annual custom; rather does it become a source of spiritual strength for the year ahead. So, conscious of our dependence upon divine grace, we are here meeting in quietness and confidence, finding strength in this fellowship and assurance from this association-therefore let us worship God.

Mr HURFORD-The second lesson, to be read from the Living Bible, is from Romans 8 : 24-30:
We are saved by trusting. And trusting means looking forward to getting something we don't yet have-for a man who already has something doesn't need to hope and trust that he will get it. But if we must keep trusting God for something that hasn't happened yet, it teaches us to wait patiently and confidently.
And in the same way-by our faith-the Holy Spirit helps us with our daily problems and in our praying. For we don't even know what we should pray for, nor how to pray as we should; but the Holy Spirit prays for us with such feeling that it cannot be expressed in words. And the Father who knows all hearts knows, of course, what the Spirit is saying as he pleads for us in harmony with God's own will. And we know that all that happens to us is working for our good if we love God and are fitting into his plans.
For from the very beginning God decided that those who came to him-and all along he knew who would-should become like his Son, so that his Son would be the First with many brothers. And having chosen us, he called us to come to him; and when we came, he declared us 'not guilty', filled us with Christ's goodness, gave us right standing with himself, and promised us his glory.
So ends the second lesson.

The setmon was preached by Colonel William Cairns, A.M., Chief Secretary, Australian Eastern Territory, The Salvation Army.

Colonel CAIRNS-The pastor who asked the small boy did he say his prayers at night was rather pleased when the lad said 'Yes, sir, indeed I did'. The pastor had some fresh think-
ing to do when he asked the lad did he say his prayers in the
morning and the boy said 'No, sir, I do not. I am not scared in the morning'.

If we are looking for a theme for the sermon it could well be: 'A source of authority available to all of us'. The well informed members of this congregation hardly need me to say that there are many ingredients in prayer and the only time that we resort to prayer is not when we are anxious. However, having said that, I do suggest that the principle applied by the little boy is often the principle applied by many adults.

In a moment our attention will be focussed on a couple of
verses used by Saint Paul when he refers to prayer. But before doing so, let me say that some very awful things happen to people who say their prayers. It is one thing to think of prayer
as a means of comfort, but when we look into this Book of authority we find that some dreadful things happened to great
men who undergirded their lives with prayer. They finished up in strange places, indeed they finished up in the very places that were not of their design.

In this particular generation there has been a rediscovery in prayer. There are some very doubtful religions which proliferate rather profitably today and which offer prayer in various ways such as meditation, auto suggestion, chanting, recollection-just to name a few. People are convinced that there is a spiritual realm to be tapped as a serenity source. Prayer is being marketed as a kind of commodity thing, a conveyor of tranquility. It is one thing to talk of having faith and being led of the Holy Spirit or having peace. It is, yet quite another thing to walk in faith, to be led as was Jesus Christ by the Holy Spirit and to have a tranquil spirit in all kinds of disturbing situations. There is abroad a perverted kind of an idea that people can consume God's power. Anyone who wrestles with God in prayer, and that is a very good Old Testament phrase, may well put their life at stake. For instance in the New Testament after Mary's magnificant prayer at the annunciation, she did not become a heroine of the Nazareth society but rather a pariah. The well worn phrase that prayer changes things, meant for comfort, can be a tricky bit of jargonindeed.
If you look at the Old Testament character of Daniel, for instance, who spent three times a day in prayer, you find that he finished up very near to the jaws of lions. If you look, at another one, Jeremiah-of whom at last one expositor has said that he was the greatest, healthiest character of the Old Testament-you find that he finished up in a pit. If I might refer you to the fifth chapter of Jeremiah, you could almost be reading a daily paper as you read of carelessness of sacred things, the 'God is dead' idea, false statements by people who influence and mould public opinion, gregarious lawlessness and unfaithfulness in human behaviour with neighbours. It is all there in that fifth chapter and yet prayer undergirded this extraordinary prophet of God during 40 years of ministry

when his policy did not change at any time. But this man finished up in a pit. Yet another is the most powerful and the greatest authority of all time, who yet has not been superseded nor has his authority been surpassed, who arose early in the morning a great time before it was day to pray and he finished upon a cross.
So prayer is not just a comfort commodity. It can bring comfort. It can bring lasting comfort to people who know how to use prayer, but I see in this Book of authority that, wisely used, it is a mind changer, an attitude conditioner, a fortifier of obedience. I am talking of an authority that can come to people by the use of prayer.
Dr Robert Schuller, who is becoming increasingly popular in Australia through his Hour of Power broadcasts and television programs, has a great gift for a simple playing with words and he says that God answers prayer in a number of ways. He says to some 'No', when such a prayer is of no value to the pray-er or to people for whom he prays; or he may answer 'Go', when the prayer is of value to the petitioner and to others; or yet he may answer 'Slow', when the timing is bad; and yet he may answer 'Grow', when the supplicant needs to mature in faith and in an understanding of what his prayers are all about. Now each of these kinds of responses I can find in my Bible. There are simple illustrations of it. The basis of all my words this morning are taken from this Book.
I believe, with many people, that the world today detests authority and rather worships relevance. There are some Christians who claim that this Book has authority. There are many who fear that it has and wish that it did not have. There are some people who wish that this Book, the Bible, had relevance and fear that it does not. I say this morning that I believe it has both and both the authority and the relevance of which I speak are to be found in the person of Jesus Christ, the Son of God. Jesus Christ, of course, is timelesS.
It is a fashionable thing in sermons today to introduce personal testimony. I say the authority which I find in this Book and which I find in prayer is not an oppressive one but rather a liberating one. So here this morning, taking the authority of Saint Paul-his authority is Jesus Christ-I refer you to just two verses. One is found in the letter that he wrote to the Church at Rome. At the time he wrote it was but a small group of Christians. Taking the Living Bible which was read to us earlier, he states:
. . . the Holy Spirit helps us with our daily problems and in our praying. For we don't even know what we should pray for, or how we should pray . . .
We are talking about non-specific prayer now. Those of you who are familiar with your Authorised Version will remember that the reading is 'the Spirit helpeth us in our infirmities'. Some of the new translations, for instance, the Jerusalem Bible and the New International Bible, speak of the Holy Spirit helping us with our weaknesses. Let us take the plain approach to it in the paraphrase read to us this morning: The Holy Spirit helps us with our daily problems and in our praying, espeCially when we do not know how to pray.
Now there are many things that level people in a congregation of this kind and one of them is that from time to time we feel a sense of inadequacy within ourselves. I was at a students' dedication service the other evening and we sang a hymn that said: 'And when my faith and will are weak, sustain me to the end'. Now being in a Wesleyan church I would take the liberty of using a Wesleyan illustration. John Wesley was standing in a farmyard speaking to a farmer on one occasion and he saw a cow leaning up and holding her head over a stone wall. John Wesley asked the farmer the simple question: 'Why is the cow leaning on and looking over the wall like that?' The farmer, being a very simple man, gave a very simple answer: 'She is looking over the wall because she cannot see through the wall'.

1524 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981 Australian Parliamentary Christian Fellowship

With the problems and setbacks, the disenchantments, the
rejections, disappointments and discouragements, that we all have from time to time, we seem to ,be up against a stone wall experience, a God-forsaken experience.. To be explicit in prayer would be very difficult and it is good sense to apply what has now become the famous Wesleyan principle of the cow and the stone wall-you just wait. This is what the Bible
says. Paul writes to the Church at Rome arid says: 'You do not know how to pray; you do not know what to say. Then just hold on. The Holy Spirit will help you in your problems'. You
know that this is not just the teach.ing of Jesus Christ or of Saint Paul. It was also the teaching of'socrates who said to his
disciples: 'Do not engage in specific prayer, There is one who
knows the end from the beginning and knows that which we do not know. Do not engage in specific prayer'.

Let me speak about specific prayer. When Paul was writing
to a young church that had faced opposition and was formed and forged in the fires of persecution, he said:

Be careful for nothing; but in every thing by prayer and sup-

plication with thanksgiving let your requests .be made

known unto God.

'

IfI may quote from Canon J. B. Phillip's translation, he says:

Tell God every detail of your needs in earnest and thankful

prayer, and the peace of God, which transcends human

understanding, will keep constant guard over your hearts

and minds as they rest in Christ Jesus.

.

That seems to be a contradiction to the principle of nonspecific prayer. He is writing to two different sets of people in different circumstances. It is not a contradiction. There is a
time for all of us to be specific in prayer. There is the man here who knows the way that he must take. As the Bible says, he hears a voice in his mind that says: 'This is the way, walk ye in
it'. He has the authority of knowing where he is going, what he is doing. He has the compulsion within him to direct him. Now Paul says: 'Let that man seek also, the value of a higher authority and speak. to God the details of that particular plan so
that on the road that he is taking he will be at peace and without anxiety. As confident as any man is in the line that he
takes, let him always appeal to a greater authority to help him'.

Now Moses of all men was a leader of unique authority. The whole of his life was undergirded by prayer and those of us here who can recall seeing the film 'The Ten
Commandments'may recall the incident wheri Moses came down from the mountain after communion with God. The Bible says that his face shone. The script writer of 'The Ten
Commandments'-not all that come out of Hollywood is bad-wrote this beautiful statement and had Moses say: 'I do not fully know the power that drives me on, but I know the way that I must take'. This man who had been in communion
with God knew the way that he must take, having been in conversation with a greater authority.

There are those of us here who are responsible' for working for purer and juster laws; for peace between nations, for the
healing of disease, for the relief of poverty, for the rescuing of the fal1en and for the moulding of public opinion in our time. The hope of many people out there is that such privileged and
chosen people as we are will reinforce that authority by the authority that comes from. prayer, ,whether it be non-specific prayer on occasions when we just hold on, or when we talk to God the specific details of our plan. I see the apostle and I see Christ encouraging us from scriptures to do this.

Many of us have been in the Elephant and Castle area of
London and we have gone past Charles Spurgeon's church. He was contemporary with Gladstone and he wrote to Prime Minister Gladstone on one occasion and said:

I believe in no man's infallibility but it is restful to believe in one man's integrity, such integrity essentially coming from a man's character but kept pure and clean by prayer.

My prayer for this congregation is aptly described in a beautiful poem by Albert Osborne who wrote:
My life must be Christ's broken bread. My love his out poured wine, A cup o'er.filled, a table spread Beneath hj~ name and sign,
Here is the prayer:
That other souls refreshed and fed, May share his life through mine. Amen.

Forth in thy name, 0 Lord I go, my daily labour to pursue; the, only the, resolved to know, in all I think, or speak or do.
The task thy wisdom hath assigned
o let me cheerfully fulfil;
in all my works thy presence find, and prove thy good and perfect will.
Thee may I set at my right hand, whose eyes my inmost substance see, and labour on at thy command, and offer all my works to the.
Give me to bear thy easy yoke, and every moment watch and pray, and still to things eternal look, and hasten to thy glorious day;
for the delightfully employ whate'er thy bounteous grace hath given, and run my course with even joy, and closely walk with the to heaven.

God the Son, who has redeemed you, guide and support you by his presence in the work which he has called you to share with him. God the Spirit, who strengthens and renews you, fill your hearts and minds with courage, love, wisdom, patience, strength and perserverence that you may accomplish God's will. PROPOSED NEW SITTING HOURS
Mr SCHOLES (Corio)-by leave-I understand from published material that discussions are currently taking place about prospective changes to the hours of sitting of this House. I ask you, Mr Speaker, to discuss with the Lead~r of the House (Mr Sinclair) the possibility of such discussions involving all members of the House so that an amicable arrangement .can be made which is suitable to all sides. I do not believe this matter is the prerogative of any section of the Parliament.
Mr SPEAKER-I will convey the honourable member's feelings to the Leader of the House.
PERSONAL EXPLANATION'
Mrs DARLING (Lilley)-I seek leave to make a personal explanation.
Mr SPEAKER-Does the honourable member claim to have been misrepresented?
Mrs DARLING-I do.

Perso,nal Explanation

I) April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1525

Mr SPEAKER-If the honourable lady wishes to make a personal explanation, she may proceed. However, I indicate to her that it is the practice of the House for an honourable member wishing to make a personal explanation to let me know of that request earlier so that I will know when. she stands the purpose of her standing. The honourable lady may proceed.
Mrs DARLING-Thank you, Mr Speaker. I will do that in the future. Today's Brisbane Courier-Mail contains a story on page 2 headed 'Questions Pose a Costly Question'. Unfortunately, copies of the Courier-Mail arrive late in the national Parliament so I do not have a copy of it. I have had my secretary in Brisbane relay the story through a parliamentary stenographer. With your leave, Mr Speaker, I wish to read a part of the report in order to establish the basis of my claim of misrepresentation.
Mr SPEAKER-First, the honourable member should state the misrepresentation. She is then entitled to state the true state of affairs.
Mrs DARLING-Do you wish me not to quote sections from the newspaper?
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable lady will state how she has been misrepresented.
Mrs DARLING-There are two points of misrepresentation. The Courier~Mail report states:
How much does it cost for the Public Service to answer a member's questions? A small fortune!
How do you find out how big the small fortune is? You get the Public Service to spend a small fortune.
That, at any rate, appears to be the theory behind a little clash between Mrs Darling (Lab., Qld), and MrCameron (Lib.,Qld).
Actually, Mrs Darling did not put an endless series of questions on the Notice Paper. Only a record 61 on Tuesday and 13 more yesterday.
Her questions covered such issues as pensions and the Utah mining company's wage policy.
The article then lists some of the items 'on which I asked questions. However, it goes on and this is my point:
But yesterday, Mr Cameron took issue himself, with his own question on notice, No. 1173, "Motivated by a concern of cost to the taxpayer."
Outside Parliament, Mr Cameron said: "Mrs Darling may well have set a record with her questions. Her reasons for asking are easily understood.
"However, it probably will cost a small fortune to provide her with all the answers. I do not dispute the rights of members and senators to seek required. information.
I wish to raise two points relating to this report. Firstly, I made it perfectly clear in my maiden

speech and since then that I entered the Parliament with the intention of giving real representation to all of the people in my electorate. I cannot be held responsible if the honourable member for Fadden (Mr Donald Cameron) does not share my views on parliamentary representation, a basic principle of which is that members of Parliament should vigorously seek information from the Government on issues of concern to their electors. I contend that I was doing so. Obtaining information during Question Time is like extracting blood from a stone. The Notice Paper is the only opportunity for seeking and getting information.
Secondly, I claim to have been misrepresented on the grounds of the imputation contained in the statement, as reported, by the honourable member for Fadden. Plainly, he has attempted to impute some dubious reason or ulterior motive to my quest for information. Today, it has been pointed out that a Liberal member of this House placed more than 600 questions on the Notice Paper. The honourable member for Fadden alleged that my questions cost a small fortune. I dispute that strongly; but, if it were remotely true, surely the questions of the honourable member for Canning (Mr Bungey) will send the Government broke. My real point is that I was pursuing information I thought was necessary for me to represent the people in my electorate. I disclaim and rejectany imputation put forward by the honourable member for Fadden.
Mr SPEAKER-Arising out of what the honourable member for Lilley has said, I indicate to honourable members that it has been the practice of the House that where a question is put on the Notice Paper and to answer in the form of the question as stated would involve an extent of work and involvement by members of the department of a Minister, it is reasonable for the Minister to approach the member who put the question on notice for the purpose of ascertaining the specific point of, information sought so that, if possible, there can be a minimisation of the extent of work involved in answering the question. That is not meant .to diminish in any way the right of the honourable member to ask for the information, but it is a sensible course to follow in order not to cause too great an expenditure on the obtaining of information which might not in fact be the essence of what the honourable member is seeking.

AUSTRALIAN BROADCASTING TRIBUNAL: REPORT OF
ADMINISTRATIVE REVIEW COUNCIL
Mr VINER (Stirling-Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs)-For the information of honourable members, I present the report of the Administrative Review Council on the procedures of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal. The Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) will be making a statement later today outlining the steps the Government will be taking to invite comment on the report. The Government in this case has taken the step of tabling the report in manuscript form prior to printing. This has been done to ensure that there will be opportunity for public comment on the recommendations made by the Administrative Review Council before the Government reaches a decision on the recommendations. Limited copies of the report will be available in the Bills and Papers Office, the Senate Records Office and the Parliamentary Library. It is hoped to make printed copies available shortly.

GRIEVANCE DEBATE
