Mr CUNNINGHAM (McMillan) (11.35)- Today in this debate I wish to raise the matter of housing and the lack of social planning by the Fraser and Hamer governments which has

Grievance Debate

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1527

created a crisis shelter situation in the Latrobe Valley area of my electorate. The occurrence of resource development is causing suffering which has extended across the whole of the electorate of McMillan. I wish to outline some of the present problems for the benefit of members of this House. Statistics and facts collected during 1979 and 1980 highlight the accommodation situation in the area. Over 1,000 families are on the Victorian Housing Commission's waiting list. Emergency accommodation facilities are unable to house anywhere near those in need. Atotal of 350 youths need emergency accommodation every year. Over 250 requests for places to rent are made each week to real estate agents. About 3,000 people live in caravans. Many people live in substandard or overcrowded housing. The Housing Commission estimates its waiting periods for the area as follows: For Traralgon it is indefinite, for Morwell it is at least three years and for the city of Moe it is 13 months.
For those who are able to find a home to rent, market rents in the Latrobe Valley are as follows: A three-bedroom house in Traralgon costs from $70 to $120 a week. In Morwell it would cost from $65 to $110. A two-bedroom flat in Traralgon ranges from $70 to $100. In Morwell it ranges from $60 to $90. There is also the problem of the high bondage rates for people who wish to rent. A flat bond is between $250 and $300. The bond for a three-bedroom house is between $300 and $500. For those who live in caravans, the cost of an onsite caravan park is from $50 to $80 a week. The house and land price increase in the Latrobe Valley has been far ahead of the State average..
I wish to refer toa report which has been produced in the last 12 months. The report is entitled 'Planning for the Community' and is on housing needs. It was compiled by the Mission of St James and St John. It arises out of a housing need study project which commenced in June 1980. It is the result of a joint effort between the project officer, other staff members of the Mission of St James and St John and residents of the Latrobe Valley. The concern which gave birth to this project originated from the experience of family aides working with vulnerable low income families. Many of these families have housing problems which are caused by the influx of workers in the valley. This concern about housing, combined with the commitment to social justice by the Mission of St James and St John, has brought together this report on planning principles, social responsibility and community consultation, all of which have special reference to the Latrobe Valley's housing problem. The report discusses the nature of planning, who plans it, for

whom it is planned, who benefits from it, the concept of corporate social responsibility, the planning structures and authorities involved in the Latrobe Valley and the housing situation in that area.
In the planning of future developments or extensions of present developments, there should be meaningful consultation with community groups affected by these developments. These consultations should be public and they should be conducted with genuine community representatives, such as local government and community groups. This should be undertaken on a systematic basis over a period and in some depth. The information should flow in a two-way process with real options for action, so that consultation has a chance to affect the decisions that will be taken. Professor Milton Friedman, who was in this Parliament building just two days ago, sees the social responsibility of business as being to seek profits and not to seek to serve other social ends except as decreed by government in democratically arrived at decisions. These views are consistent with the Fraser Government's ideology, which has permitted the accommodation crisis to reach the extent it hasin the Latrobe Valley.
Local control of the future of the Latrobe Valley has been reduced following the action of the State Government's Minister for Planning in this way: Several committees have been asked to wind down. These committees are the Central Gippsland Regional Planning Authorities Interim Committee, the Central Gippsland Brown Coal Resource Working Committee, the Latrobe Valley Co-ordinating Committee, the Latrobe Valley Development Committee and various subcommittees. In their place the State Government has initiated just three committees. They are the Latrobe Valley Ministerial Council, the Latrobe Valley Consultative Committee and the Strategic Planning Advisory Group, which is known as SPAG.
Attempts that have been made to co-ordinate development in the Latrobe Valley are left to the Strategic Planning Advisory Group which reports to the Ministerial Council in relation to the area. There is considerable doubt whether this Group can amend or make plans or whether it simply looks at how such plans are best realised. It is feared by locals that the bureaucrats committee will vet ideas which are from the local representative consultative committee and which are meant for the Ministerial Council, thus effectively removing any involvement by local bodies in Latrobe Valley planning.

1528 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Grievance Debate

This step, undertaken by the State Government, effectively centralises decision-making power because only the Latrobe Valley Consultative Committee has local representation in its capacity as an adviser to the Latrobe Valley Ministerial Council. The Federal Government this year announced that there would be an observer on the Latrobe Valley Ministerial Council. That is a little late considering that it has been in power five or six years. As the honourable member for McMillan I would like to point out at this stage that I have not been informed as to what method this will take. The only information that I have been able to glean up to date has been a Press release by the former member for McMillan stating that the Prime Minister and the Premier of Victoria have decided that the Federal Government should have an observer. Who he is or what he is, I still do not know. The Latrobe Valley local opinion was expressed in the Latrobe Valley Express on 15 April 1980 and 29 April 1980. It clearly indicated how the local people feel. The article stated:
Obviously the sudden emphasis on coal as an energy source has over-ridden the niceties of democratic and public participation. The fact that the meetings are closed to the public is to act not for the Latrobe Valley but for those arms of the State Government with vitalinterests in the Valley.
The committees are interdepartmental task forces. The insistence that the local committees be disbanded and the subsequent closed meetings reveals a strong emphasis on centralisation rather than on co-ordination and consultation. Furthermore, the fact that social and economic planning has not to this stage complemented industrial development is evidence that these committees are not co-ordinating development. The Latrobe Valley Express says that it is not an effective departmental committee but is a front to confirm the fears that the State Executive firmly controls the Latrobe Valley. Once again I refer to the report and quote from its conclusions and summary. At page 14 it is stated:
While there is a commitment to comprehensive planning within various legislations establishing statutory bodies, there appears to be inadequate attention given in the development plans of those statutory bodies to the human impact of those developments.
It states further:
We believe that it is because of the failure of community consultation in the past and the general failure to engage adequately in social and economic planning, that there is such a critical shortage of rental and public housing in the Latrobe Valley. We believe that only the closest co-operation between the Ministry of Minerals and Energy and the Ministry of Housing can begin to alleviate this shortage, which demands the Government's highest priority.
In summary the report concludes that people matter more than things. The social environment

is also within the scope of planning. This report on planning and housing needs in Victoria's Latrobe Valley concludes that the community networks must also be examined in an inquiry into the environmental effect of physical developments. Statutory bodies need to pay more attention to how they can best implement socially responsible planning. The Government must engage in genuine consultation with local committees. Consultation, if genuine, means that government decisions can be changed. The report calls on the State Government to reaffirm the importance of local government in planning and the current housing situation. The Latrobe Valley housing situation has been caused by the neglect of planning and the Fraser Government's approach to cutting back funding for housing. In the last five years we have seen funding go from some $103Il). down last year to $64m. The way the housing agreement is shaping up this year money. will be paid to the Federal Government. It appears from figures available that State governments will be paying more to the Federal Government than they are receiving. Hence people in these development areas cannot look forward to this Fraser Government assisting them if it continues to follow its present policies.
Mr JULL (Bowman) (11.45)-In following the honourable member for McMillan (Mr Cunningham) in this debate I think that one aspect of the provision of welfare housing should be brought to the attention of the House. It was not my intention to talk about this subject this morning but it seems an opportune time to refer to it. I believe that all members of the Government are cognisant of their obligations to provide welfare housing in certain areas. The area which particularly concerns me at the moment-this is a phenomenon which has just happened since the introduction of the Family Law Act-is the provision of housing to deserted wives and newly separated wives. In Queensland at the moment about 56 per cent of the total applications before the Queensland Housing Commission come from supporting mothers.
In my electorate in a new block of units that was funded by the Commonwealth Government and opened just last year, 42 of the 44 units are occupied by supporting mothers. Obviously we have an obligation to look after them. I am wondering whether we should do our sums to see whether a system can be devised whereby the payment of a supplementary rent allowance could be introduced to ease the pressure on organisations such as the Queensland Housing Commission and to try in some respects to maintain those deserted wives in their own family homes. I suppose a

Grievance Debate

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1529

number of cases can be cited. Obviously some wives are just thrown out of the family home. Others are left with the family homes but quite often are in a situation in which they have to pick up the entire running costs of those homes. If maintenance money is not forthcoming they are in great difficulty. It would seem to me to be a much more logical step to try to continue to maintain those supporting mothers in their own environment, in their family homes, for as long as possible rather than have the situation in which we have to spend an incredible sum of money to provide this sort of accommodation. That is not the particular subject about which I wish to speak today.
The next subject. I would like to broach touches a few sensitive nerves in this chamber. But it is a situation that I think we will have to examine very closely in the not too distant future. I refer to the issuing of identity cards to all Australians. I would like to cite as an example, a case that came before me in my electorate in Brisbane last week. One of my constituents who owns a small supermarket came to see me. A few days previously he had been visited by the Australian Federal Police. This supermarket is located in a fairly isolated area. There is not a bank within five kilometres of this population region. For the last three and a half years, since he took over this supermarket, this man has made it a practice to cash pension cheques. The visit by the Australian Federal Police last week resulted in a situation in which he found that he had been cashing cheques for the last three and a half years for a woman who was known to him, or so he thought. This woman had been presenting cheques on behalf of a person who has been dead for nine and a half years. The problem in the situation is that there is an obligation now on the shopkeeper. He is in factresponsible for some of those debts to the Commonwealth.
This is not the first time that this type of situation has come to my notice. There must be some criticism of the Department of Veterans' Affairs in as much as it has not brought its records up to date, obviously for the past nine and a half years. There again, there have been a number of cases where people, purely genuine in their motive, have cashed Commonwealth Government cheques but have been left holding the bunny. A number of allegations also have been made in recent times about a number of people attending tertiary institutions in Canberra who allegedly are receiving the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme allowance and also are in receipt of the unemployment benefit. I am sure that each and every member of this House has had cases of

people fiddling the system brought to his attention.
It is a very difficult situation to get around. It is a very sensitive situation to get around. But it is interesting that in most countries some form of national identity card is issued. Maybe this is one way that we can achieve some control over the social security benefits that are paid. It may be one way in which we can keep a degree of honesty in some sections of the community. From my investigations it would seem to be almost impossible to determine how much money the Commonwealth Government is losing through some of these practices. Although, as I say, it is a terribly sensitive issue, I believe that some very close investigation should be carried out to see whether, in fact, some sort of national identity card system could be instituted in Australia, what would be the long term ramifications of that, what the cost would be and what the cost savings to Commonwealth revenue might be.
The third point I want to make during the grievance debate concerns the Income Tax Assessment Act, especially section 23KC and section 23AD (3) (c). These sections of the Act apply to recipients of compensation payments made to victims of nazi persecution and other persecution during World War II. This is a matter that I have raised in the House before. It is a matter which I understand has just been dealt with by the national conference of the Returned Services League. That organisation has extended its sympathy to the victims of nazi atrocities and some Japanese atrocities and is. pressing the Government to make changes to these sections of the Act. These changes should be made because I understand that Australia is one of the few countries that taxes this form of reparation payment.
I wish to bring to the attention of the House two cases in my electorate. One concerns a Dutch born person who was a reserve officer in the Dutch Free Forces in Indonesia during World War II. He fought the Japanese and was duly captured by them. I think he was sentenced to a 15-year prison term. After three years he escaped from the Japanese prison camp. Some time later he was reunited with his wife who had also been a prisoner in another camp. They returned to Holland to find that the Nazis had destroyed all of the family property and that his father had been shot by the Gestapo. He was left with absolutely nothing. He finally migrated to Australia penniless. He worked very hard as a librarian. His four children serve the Commonwealth Government in various departments. They are making a great

1530 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Grievance Debate

contribution. One, for example, is with the Australian Army and he has just been involved in an Australian training course in Pakistan.
This gentleman received no income at all in terms of war reparations. Some years ago the Dutch Government decided that it would cover the situation of personnel who had been imprisoned in Java. Of course, the first thing that happened when reparation payments were made by the Dutch to people in Australia was that they were SUbjected to taxation. I feel this is an unfair situation especially in light of the fact that most countries have some form of agreement on the non-taxing of these types of reparation payments, whether they be made to the victims of nazi or Japanese atrocities.
The second case which I raise is quite similar to the one I have just outlined. It concerns a man of Polish extraction who escaped from Poland. He joined the Polish Free Forces in Britain and fought during World War II. He married after the War and became an English citizen-a resident of the United Kingdom. Finally he migrated to Australia. Now he is of advanced age and is very sick. Obviously the toll of war is telling. He applied for a service pension in Australia but found out that in fact he was not eligible to receive any sort of a pension.
I believe that the number of these cases coming to light is very few and certainly the cost to the Commonwealth would not be all that great. But this is a terribly sad situation in terms of pure humanity and in light of the incredible suffering of so many of these people who fought bravely and well and who accepted the consequences of what they did. It would also be sad if we had to knock back their applications in Australia for any form of war service pension or, most importantly, we taxed some form of compensation that may be paid by' the government of the country of their birth on the basis that they have been victims of atrocities of World War II. I sincerely trust that in the run up to the federal Budget the Treasurer (Mr Howard) and the Minister for Veterans' Affairs (Senator Messner) will look very closely at these situations to see whether we cannot get some justice for the very few people in Australia in this category. Most importantly, of course, we should recognise them as fine citizens who have made a tremendous contribution to the free world.
Mr WEST (Cunningham) (11.55)-We are most concerned about the way this Government and the Minister for Trade and Resources (Mr Anthony) is pushing Australia headlong into the irresponsible endorsement of new uranium projects despite increasingly tough market conditions

and with great risk to future generations and to the Australian and international environments. We cannot afford this rush on economic, environmental and safety grounds. The reasons should be well understood even by this Government. Firstly, there has been a complete failure by any nuclear nation to dispose of eve,n one pound or one gallon of high level waste safely and permanently. Governments are increasingly fearing catastrophic nuclear accidents. I ask the House to note that the clean-up bill from the Harrisburg incident has been estimated at $1,500m and that millions of litres of radioactive waste remain in the plant to this day and will remain there precisely because of the high level of radioactivity. It is totally impossible to devise or police safe non-proliferation arrangements in this dangerous and complex world. Forecasts of world demand and the price of uranium continue to fall and so-called safeguards standards are reduced as this Government struggles for a share of a contracting world market. There is no workable Commonwealth State legislative framework-just an ineffectual hybrid that mocks environmental protection needs.
There are new mining projects awaiting or receiving approval which involve new mining techniques both environmentally dangerous and economically unsound. I draw attention of the House to some of the new projects. The first project I wish to refer to is at Koongarra in the Northern Territory. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) stated in August 1977 that this area which was specifically excluded from Kakadu National Park to allow uranium mining 'has particular environment problems because of the sensitivity and fragility of the environment of the area and the location of the deposit upstream of a major wetland area'. But the Minister for Trade and Re" sources gave the blueprint for the destruction of this magnificent and unique area when he stated in the House on 24 February last that the then Minister for Home Affairs and Environment had advised him 'that the object of the environment protection legislation had been met and completed in relation to the Koongarra project and that there were no environmental objections to approval being given to Koongarra, provided certain conditions were met'. What were those conditions? They have not been made public. I now call on the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) to do this so that we will know whether the local environment in the Kakadu National Park has been protected. If Koongarra goes ahead, the two catchment areas of the Kakadu National Park will be threatened

Grievance Debate

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1531

by uranium mining. Furthermore, the Government plans to change key sections of the National Parks and Wildlife Conservation Act to allow Denison Mines Ltd to extend leases into the park area. This makes a farce of the whole concept of a national park. It is a disgrace for this to happen in an area nominated by the Government for the World Heritage list.
Let me turn now to South Australian projects about which I am concerned-Honeymoon and Beverley. These two projects involve the extraction method of insitu acid leaching which United States experience shows results in contamination of the water table, ground waters and streams. It is designed to inject thousands of gallons of acidified water down a number of wells. After the solution dissolves uranium in the ore body it is pumped to the surface and the uranium yellowcake recovered. Then highly radioactive liquid waste will be returned to the mined out areathat is the water table-and solid wastes will be simply covered with clay. Only 50 jobs will be produced from the $25m investment-$500,OOO per job.
The United States Federal Water Pollution Control Administration sets strict controls on monitoring procedures for the same process proposed at Honeymoon. It specifically recommends against the type of monitoring system planned for Honeymoon as it is ineffective and technically backward. This is another example of mining companies expecting less than stringent environmental standards in Australia than prevail in the United States.
The larger Beverley deposit lies on the other side of Lake Frome in South Australia where the same hazardous mining technique is also proposed. It is particularly offensive to the Opposition and myself to read these bogus environmental impact statements and the smug way that these companies assume their projects will be finally accepted under the current confused welter of Federal-State environmental legislation. But, given such a loose legislative framework, this is to be expected. Currently the States are handling development applications calling for environmental impact statements which are studied by the Federal Minister for Home Affairs and Environment under pressure from the Department of Trade and Resources organisations to subject them to minimum analysis and not to put them to a national inquiry under the Federal environment protective legislation. There is no uniform State legislation covering general environmental matters on uranium mining.

How many environmental impact statements does the Government hold? When will we hear the reports? When will they be the subject of public inquiries? Just how many public inquiries into uranium projects has this Federal Government initiated? I say that it has not initiated a single one. It was an Australian Labor Party Government which initiated the famous and worthwhile Ranger uranium inquiry. There is an interdepartmental committee report which clearly indicates that the prime Government preoccupation is to remove any legislative impediments to the development of an Australian commercial nuclear industry. This Government will move to allow the legislation regarding the administration of development approvals and environmental studies to be left to the States and seek to restrict Commonwealth activity to the simple commercial promotion and implementation of overseas safeguards agreements.
How stupid and irresponsible it is of the Government to allow the deplorable State competition which has arisen over aluminium and coal-to-oil projects, with the hawking of cheap power and the diminution of environmental standards, to extend to uranium mining. If these hawks in West~n:t.Australia, Queensland, in the Federal Government, and perhaps in South Australia proceed with the projects companies are clamouring for, Australia will have a multiplicity of environmentally damaging and economically wasteful mines producing uranium surplus to the world market. We could see Ranger producing 3,000 tonnes per annum, Jabiluka producing 4,000 tonnes per annum, Nabarlek producing 1,000 tonnes per annum, Yeelirrie producing 2,000 tonnes per annum, Koongarra producing 1,000 tonnes per annum, Honeymoon producing 450 tonnes per annum, Beverley producing 750 tonnes per annum and, if the Roxby Downs project proceeds, another 2,000 tonnes per annum, making a total of 14,200 tonnes per annum. There is no way that all of this stuff will be sold. We will be lucky to get rid of half of it, judging by the depressed international market, for the industry is declining. Uranium market requirements are steadily weakening. In the past year in the United States of America there have been 19 cancellations of nuclear power projects and not one new order.
The Minister for Trade and Resources has been lamentably one of the super-optimists. He has been consistently wrong in his predictions. He predicted that Western nuclear generating capacity would be almost 400 gigawatts by the early 1980s, but the production has turned out to be

1532 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Grievance Debate

only 122 gigawatts. The Australian Atomic Energy Commission said in 1975 that world needs would be 107,000 tonnes by 1985. In fact in 1980 the Commission had to reduce that prediction to 39,000 tonnes. It said that we could meet 20 per cent of the world's requirement. So, if the Australian Atomic Energy Commission's usually over-optimistic predictions are reliable, maximum Australian share of the world market in 1985 would be 7,000 to 8,000 tonnes per annum at a very optimistic level. This Governm,ent is going to allow the States to commit themselves to producing more than twice that amount.
In a glutted market, four things will happen. First of all, the price will come down. That is already happening. Secondly, there is a clear message that the smaller Australian mines that come on stream later will miss out in a contracting world market. Thirdly, nuclear proliferation safeguards agreements will be watered down by this Government and so will the environmental standards. Then, when the real pressure goes on to recoup the total billion-dollar capital investment, thousands of tonnes of Australian uranium will be flogged off at rock bottom prices and placed in stockpiles around the world by nations that we will never be able to trust in the long term to meet their obligations under the obsolete safeguards agreements which have been negotiated by this Government. But the real and chief architect of this commercial and environmental stupidity and vandalism is the Minister for Trade and Resources. He has the gall to demand full Federal responsibility for the uranium industry.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr CARLTON (Mackellar) (12.5)~Today, I wish to deal with the question of the quality of top management in the public sector. By 'top management' of a department, I mean the combination of Minister and permanent head, who together have to play the role equivalent to that of chief executive of a private sector business enterprise. It will be my thesis that, despite substantial expenditures on the Public Service, despite attractive levels of remuneration and associated benefits such as indexed superannuation and relative security of employment, Australians are not getting value for money when we look at the managerial capacity of the Public Service. Certainly we have a dazzling selection of individual talent and some functions are run with considerable skill, but I am obliged to conclude that there are failures in top level management of such an order that they must be a product of an inadequate management system. This is a great pity because, in a wealthy and

well educated country such as ours, devoting a significant part of its wealth and talent to public sector administration, there is no valid excuse for a failure to employ techniques that are by no means new if we look at the best experience around the world. In the time available this morning I can cite only one example.
The events of the past few weeks following the release of the Holcroft Independent Public Inquiry on Domestic Air Fares demonstrate a management failure of substantial proportions. Holcroft's methodology for setting air fares in a non-market situation may be disputed, but what cannot be disputed is the incapacity of the Department of Transport to respond to a series of questions that were pertinent, in a top management sense, to the resolution of the actual po~icy issues involved, and not just to a limited defence of an existing policy position. The distinction is quite critical, and I hope the real lessons to be learned from the last few weeks, that of managerial deficiency, not back bench revolt, will, in fact, be learned by the Government and its Public Service. I regret the necessity to mention a specific department as an example of inadequacy of top management, as I have no wish to attach personal blame to anyone. However without mentioning specific examples, I cannot demonstrate what failure actually means in this context. Public servants will want to remind me that I defined top management earlier as the combination of the minister and the permanent head, and therefore top management failures can be attributed to failures in political direction as much as failures of Public Service management. That is certainly true, but again I emphasise that my purpose is not to level personal criticisms at Ministers any more than at permanent heads. It is the system I wish to criticise, and I also wish to make constructive suggestions.
Let us therefore look firstly at the ministerial component of top management. The capacity of a political party to see its philosophy translated into policies that are implemented effectively depends on the capacity of Ministers to direct and control the Public Service. Under the existing system, Ministers suffer severe limitations in their capacity to meet these demands. Some say this derives from a genetic or environmentally induced inadequacy that is not susceptible to treatment. I would say from my own observations that our Ministers stand up well by comparison with chief executives in industry or in other large organisations in terms of intelligence, conceptual ability, common sense and drive. They do not usually possess the particular skills of a well-trained chief

The doctrine of ministerial responsibility and the prysent organisation of our parliamentary system remove most of a Minister's freedom to order his own priorities and plan his use of time to best advantage.
So the first task for reform is to try to turn the Minister's job into something that can be done well by a civilised human being. This will require: Firstly, changes in the running of Parliament, including sensible sitting hours, modern voting methods and freedom of Ministers from quorum calls; secondly, a more predictable pattern of Cabinet and Cabinet committee meetings, with greater insistence on advance presentation of papers; and thirdly, a substantial increase-at least doubling-in ministerial staff support, including a capacity to employ more higher level staff. Of the principal areas of top management weakness within the Public Service, the first appears to be that of strategic planning-thinking deeply, creatively and continuously in terms of the question: What are we trying to do and how can we do it most effectively in the face of economic, organisational or political constraints.
The second is the area of managerial philosophy which, translated colloquially, means, 'the way we do things around here.' I acknowledge the need to allow the maximum possible variety of individual approaches to problems and situations, but I believe that the public is entitled to expect within its Public Service the acceptance of a clearly-stated operating philosophy covering such practical matters as ethical standards, fact-based decision making, sensitivity to external forces, advancement through superior performance and a sense of urgency to compensate for the privilege of monopoly.
The third is the area of performance standards and control systems. Outside of the standard budgetary procedures for crude financial control, there should be more broadly defined performance standards, both quantitative and qualitative, and information systems to measure performance against plan.

lieve that that would be a bad solution.

I turn now to the Public Service component of top management, the permanent head. If we are to have a strengthened political input to policy development from the Minister and his office, it is essential that we have a much more effective managerial component, both as to policy development and implementation, from the permanent head and his organisation. The kind of management that is provided by the Public Service must take into account to a much greater extent than it does the fact that the Minister is unlikely to come in as a ready-made chief executive who can be expected to ask all the right questions on policy issues. It must be a part of the function of the per-

'

ment. It is not surprising that top management skills are so noticeably deficient in the Public Service. There is no provision for acquiring them to be present. There seems to be a rather charming, gentlemanly view that these skills are acquired by instinct or experience. A high proportion of our top public servants should have post-graduate training in public or business administration, either in Australia or overseas. Those whose earlier careers. have missed this kind of training should be sent on mid-career intensive courses at the best institutions around the world. Those, it seems to me, are the four main weaknesses in the public service component oftop management.

The actual person who is the permanent head has a long-term effect on the quality of management in a department, and not all appointments have been elicitous, so why not have the best.
I conclude by quoting from a statement by Marvin Bower in his book entitled The Will to Manage:
Managing is the activity or task of determining the objectives of an organisation and then guiding the people and other resources of the organisation in the successful achievement of those objectives.
Any business can be permitted to drift with the tides of circumstance, subject to the external and internal forces at work. Alternatively, it can be managed.
Mr Deputy Speaker, let us choose to manage.
Mr LEO McLEAY (Grayndler) (12.14)- This morning I would like to bring to the attention of the House the plight of many people in the Middle East who come from the small country of Lebanon. Prior to the Lebanese civil war, which took place between 1975 and 1976, Lebanon was one of the jewels of the Middle East. Unfortunately, one cannot say that about Lebanon today. There are in Australia approximately 200,000 people who have either come from Lebanon or are of Lebanese descent. They are all very concerned about the escalation in the conflict in Lebanon which has occurred over the last few weeks. They are fearful for the lives and safety of relatives that they have left in that country and very concerned that the-Australian Government and the Australian people should take some action in that regard.
It has been reliably reported that between 1975 and 1978, during the continuing conflict in Lebanon, almost 60,000 people were killed and almost one million fled their homes. A number came to Australia as a direct consequence of that conflict. Newspaper reports last week from Beirut indicate that approximately 200 people have been killed and more than 500 wounded. The level of conflict in northern Lebanon is comparable to that reached during the civil war. As I have mentioned, that causes grave worry to many of the Lebanese community in Australia.
In my electorate I have a wide range of people from Lebanon. Many are Christians and, as well, many new arrivals are Moslems. However, they share the common view that they would like to see the conflict in their recent homeland cease. If one seeks a common view among the Lebanese one finds that it is that Lebanon should be for the

Lebanese. They have managed in that small country for many years to have a balanced political relationship with each other. one that before the last decade had never flared into open conflict. They had a remarkable political system which involved the religious sharing of positions of power and authority. Indeed, if one looks at the Cabinet line-up in Lebanon, one could not find a more diverse group of people managing to do their very best to govern a country. I believe that because of the conflict that has occurred over the last few weeks, and the escalation before then, there now is a case for Australia to reconsider its entry criteria for people from Lebanon and the Middle East. I have stated in this Parliament on a number of occasions that I believe that the quota which has been set for this year-2,500 migrants from the Middle East-is ridiculously low. The Government's humanitarian program of accepting refugees from South East Asia is comIIlendable, b.ut I feel that it should take an equally commendable approach to the problem of the Lebanese who wish to migrate to Australia. There is a significant lack of appropriate criteria in respect of intending immigrants from Lebanon. The provisions of the Numerical Multifactor Assessment System are very strictly applied to Lebanese, with a consequent high failure rate. Special consideration should be given to applicants who have relatives in Australia who will undertake to help them settle into the Australian community.
The Lebanese in Australia have been a very successful ethnic group. They have settled in well, and frequently have engaged in small business enterprises. I believe that the statistics would show that the Lebanese have the highest proportion of self-employed of any ethnic group. They have provided a great deal of wealth for the Australian community. The Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs should allow the application of NUMAS to be modified in respect of the Lebanese, and should also enable applications to be made at centres other than Damascus. At present people must go to Damascus to apply to emigrate; there is no alternative. Some find that situation most difficult and, indeed, with the conflict at present centred on the area between Beirut and Damascus, well-nigh impossible. One cannot really solve the problem in Lebanon without considering also the Palestinian problem. I do not think we will ever have a peaceful solution in Lebanon unless the world's governments seek to provide a Palistinian homeland. There are in Lebanon large armed camps of Palestinians who are prosecuting their disagreements with others across the Lebanese borders. That is causing great

Grievance Debate

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1535

difficulty to the Lebanese. 1 think the world must come to a solution of the problem concerning a homeland for the Palestinians before it can even look at solving the problems of conflict in Lebanon. 1 believe that as a first instance to achieving some sort of peace in Lebanon there should be a total United Nations force controlling the military situation there. There are allegations by groups of partiality by the Arab peacekeeping force, some of which have been substantiated and some of which have not. The only way to overcome that is by having a total United Nations force in Lebanon rather than the split situation we have at present with the United Nations force in the south of the country and the Arab peacekeeping force in the north of the country.
Lebanon is an extremely small country whose major asset is its people. They have been a victim of the super powers' policies in the Middle East and, l.ike the people of all small countries, have very httle control over external pressures that are applied to them. 1 believe that the Lebanese should be allowed to get on with the government of their own country without outside interference fiom any of the super powers and from any of the large regional powers surrounding Lebanon. This morning the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) gave notice of a motion relating to what the Australian Labor Party believes might be a peaceful solution to the Lebanese problem. 1 have obtained the permission of the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) to incorporate that notice of motion in Hansard, and 1 seek leave to do so.
Leave granted.
The notice ofmotion reads as follows-
That this House(1) Urgently requests the Secretary-General of the United Nations to initiate immediate action by the United Nations Security Council to bring about a peaceful settlement of the conflict in Lebanon and
(2) is of the opinion that such settlement should embrace:
(A) Strict respect by all members of the United Nations for the territorial integrity of Lebanon within its internationally recognised boundaries;
(B) Extending the responsibilities of the United Nations Interim Force to apply to the internationally recognised boundaries of Lebanon;
(C) ~he transfer of responsibilities currently exerCised by the Arab League peacekeeping forces to the United Nations Peacekeeping Forces, and the subsequent withdrawal of the Arab League forces;
(D) Disarming of factional and communal militias; and
(E) Concurrent steps towards a settlement of the Palestinian question which will provide Palestinians with a national homeland.

. Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-The honourable member should be aware that the incorporation of the notice of motion in Hansard will not entitle him to debate it.
Mr LEO McLEAY-I understand that, Mr Deputy Speaker. 1 believe that it would be going one small way in the right direction if this Parliament were to place on record its support for this proposal. 1 urge the Government to bring that motion on for debate as soon as possible so that this Parliament can place on record, both here and in the international forum, our support for the Lebanese people who live in this country and for their relations who want to see a peaceful solution to the ~onflagration in their homeland.
1 call upon the Government to consider seriously amending the migration rules for people from Lebanon. It is quite clear that a great conflict is going on there. We should have the same compassion for these people as we have had for people from South East Asia. We should reinstitute the special arrangements that the Australian Government had during the civil war for immigration proposals so that we can, once again, accept into the bosom of Australia the people who need to migrate to this country to escape this conflict. That would be most appreciated by the 200,000 Lebanese living in Australia. 1 believe that it would be most appreciated by the Australian community as a whole.
Mr GOODLUCK (Franklin) (12.23)-1 am pleased to enter this debate. 1 would like to bring forward several grievances. Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (12.33) -It is generally accepted within the Australian community that although the laudatory statements made by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) and the Minister for National Development and Energy (Senator Carrick) concerning the Rundle shale oil venture were made primarilY for political purposes, they had the thoroughly

predictable result of talking up the value of the shares. It is now public knowledge that Senator Carrick was aware of the company's difficulties as early as 18 February and immediately informed the Government of those difficulties. I am concerned at the trading pattern of shares on the Sydney Stock Exchange, a pattern that I believe establishes a prima facie case for investigation and public disclosure. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a table showing trading in Rundle oil shale partner shares from 2 February 1981 until 3 March.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-Is leave granted?
Mr Street-I have not seen it.
Mr HOLDING-It is just a straightforward table. These figures indicate that in Central Pacific Petroleum and Southern Pacific Minerals a fairly normal movement of shares took place from 1 February until 18 February. In the two days immediately after 18 February-that is immediately after Senator Carrick was informed of the company's difficulties and informed the Government of the difficulties confronting the companies-on 19 and 20 February, some 264,000 shares were traded in Central Pacific Petroleum and 345,000 shares were sold in Southern Pacific Minerals. That pattern of share trading is well above the previous share movement. When confronted with those figures .during Question Time today the Prime Minister, by imputation, suggested that any leakage of information could have come from within the companies concerned. He pointed out also that he did not spend his time studying stock exchange figures. I am perfectly aware that the Prime Minister, when he is at Nareen, spends his time counting sheep and, when he is in Canberra, spends his time presiding over them. However, these figures permit only three explanations. The first explanation-which I think would be most acceptable to all honourable members in this House-would be that the figures are purely coincidental and do not represent in any way any improper use of inside information. That is the first alternative.

Mr HOLDING-I apologise to the Minister. The second alternative is the explanation implied by the Prime Minister that the difficulties were known to the companies and to their managements. If there were any leak of information it could have been from this source. Certainly there is some disquiet on the Sydney Stock Exchange as to what has been described as a belated release of information and the possibility of insider trading. I can make no judgment in respect of that other than to make the observation that the nature of the difficulties confronting the Rundle partnership were known to the companies well before 18 February. If there were to be insider trading based on company knowledge the opportunity for that was there well prior to 18 February.
The third alternative is that if insider trading had taken place its source is within the Government and those associated with the Government. Today we have had exercises in righteous indignation from the Prime Minister in his denial that he or his family owns or have owned shares in these companies. We have had similar denials also from Senator Carrick. Of course, we are bound by those explanations and must accept them. I point out to the House that questions were directed to Senator Carrick in the Senate on 21 May 1980 and 23 May 1980 seeking information as to whether any members of the ministry held any shares in the Rundle oil companies. Whilst Senator Carrick was unable in the first instance to give any information on that matter, he subsequently indicated as follows in an answer to the Senate:
All Ministers have made declarations of pecuniary interests in accordance with the arrangements referred to by the Deputy Prime Minister in his statement of 22 November 1979 on the Report of the Committee of Inquiry coricerning Public Duty and Private Interest.
Whilst Senator Carrick indicated that he had no shares, all he was able to do-indeed, all that has been done by the Prime Minister to date-was to indicate to the House that the Prime Minister was satisfied with the declarations made by Ministers.

Grievance Debate

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1539

It is not a question of whether the Prime Minister is satisfied on these matters. The people of Australia, and all those people who have had shares in the companies and who have lost considerable sums of money as a result of what has occurred, have to be satisfied. When the Prime Minister is confronted with evidence of share trading and a very direct question as to the extent of ministerial interest or, indeed, the interest of Government members who may support the Government on this issue, it is thoroughly unsatisfactory, from the point of view of the people of Australia and this Parliament, for the Prime Minister to wax indignant.
That brings me to the next proposition that I believe has to be made in respect of this matter. It is no joy and it is no comfort to any member of the Opposition or, I believe, to any member of the Parliament to raise these matters in this way. I point out that the practice and the procedure which has been adopted by the Prime Minister's own party in his own State is for all members of the Victorian Parliament to sign a register which indicates the extent of their private interests. We are confronted with information of this kind. If this were a question which was within the jurisdiction of the Victorian Parliament, as a result of the policies of the Labor Party-subsequently adopted by the Liberal Party in that State-the matter would be resolved in five minutes. The interests not merely of the members of the Cabinet and their families but also of all members of Parliament are publicly known.
I believe that that is in the interests of parliament, and I believe that it is in the interests of senior public servants who are possessed of this type of information, to be in a situation where there is a register which indicates the extent of their interests. On that basis there can be, as there ought to be, complete public confidence in the knowledge that comes into the keeping of Ministers and senior public servants in the normal course of their duties. One does not like suggesting or implying impropriety. It is up to the Parliament and to this Government to introduce a systemthis Government bas turned its back on any system-which would place the Ministry and the members of this House above any allegation of this type.
The trading figures in respect of the Rundle shale oil speculation indicate a prima facie case for investigation. I ask the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) or a Commissioner for Corporate Affairs to instigate such an investigation as a matter of urgency and to disclose publicly the results of those findings. Again, I point out to the Government that it should not be necessary for a

member of this House to make this request in this way. Properly established procedures would put these kinds of matters beyond doubt. The Parliament is not served by the righteous displays of indignation that we saw from the Prime Minister this morning.

ESTABLISHMENT OF HIGH TECHNOLOGY INDUSTRIES
Discussion of Matter ofPublic Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)~Mr Speaker has received letters from the honourable member for Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) and the honourable member for Herbert (Mr Dean) proposing that definite matters of public importance be submitted to the. House for discussion today. As required by Standing Order 107, Mr Speaker has selected the matter which, in his opinion, is the most urgent and important; that is, that proposed by the honourable member for Lalor, namely:
The Fraser Government's abject failure to promote the establishment of Australian based and owned high technology industries, especially in computers and biotechnology, and to change Australia's extremely adverse position in technology transfer.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number a/members required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor) (12.46)-The Fraser Government deserves the severest condemnation of the House for its abject failure to promote the establishment of Australian based and owned high technology industries and to change Australia's extremely adverse position in technology transfer. Historically, we have a long tradition of avoiding the challenge of developing our own indigenous technology. We have adopted the colonial model of technology transfer. As a result, one vital area after another has fallen into foreign ownership and the commanding heights of

the Australian economy are in foreign handsmotor manufacturing, food processing, drugs, chemicals, plastics, petrol and advertising, as well as control of so many of our raw materials. Unlike the Swedes, the Norwegians or the Japanese, who see the value of developing indigenous technology, we are happy to see ourselves, in Sir Henry Bolte's memorable phrase, as 'the world's quarry' and to buy our technology off the shelf.
Australia is in a particularly disadvantaged position in the computer industry. Australian imports of computer equipment rose from $64m in the year ended June 1973 to $291m in the year ended June 1979. Australia spends nearly $14 on imported computer equipment for everyone dollar spent on local manufacture-the most unbalanced proportion of any advanced country. The ratio of spending on imported versus locally produced computer equipment is as follows: In Australia the equivalent of $13.60 for everyone dollar spent locally; in Spain the ratio is 5.9:1; in the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics it is 2.7:1; in Brazil it is 2.1:1; in Belgium, Canada and Switzerland it is 1.8:1--
Mr John Brown-What about the Poms?
Mr BARRY JONES-In France it is 1.5:1; in the Netherlands it is 1.3:1; in Italy it is 1.2: 1 and in the United Kingdom-I answer my colleague from Parramatta-it is a position of equal balance-one dollar spent locally for everyone dollar spent on imports. The worst way of developing a comparatively new technology is to invite a foreign multinational corporation in and ask it to make the running. This is what happened with the Australian motor industry with the result that the Holden which used to be known as Australia's own car is now a German car with minimal modification for local conditions. Any basic decisions about that industry will be made in Detroit or Tokyo, not in Canberra or Sydpey.
The Government's extraordinary exercise in inviting the National Semiconductor Corporation from the Santa Clara Valley-Silicon Valleynear San Francisco reveals that this method which has proved so disastrous in motor manufacturing will be followed to the letter in one aspect of computer chip manufacturing. The Government has proposed to National Semiconductor that it will provide $19m in land, plant and facilities if Natsemi will set up a silicon wafer fabrication plant in Canberra, in the hope;: that it will provide a diversified employment base in Canberra. The aim is to be commended. Canberra does need a wider base than public service employment and a high technology industry is worth striving for. But, will Canberra get what it wants?

High Technology Industries

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1541

The proposal under the Government plan is that a feasibility study will be carried out. That is a good idea, but who is to carry out the feasibility study? Is it the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation? No. Is it the Treasury? No. Industry and Commerce? No, again. A parliamentary committee? No. The Minister forNational Development and Energy (Senator Carrick)? No, not even him. Is it the Department of Science and Technology? No, to the power of three. The feasibility study will be carried out-wait for it-by the National Semiconductor Corporation of Santa Clara, California. This is better than appealing from Caesar to Caesar; it is a matter of appealing from Silicon Valley ~o Silicon Valley. Who has pushed the plan? The Treasury was against it and so was the Department of Industry and Commerce. The CSIRO has had its arms twisted up its back over the matter. The Fraser Government is lost at sea; it is clutching not at straws but at wafers.
The Minister for the Australian Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) says that Natsemi will employ 1,200 people at the plant, in addition to other support and construction jobs. Natsemi's Press release uses many figures, but these are not among them. United States sources in the semiconductor industry regard the figure of 1,200 as pure science fiction, as wafer production at the rate of 15,000 a week is not labour intensive. In February, I asked a senior executive in the Santa Clara valley what would be his attitude to a government which made an invitation of this nature to Natsemi, and he said: The view we would take is that it would confirm that you are feeble-minded'.
Mr Hodgman-You are an absolute knocker.
Mr BARRY JONES-I am simply telling the truth. It is essential that we understand that what is proposed is not a silicon chip manufacturing plant. It is a plant for faQricating the wafer on which the components of the microcircuitry which comprises the finished silicon chip will be imprinted. The wafer,whilst an essential component, provides only one element in chip making. The making of the chips and the attachment of terminals, which is labour intensive, will be done elsewhere. Canberra will be merely one link in a long technological chain. Where is the tech~ nology transfer there? The silicon wafer has the same relationship to the chip as photographic paper has to a finished photograph or, as I said on the ABC Science Show, it is like having a factory which makes communion wafers without having priests or a church for the sacrament to be administered ..

I have in my hand a clear plastic container which holds finished Natsemi chips. It bears a label which reads as follows:

National Semiconductor Corp. ANTI STATIC. Caution:

This container provides protection for static sensitive devices.

Handle devices with caution upon removal.

.

I ask honourable members to listen to this; it is printed in capitals:

MADE IN ONE OR MORE OF THE FOLLOWING . COUNTRIES: KOREA, HONG KONG, MALAYSIA, SINGAPORE, TAIWAN, MAURITIUS, THAILAND, INDONESIA, MEXICO, PHILIPPINES. THE EXACT COUNTRY OF ORIGIN IS UNKNOWN.

If the project goes through-that is, if it suits Natsemi's purpose-Australia's name will be added to that long, distinguished list. I wonder whether we will secure a higher degree of technology transfer than Mauritius. I hope so.

Whilst the Government has made this extraordinarily generous offer to Natserili, which is quite inconsistent with the Friedmanite free market philosophies spouted in this House by members of the Government in the past week, it has rejected help to Australian firms in the computer area. The Hartley Computer company of Brisbane is an admirable example of a small Australian firm which has mastered the new technology,first by producing first class computer
software fora specialised market and then by producing its '3900' mainframe computer which already has penetrated a small but significant market in Britain, Canada, New Zealand and even the United States, as well as in Australia. The shadow Minister for Industry and Commerce, the honourable member for Adelaide (Mr Hurford), and I visited the Hartley plant on 17 March. What help has this innovative Australian company received from the Fraser Government? Zero! Its turnover has gone from $600 to $23m in six years, with no help whatever from the Liberal National Country Party coalition. It was not offered $19m, or even $lm for that matter, and it desperately needed bank accommodation and venture finance. That venture finance was singularly lacking. I asked David Hartley what his response would be if he were offered $19m in facilities in Canberra and whether he would carry out a feasibility.study? He just laughed; of course he would. When 'I asked whether he could use the assets usefully in Canberra, subject to working out the best way to get the best value for moiley not just for his company but for the Australian community, he answered, 'Of course'. The point is: He was never asked. It is a supreme irony that, in the very month that the National Semiconductor project is beir.6 discussed, Dr Bill Caelli and his company, Electronics Research Australia Pty

1542 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

High Technology Industries

Ltd, is moving out of Canberra and up to Queensland.
Mr Hodgman-He supports Natsemi. Didn't you know that?
Mr BARRY .JONES-There has been a slight shift in direction.
Mr Hodgman-Check your facts. He publicly supported it.
Mr BARRY JONES-I have checked the facts. He was with me yesterday when I spoke along exactly these lines. This extraordinarily blind Government is saying 'yes' to large enterprises and 'no' to small ones, 'yes' to a multinational corporation and 'no' to an Australian company, and 'yes' to a part technology and 'no' to a comprehensive technology. Large firms should be encouraged to recognise that the hiving-off process, where large forins of enterprise break away from existing entities, has been very successful in the United States and Europe in generating new ideas and new employment. Many of the most productive enterprises--
Mr Hodgman-You are against it, and you are against the Territory, too.
Mr BARRY JONES-We are saying that the Australiancommunity, and the Canberra come munity, will not get the best,value for money out of it. If the Government wants to have an inquiry, let us refer it to a parliamentary committee arid hear the evidence. Let us hear what are the specific proposals, and then let the Parliament look at it. The Government should not give the matter to the proponents of the scheme and say 'You are the best people to conduct a feasibility study'. What absurd, almost insane, precedents, would be set in every other area of government funding. We would have to ask all the applicants for funding whether they could use the money and, if they said yes, we would say: 'Right, give it to them'. Many of the most productive enterprises in microelectronics and biotechnology have been developed by new small firms and resisted by old, established corporations. This Government will do to the computer industry exactly what it has done to the motor car industry unless it encourages the development of new, small, innovative firms that can do their own things. This hiving-off process, encouraged in the United States by enterpreneurial spirit and the availability of risk capital-neither of which - are very apparent here-is contrary to the Australian tradition of playing safe by adding new enterprises to existing corporations and importing both technologies and ideas.

At the moment Australia has six great strikes against it getting into new technology areas. First, there is a prevailing lack of intellectual vitality, second, a contracting educational base, third, inadequate investment in research and development, fourth, the absence of an entrepreneurial spirit, fifth, the absence of risk capital for new ventures, and, sixth, the existence of the Fraser Government. The Fraser Government is engaged in a constant sell-out of Australian national interests. It has no interest in promoting Australian intellectual property. Its sell-out on research and development has been appalling. We rank as one of the lowest countries in the technologically advanced world because we have not encouraged our own technologies. Instead of that, this Government offers us a part technology, not the whole technology. A measure of our backwardness in the area is that the Government does not understand the implications of it all.
Mr Hodgman-You are against it.
Mr BARRY JONES-I am not against it; I am for the best value for money for the Australian community.
I want to say a few words about biotechnology. The application of biological organisms, systems or processes for manufacturing and service industries is the potentially largest of all future growth areas in the economy. Biotechnology will not be a large employer. Some biotechnological processes, such as brewing and fermenting, are very ancient. However, the techniques of gene splicing by which genes from recombinant DNA are spliced into plasmids from specific bacteria, which are then able to replicate, thus manufacturing the desired substance, were developed only in the 1960s. Biotechnological processes have a very low labour content. Many problems need to be faced up to in the whole question of biotechnology.
This Government does not even recognise the nature of the challenge. It has done very little in this area. Very small sums have been allocated to universities for them to carry out research. One small company has received a total of $450,000something of that order-over a long period. It will be tragic ifthis is to be another area in which Australia simply misses the bus and gets in too late. A measure of the ignorance of the Government and the lack of intellectual vitality that is so prevailing in the Government was evident a couple of weeks ago when I asked the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) what the Government was doing. He was not able to provide an answer.

Mr THOMSON (Leichhardt-Minister for Science and Technology) (2.15)-1 welcome this opportunity to talk on this subject of great national importance. It is far too seldom that this Parliament gets an opportunity to talk about science and technology. We need to spend more time discussing it and informing the Parliament and the people of Australia. I will say at the very outset that the Government is not idle in this field. It is doing a great deal. I had lunch today in the dining room with the South Australian Minister who is responsible for industrial affairs. He made the point that exciting things were happening in South Australia and that many of them were supported by the Commonwealth. This is happening in all the States. It is a co-operative effort between the Commonwealth and the States to introduce high technology. Admittedly we are just starting, but we have started.
The Government's attitude is to maximise economic, social and other benefits and to minimise the social consequences. I am sure my friend from Lalor (Mr Barry Jones) would agree with those priorities. This will not happen quickly. It cannot happen overnight. A great many threads must come together to form the whole pattern of this policy. We have to mobilise the intellectual forces of the nation. This is not an easy job. It will need co-operation from both sides of the Parliament. Yesterday the Opposition spokesman on science and technology, the honourable member for Lalor, spoke at the National Science Forum in Canberra. I read his speech and I agree with much of it. One of the ways this is being done is through the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentive Scheme. The Government is aiming at assisting new and existing technologies. This scheme certainly has been one of our great successes. Only yesterday in the Parliament I introduced a new Bill upgrading and expanding this scheme. The scheme aims at helping industry help itself. In many cases, unfortunately, industry is not responding quickly to the market and by injecting research and development finance from the Government we hope to rectify this deficiency. This year the Government is spending $54m on industrial research and development incentive grants to industry. That is an enormous increase from the $14m which was spent on the scheme only three years ago.

Mr THOMSON-,-I would like to add that almost all countries, except the United States of America, have a trade deficit in technology. It is interesting to note that Japan has a five to one technology deficit of imports over exports and that Australia has a 10 to one technology deficit. That is not a bad comparison between Japan, that great industrial leader and Australia, which is just starting out in this field. Japan has an importexport technology ratio of five to one and Australia has a 10 to one ratio. Importation of technology and subsequent adaption is the policy followed by many countries. Australian industry must be encouraged to do this.
I would like to touch on a number of other programs. I draw the attention of honourable members to the productivity improvement programs. The council has established a technology referral network in the metals manufacturing area, which is responsive to

its problems. The network is a great success and I hope it will be expanded in other areas.

We are a very inventive nation but we are not good at entrepreneurship. Therefore several workshops called adventure workshops have been held on entrepreneurship. This has been a very successful seeding operation. When the next workshop is held I will invite the honourable member for Lalor to attend.

We are taking steps to do something about this. High technology is a risky proposition and many financial institutions in Australia are reluctant to provide capital. We are awar'! of this problem. We are already doing things to bring together financial institutions and high technology industry. Several workshops have been organised by my Department. A large symposium will be organised by the Australian banks and the Academy of Technological Science to discuss this problem. Something is being done.
