TWENTY-SIXTH COMMONWEALTH PARLIAMENTARY CONFERENCE-
ZAMBIA
Mr FISHER (Mallee)-by leave-I present the report of the Australian branch delegation to the Twenty-Sixth Commonwealth Parliamentary Conference held in Zambia in September-October 1980.
Ordered that the report be printed.
Mr FISHER-The Commonwealth of Australia branch delegation to the Twenty-Sixth Commonwealth Parliamentary Conference in Lusaka, Zambia during September-October 1980 comprised Mr J. Corbett, M.P. as Leader of the Delegation, the Honourable G. M. Bryant, E.D., M.P., Mr A. W. James, M.P. and Senator the Hon. R. Bishop. As all members of the House of Representatives who attended the conference retired from the House at the dissolution of the last Parliament, I have been asked by Mr Corbett to present the delegation's report to the House and I feel it a privilege to do so.
At the outset it should be noted that the size of the branch delegation to the conference was reduced when two proposed members of the delegation found it necessary to withdraw because of the general election to be held in October last year. The conference was attended by 184 delegates from Commonwealth countries together with observers from several other organisations. It proved to be a most successful conference and a

summary of its proceedings is included in the delegation's report. Members of the branch delegation were greatly impressed by the arrangements made by the Zambian branch and by the warmth of their welcome to Zambia. No effort was spared by the host branch in attending to the comfort and convenience of the delegates throughout the conference and the associated tours in Zambia.
Members of the delegation also had the honout of presenting Australia's independence gift to the Parliament of Zimbabwe when travelling to Lusaka. In April 1980 the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Praser) announced that two dispatch boxes would be presented as an Australian independence gift for the chamber of the Senate of Zimbabwe. Members of the delegation made the presentation on Thursday, 18 September 1980 at a specially convened meeting of the Zimbabwe Senate. The gift was the first of any nation to the Zimbabwe Parliament and members of the delegation were privileged to sit on the floor of the Senate chamber for the presentation ceremony. The delegation was warmly welcomed .and enjoyed generous hospitality during its brief stopover in Zimbabwe.
I feel privileged to have had the opportunity to present this report on behalf of my former colleague and National Country Party Whip in the House of Representatives, Mr Jim Corbett. Mr Corbett, as member for Maranoa for 14 years served this Parliament of Australia and its people with distinction in that period. His leadership of this Commonwealth parliamentary delegation was the culmination of an outstanding parliamentary career. I commend this report for the consideration of all honourable members.
AUSTRALIAN DELEGATION TO THE ORGANISATION FOR ECONOMIC
CO-OPERATION AND DEVELOPMENT DEBATE OF THE PARLIAMENTARY ASSEMBLY OF THE COUNCIL OF EUROPE
Report
Mr CHARLES JONES (Newcastle)-by leave-Mr Deputy Speaker, I present the report of the Australian Delegation to the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development Debate of the Parliamentary Assembly of the Council of Europe held in Strasbourg on 25 September 1980.
Ordered that the report be printed.
Mr CHARLES JONES-I ask leave to make a short statement in connection with the report.
Leave granted.

Assembly of the Council ofEurope

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1547

Mr CHARLES JONES-The statement that I make is on behalf of Mr Bruce Graham, the former member for North Sydney and myself. This is a completely bipartisan statement so the first part is according t6 Graham and Jones and the second part, with the leave of the Minister at the table, the Minister for Communications and Leader of the House (Mr Sinclair), is according to Jones. The bipartisan part of the report is that Australia has been a member of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Developmentthat is, the OECD-since 1971. Prior to that, Australia was a member of the OECD Development Assistance Committee. In 1979, Australia became a member of the International Energy Agency-that is the IEAan autonomous body of the OECD. The OECD is an instrument for inter-governmental co-operation among 24 industrialised countries on matters relevant to economic and social policy. It offers Australia the unique opportunity to participate in an international organisation alongside other Western industrialised nations in a wide range of work related to economic and social matters. The areas covered by the OECD include major economic policy issues, energy, trade, science, technology, manpower, education, environment, transport and North-South questions. We are grateful for the Council of Europe's annual invitation for members of the Australian Parliament to participate in the debate on the activities of the OECD, which those attending have found an interesting, valuable and stimulating experience.
Both the former member for North Sydney and myself have had the privilege of attending the OECD annual debate, he representing the Government and I representing the Opposition. At the different times when we have been there we have found it an excellent opportunity to meet members from other parliaments, not only in Europe but also from associate countries such as the United States of America, Canada, Japan and New Zealand. That gave us the means whereby we could exchange points of view. Likewise, attending the OECD debates, as well as the meeting of the Council of Europe, allowed us to attend internal committee meetings and to put points of view concerning what is happening in Australia. It gave us the opportunity of meeting the Council's economic committee. The Australian and New Zealand delegations were there and we all put forward matters that were of concern to us regarding the policies of countries in Europe, particularly those in the European Economic Community.
I, as a member of the International Socialist Movement, was allowed to attend the socialist group meeting. The Parliament is broken up into

political groups. There was a socialist group, a conservative group, a liberal group and a communist group. So I, as a member of the International Socialist Movement, was permitted to attend a meeting of the socialist group with an open invitation from the chairman, who happens to be a member of the British Parliament, and thereby take part in the discussions that were under way. It is of great value to me and to members of parliament in Australia that we can attend these meetings. I see on my right the honourable member for Port Adelaide (Mr Young). He will be going there this year as he has been selected by his party already. The meeting will be of great value to him.
Mr Young-Because of' his economic background.
Mr CHARLES' JONES-What economic background? Do not interrupt! It is of value to all of us, even members of the Government if they would like to take the opportunity to attend meetings of the conservative. or liberal groups. Just the fact alone that one can attend meetings as well as the fact that honourable members can meet people and put their points of view forward is beneficial. I believe that Mr Graham and myself put our points of view forcibly at a meeting of the Council's agricultural committee. The chairman of the committee decided that he had had enough of our forceful argument and made the excuse that we all had a commitment to meet, a social function as guests of the British delegation, and terminated the meeting. With the assista'nce of the advisers who were with us, we really gave them a hammering. As far as other activities of the debate were concerned, we should be appreciative of the special privilege extended to visiting delegates by the chairman, the President of the Council. That privilege included the associate countries. The Council gave us unlimited time to speak on the debate dealing with the OECD annual report, whilst restricting their own members to 10 minutes. I am pleased to say that we did not abuse that privilege. I think that we spoke for something like 16 or 17 minutes. But the fact is that the Council still extended that privilege to us.
One factor that came out of the meeting this year was the opportunity for countries to put forward resolutions on the debate and to bring up those matters which they think should be brought into the debate. The Canadians started it. They did not put it to a vote, I am pleased to say-not that I had any strong feelings on their resolution-but at least it has raised the question of what the associate countries can do. I believe that the Council agreed with what was done by the Canadians. These are a few of the points that can be taken note of in the report.

1548 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Assembly of the Council ofEurope

I raised a number of matters in the debate, and one of them was a matter I had raised two years earlier concerning a visit of OECD officers to Australia. Their mission was to put together the report on this country. I raised this matter in this Parliament during a grievance debate and again in 1978. The Secretary-General of the OECD spoke to me privately about the matter and therefore 'I will not discuss the tone of that discussion publicly. On this occasion he raised the matter at the Council in response to my speech. He made a statement to the effect that the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) in Australia had been approached to meet the OECD delegation when it was in Australia. On the previous two occasions when I attended the Council meeting I said that when officers of the OECD come to Australia they shpuld not confine their discussions only to people in government departments; they should talk to chambers Of commerce, the Chamber of Manufactures or whatever other industry or organisation they wish to talk to if they feel they can get information from them. They should talk to the Executive of the Australian Council of Trade Unions and not only to the members of the Government but also to the Leader of the Opposition and the Opposition shadow Treasurer, in this case the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis). I made that proposition on two occasions in this House. In the reply of the Secretary-General, Mr Van Lennep, he said that the Leader of the Opposition in this Parliament and the shadow Treasurer in this Parliament had been approached to talk to the delegation. That reply put me in a pretty awkward situation. When I came back, I approached both of these men and the Leader of the Opposition said to me without any reservation that he was not approached to discuss internal matters with the OECD mission, nor had the honourable member for Gellibrand been approached. No 'arrangements had been made to discuss the matter with him. Whilst I am not going to war with Mr Van Lennep, the facts are all in this report. I make it very clear that neither the Leader of the Opposition nor the shadow Treasurer, Mr Willis, was approached by the mission.
I hope that when future delegations come to Australia they will do as I have suggested and get as broad as possible an impression of what people in this country think about the state of the economy, environmental matters or whatever matter it is on which the mission is to report. I thank the Leader of the House for the courtesy he extended to me in giving me leave to make a statement on the report. I think the delegation is well worth supporting. I believe that the Parliament should

continue to send delegations to the Council of Europe. Such delegations should not only take part in the annual debate on the OECD but also should accept the invitation of the Council and take part in the debate on those items that are of concern to Australia. That would give us the opportunity of putting forward our point of view to people who are acting in the interests of Europe. '
Dr EDWARDS (Berowra)-by leave-I will not detain the House for long. I pay tribute to the work of our former esteemed colleague, Mr Bruce Graham, formerly the member for North Sydney, who led the Australian Parliamentary Delegation to the Council of Europe in Strasbourg for the debate on the work of Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development. I was scheduled to lead the delegation but just prior to it I was elected to the Executive Committee of the InterParliamentary Union at its Berlin conference and was unable to do so. It was at very 'short notice that Mr Graham took my place. As indicated 'in the report presented by the honourable member for Newcastle (Mr Charles Jones), Mr Graham addressed the Council and put before it the policies of this Government and facts relating to the Australian economy. He pointed to our success in containing inflation to a rate of about 10 per cent compared with the OECD average of 12t per cent-2t percentage points higher. He pointed to the relatively strong real growth taking' place in the Australian economy. This has been associated with an increase inemployment in Australia, as honourable members will be aware, of about 150,000 over the previous year. This was a faster growth than that which occurred in most of the other OECD countries. Australia, with its inflation rate and international competitive position put right, and its rich endowment of resources, is embarked on a great period of growth and development to the benefit of all Australians and the whole world. The only cloud on the horizon now is the burgeoning rise in wage costs, which was not fully apparent at the time Mr Graham spoke.
As he himself said a moment ago, the honourable member for Newcastle stated in his own speech to the Council that the annual published reports of the OECD on the Australian economy do not result from wide consultation in Australia and present largely the views of the Australian Treasury and Government. He said that the OECD survey was 'simply a mouthpiece for the Australian Government'. Mr Emile Van Lennep, the Secretary-General of the OECD, replied specifically to that point in his concluding address to the debate. The honourable member for Newcastle referred to one aspect of this reply. I will

Broadcasting and Television Act

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1549

read the reply in full. I refer to annex C of the report which contains Mr Van Lennep's remarks. He said:
Let me state the facts about the visits of the last two missions of the Secretariat to Australia.
The first of these two saw, apart from the government departments, the Australian Employers' Federation and the Australian Council of Trade Unions.
He then says that the mission had sought appointments with the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) and the Opposition spokesman on Treasury matters, the honourable. member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis), a matter about which the honourable member for Newcastle has commented. Mr VanLennep continued:
The second mission, a year later, again saw government departments-as OECD missions do in all countries they visit-and apart from that made a tremendous effort to obtain other views, through visits to the Melbourne Economic Institute, to the Monetary Group at Monash University, and to the Institute of Labour Studies at Flinders University.
I can assure Mr Jones and, as far as necessary, others, that the OECD Secretariat also has the ability to read (Ind to follow very carefully the development of views expressed in individual countries, and it also reads newspapers.
I can assure Mr Jones, therefore, that the Secretariat, in its well-known and generally praised efforts to have an intellectually independent and certainly not a politically biased international analysis, takes into account, first, the internationally agreed strategy of the OECD and,. secondly, the particular aspects on the national basis-political, economic, social and others. fhope that Mr Jones will agree with ine that perhaps it is not necessary to raise this matter again. I hope that the' facts I have given have shown that we act according to his general wishes-namely, with full objectivity.
If the Government's approach to economic policy is what seems to come through in the OECD annual reports on the Australian economy it is simply because those policies are in line with the broad consensus of economic opinion throughout the world, certainly amongst OECD countries.
Apart from the main OECD debate, as the honourable member for Newcastle has stated, in committee and other discussions my former colleague, Mr Graham, and the honourable member for Newcastle spoke out strongly on Australia's behalf, especially in attacking the unconscionable levels of agricultural protection in the European Economic Community-particularly the insanity of selling heavily subsidised surplus sugar to our northern neighbour, Papua New Guinea. The delegation, Mr Graham and the honourable member for Newcastle, are to be commended for their efforts on Australia's behalf in the Council of Europe. I certainly reiterate my grateful personal thanks to my former colleague, Mr Bruce Graham, for the great job he did on that occasion.

GOVERNMENT REVIEW OF ASPECTS OF THE BROADCASTING AND TELEVISION ACT 1942-80
Ministerial Statement
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Minister for Communications)-by leave-The House will recall that on 4 December 1980, in replying to a question in this place, I announced that the Government was to examine the following issues in relation to the Broadcasting andTelevision Act 1942-80:
1. The machinery and procedural aspects of sections 90 to 92KA of the Act.
2. Inadvertent breaches of the Act by shareholders in companies acquiring interests in radio and television broadcasting licences through share trading on the stock exchange.
3. Networking, in capital cities and regionally.
This statement relates to those matters, although the Bill that I hope to intrqduce shortly into the House may well embrace a wider compass.
At the outset of the review, comment was invited from interested organisations and individuals. Subsequently, submissions were received from television and radio broadcast operators, their industry bodies, public interest groups, unions and organisations representing institutional investors. More than fifty substantive submissions were received and taken into account in the review of the Act and in the development of proposed amendments.
The review of these aspects of the Act has now been completed. To avoid the widespread speculation on the result and to ensure some opportunity for public debate on the conclusions the Government has reached, I now announce. the broad thrust of those conclusions in anticipation of the introduction of a Bill to incorporate the changes outlined below when Parliament reSumes after the Easter recess. This statement will ensure that there is a full awareness of the Government's intention' by the Parliament, the public, the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal, and the Administrative Appeals Tribunal.
It is important to stress that, with the amendments to be proposed, the basic structure of decision making will remain the same. The Government will continue to carry the responsibility for the formulation of policy governing the development of the broadcasting system, and the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal will retain the responsibility it has had since 1978 for regulation

1550 REPRESENTATIVES. 9 April 1981

Broadcasting and Television Act

and administration. Consistent with this arrangetelevision within a capital city. The Government

ment and with the recommendations of the Adis concerned that these provisions should remain

ministrative Review Council's report on the Auseffective and appropriate for the 1980s.

tralian Broadcasting Tribunal Procedures, which call for the provision in legislation of clear statements of relevant policy criteria, proposed changes will enable policy to be taken into account more effectively and introduce greater certainty into the exercise of the discretionary powers of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal, to the benefit of the public, the industry and the Tribunal.

In the recent review, the Government looked carefully at the procedures for the examination of share transactions with the objective of avoiding delays in the ABT's consideration of major transactions, minimising the necessary number of inquiries, and streamlining procedures wherever' possible. To these ends, amendments are to be made which will: Firstly, permit applications for approval or acquisitions to be made after, as well

The second major modification concerns the ownership or control provisions of the Act. It has been 16 years since the last major change to these provisions, yet there have been significant alterations in the industry over that time. Perhaps I should have said when mentioning earlIer the con-

to hold up to a 10 per cent shareholding in multistationed areas. Of course, a number of institutional shareholders have found some difficulty with this restraint at the moment in that they have become inadvertently in breach of the legislation without any direcUntention of so doing.

owning more than one commercial licence for quiring excess prescribed interests as a result of

Broadcasting and Television Act

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1551

share transactions will be allowed a period of at least six months to bring themselves within the permissible limits. Several further changes are envisaged in relation to the ownership or control provisions. In order to clarify the existing nonresident ownership provisions, there will be a redefinition of the restriction of non-resident ownership of radio and television stations. No individual non-resident or non-Australian company will be in a position to exercise control, as defined in the Act, of a licensee company. There will also be modification to the provisions under which loan interests in television stations may give rise to prescribed interests, by allowing exemption for ABT registered lenders. This amendment will increase the opportunities for stations to obtain finance by means other than bank overdraft, but will retain safeguards against undue control by a lender over the affairs of a licensee.
Finally, where breaches of the Act occur, existing monetary provisions have not been changed for 16 years, and are now to be reviewed. On the other hand, existing provisions for revocation or non-renewal of licences are obviously excessive in some cases and may provide little deterrent for persons acquiring interests in companies other than a licensee company. The proposed amendments will allow for continuing effective public scrutiny; they will provide reasonable balance between the desirability of discouraging 'trading'in licences against the importance of freeing the share market from unnecessary restrictions; and they will provide the mechanisms to ensure that procedures are adhered to strictly. At the same time, there will be appropriate transitional provisions, so that any unresolved application can be treated as a fresh application under the new procedures, unless the applicant wishes to proceed under the old provisions. This will ensure that no person or company with an unresolved application-I understand there are a good number-will be inconvenienced, unfairly penalised by the amendments, or denied any benefits to which he or it might be entitled under those amendments. It will also mean that the Government, in no way, will be intruding in any proceedings that are now partly en route.
The policy in relation to networking arrangements between radio and television broadcasting stations also has been reviewed. Networking arrangements have been entered into in Australia for many years and the Government recogniseS that, in its various forms, networking is necessary for the efficient operation of our broadcasting services. It is noted that, with the expected growth in the number and types of broadcasting stations, and the advances in satellite and other related

technology, networking will be facilitated, especially among c6mmercial television broadcasters. Under these circutristances, the Government reaffirms its suppciit for networking ar~ rangements between radio and television broadcasting stations. Such arrangements will not be discouraged, as long as they are consistent with the Government's important objective of fostering and preserving localism and where they are within the ambit of regulations to be made. I intend to discuss with the broadcasting industry arrangements for the iJpplementation of this policy and, after consideration of the industry's comments, detailed arrangements will be concluded by the Government and announced to the House.
Finally, as the House is aware, my colleagues the Attorney-General (Senator burack) and the Minister representing the Attorney-General have today tabled in the other place and here a report by the Administrative Review Council on the inquiry procedures of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal. The ARC report contains a large number of detailed recommendations covering all aspects of public inquiries. The report recommends a uniform-set of public inquiry procedures, including the use of extensive prehearing documentation and preliminary conferences. In summary, these recommendations will lead to greater consistency in Tribunal procedures.
I take this opportunity to announce that the Government endorses the general principles contained in the ARC report. There will not be any decision, however, as to the specific recommendations until the public and the broadcasting industry have had the opportunity to comment. Necessarily, this statement does not express with the same precision as legislation the changes proposed to the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942-80. The details will be included in the Bill, which I hope to present to this House as soon as possible after the resumption immediately following the Easter and ANZAC Day recess
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle)-'---by leave-The Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) has already mentioned that the report of the Administrative Review Council on the inquiry procedures of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal was presented a little earlier today: The Review Council's report looks into some of the problems which have emerged in relation to the proceedings of the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal since its establishment in 1977. There have been some serious problems in relation to the conduct of the activities of the ABT. We welcome the report of the ARC and, in fact, find a lot to commend in the recommendations of the report. However, one has

1552 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

. Broadcasting and Television Act

to contrast the way in which .the Minister is deciding to deal with the report of the Council in relation to the proceedings oJ the Tribunal and the way in which he is dealing with the forthcoming amendments in-other areas-of the Broadcasting and Television Act.
It will be recalled that when the Tribunal was established it was the objective of this Government-under a different Minister-to take politics out of broadcasting by giving the power of licensing, issue and renewal to the Tribunal rather than to the Minister, and to provide for public involvement in the whole question of the issue and n:newal of licences. The amendments in 1977 also introduced a motion of public interest as a consideration which the ABTshould have in mind when deciding whether to issue or renew a licence or to authorise the transfer of a licence. Over the last three years while the ABT has been operating the main problems which have emerged are those which have been the subject of the Administrative Review Council's investigation and report. The problems which have arisen in relation to the ABT concern the proceedings of the ABT. Those problems are: The questions of who has standing before the Tribunal; legal representation; the participation of public interest groups before the Tribunal; cross-- examination and how that should be conducted; and a number of other matters dealing with the way in which the Tribunal conducts its inquiries.
The Administrative Review Council has made a number of very important recommendations in this area. The thrust of the recommendations is to remove the question of uncertainty in relation to the Tribuunal and its procedures and to provide also for a reasonable sort of procedure which can avoid the heavy formalism possible at the moment to institute such things as preliminary hearings and so on. These are the fundamentally important problems which have emerged over the last threeand-a-bit years in relation to the proceedings of the ABT. These problems really were brought to light as a result of an appeal by the Australian Labor Party against the way in which the Tribunal conducted its investigations into the transfer of the ATV-10 licence in Melbourne. It ~ill be remembered that the High Court upheld the appeal and sent it back to the Tribunal for rehearing. That was really the catalyst which caused the reference to the Administrative Review Council. The Administrative Review Council has really addressed itself to the serious problem which confronted the Tribunal. There was one other serious problem which confronted the Tribunal and that was the personality of the first chairman. The appointment of Mr Bruce Gyngell by this

Government was, of course, a disastrous appointment and led in large measure to the problems which arose and which are now the subject of the Administrative Review Council's report. In the end that chairman had to be sidlined and a new one appointed. These are really the two problems to which the Government should be addressing itself at this time when it is talking about amending the Broadcasting and Television Act. It has already fixed up the question of the chairmanship but it should be acting urgently on the recommendations of the Administrative Review Council. The Government has said that this report wiIl be available for public comment and that, at some time in the future, some amendments will be introduced to put into effect the recommendations-or some of them-of the Administrative Review Council.
That has to be contrasted with the way in which the Government has set about amending the Act in relation to these other matters. The Minister in a very perfunctory Way announced -a review and called for public submissions. There was no formal notification of the review and no formal mechanismfor the receiving of public submissions. The Minister said that he had received 24 public submissions. This ought to be contrasted with the number of submissions which were received in relation to the major inquiries which have occurred in this industry over the last--

Mr DAWKINS-I know, of course there is. What the Government is removing is the power of the Tribunal to prevent further concentration of ownership in the cities. The Government is not dealing with the question of the drift towards concentration of ownership in the cities. The Government has two entirely different standards. Concentration is all right in the cities; it is no good in relation to the regional stations. The regional stations, of course, have not been saved by this because it is not just a question of takeovers or of being bought out by larger interests that threaten the regional stations; it is a question involving the existence of powerful networks throughout Australia. It is the networks that will control the product and, therefore, its distribution and in that way they will be effectively able to control the regional stations as well as the networks of which they are part.
I think there are just a couple of other things which have to be borne in mind. On page 4 of the Minister's statement there is a very worrying indication of what will happen in the future. Should there be a takeover just after a licence has been renewed then; does not have to be a public inquiry into that takeover until the time of the next hearings in relation to the licence renewal. Licence renewal hearings take place every three years. That means that a station could go for three years before there was any public inquiry in relation to a takeover. At the moment those inquiries can take place almost immediately but under these proposals it could be up to three years before there was any public hearing in relation to the takeover of a licence.
Mr Sinclair-That is not right.
Mr DAWKINS-The Minister should look at page 4 of his statement. Point 2 states that the amendments will:
. . . in the case of major acquisitions, that is, where the purchaser would be in a position to control the licensee company--
That means a takeover. These amendments will-
enable the Tribunal to inquire into the acquisition in conjunction with a licence renewal inquiry, which, at the request of the applicant, shall commence within three months of the application . . .
That means that if the person involved in the takeover wants the inquiry to take place within three months it will but unless he requests it the Tribunal could let it go until the next scheduled inquiry into the re-issue of the licence. That could be, as I read it-I would be delighted to have the Minister--
Mr Sinclair-That is not the intention. It is to accelerate the hearings, not to delay them.

Mr DAWKINS-That means that one would not have to wait three years before there was an inquiry, that it would take place immediately after the takeover.
Mr Sinclair-As soon as the Tribunal was in a position to consider them, which might be three or four months, but as soon as possible. At the moment there is a delay. The intention is to accelerate hearings, not to delay them.
Mr DAWKINS-I am grateful for the Minister's clarification because that is not clear from the wording of the statement though, no doubt, it will be when the legislation is before us. When one reads point 2 in conjunction with point 3 which allows the purchaser or the person taking over the licence to participate fully in the op"erations of the licensee company, one sees that there is the possibility that somebody taking over a licence could, in fact, operate it for three years and then be so well entrenched that any inquiry into the takeover would simply be a fait accompli.
The main area, however, in which politics is reintroduced into the proceedings for the issue and, indeed, the transfer of licences, relates to the question of the public interest. At the moment, as I indicated, the question of what is in the public interest in relation to the transfer of a licence is entirely at the discretion of the Tribunal. The Tribunal has the power to decide what is in the public interest. It has the power to disallow a transfer because it considers it not to be in the public interest. Now we have a limitation of that discretion-indeed, a removal of it altogether. We have four criteria on which the Tribunal can consider the question of the public interest. I will not go into them. They mean that there is a very narrowly defined area in which the Tribunal can consider the question of what is in the public interest. This has effectively meant that thy question on which the Tribunal made its decision in relation to ATV-10, that is, the undesirability of there being a further concentration of ownership in the television industry, will now be removed as a ground on which the Tribunal could disallow a transfer on the public interest ground
That flies in the face of government policy which, as I indicated, goes back a long number of years and, indeed, is against the policy of my party as well. We agree with the pre-existing policy of this Government, that is, it is very undesirable to see a great concentration of ownership in this industry. What this effectively means is that by removing the very grounds on which the transfer of the licence to Mr Murdoch was disallowed, this is handing to Mr Murdoch the television station in

Broadcasting and Television Act

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1555

Melbourne and with it the wh(lle of the 0-10 network. As well as severely circumscribing the criteria on which the ABT can make decisions in relation to the public interest there will be a provision which allows the Minister to notify of other criteria on which the ABT can consider the whole question of public interest. This is wide open. The Minister can make any declarations he likes, any notifications to the Tribunal he likes. He can add new criteria to the four which will be mentioned in the Act. He can do that without any reference to Parliament. He can effectively amend the Act by adding certain sections to it. But he does not have to bring those changes to the Parliament at all. It is true that they will have to be published but that does not allow for any public scrutiny at the time that they are issued. It certainly does not allow for any parliamentary scrutiny. This means that the Minister at any time, when the Tribunal is considering a transfer or the issue of a licence, can take into account the interests of an applicant and amend the Act without reference to Parliament to suit that applicant.
Sir William McMahon-He can't amend the Act.
Mr DAWKINS-Yes, he can amend the Act.
Sir William McMahon-That is up to the Parliament.
Mr DAWKINS-But he can. What this Bill is going to do is to give the Minister the power to notify the Tribunal of other criteria that it can take into account when it is considering the question of the public interest. That is effectively amending the Act without reference to Parliament. I am glad to see the right honourable member for Lowe (Sir William McMahon) is alarmed at this. That is, in fact, what is being proposed. I invite him to take this up in the channels which are available to him but not to me. That means that the Minister can directly assist an applicant with whom he might have a common interest. It allows for all kinds of pressures to be exerted on people. I think it is highly undesirable and brings politics firmly back into the area of the administration of broadcasting licences. I do not say that the Minister would do that but there would always be the suggestion that he might use this power, which does not require a reference to Parliament, to assist the family of his leader who we know has extensive television interests in northern New South Wales. I am not saying the Minister would, but it is open to him to advantage those interests if he wants to. Similarly, another government at another time could advantage some other sectional interest in the community that either had a licence and wanted to retain it or

wanted to obtain a licence. I doubt very much whether any future Labor government would use this power but as long as it exists any Minister with that wide power. would be subject to the most strenuous pressure to use it. Of course, the changes effectively can be made in secret.
There are two other matters to which I refer. The first is the increase from 5 per cent to 10 per cent in .the limit of the prescribed interest. This will simply be an opportunity for those people who already have an interest in broadcasting licences to extend their interests. At the moment they are limited to 5 per cent; now they will be able to extend their interests to 10 per cent or, if they already have an interest of below 10 per cent in two licences, they can begin to buy into a third metropolitan station. I do not think this is desirable. It will lead to a further concentration of ownership, a further concentration of influence, within this important industry. The second matter is in relation to the six-month period in which .people can bring themselves into line' with the ownership provisions. The Opposition has no objection to that. We think it is a reasonable provision because of the sorts of problems which have developed inadvertently through the purchase of shares over which some people who end up with a prescribed interest have no control.
I conclude simply by saying that these foreshadowed amendments do not address the real problems. The real problems go to the questions of the procedures of the Tribunal. The Tribunal, notwithstanding those problems, has begun to make great headway in putting the broadcasting industry onto a proper footing into which the public has proper input and an interest in its outcome. Also, it has done a lot to avoid the concentration of power which is so dangerous. The Victorian Government saw that as being dangerous in the media; consequently, it set up an inquiry into the ownership of newspapers. As a result of these proposed amendments, we have a number of steps backwards. We will see a further concentration of power and, more importantly, we will see handed on a plate to Rupert Murdoch the television station ATV 10 and, as I have said, with that television station will go the absolute control of the 0-10 network which operates in four capital cities around Australia. That is a position which the Australian Broadcasting Tribunal said was against the public interest. However, this Government has decided to legislate, notwithstanding that declaration of the Tribunal, to ensure that Rupert Murdoch gets what he wants and that the deal which was struck late last year between the Government and Mr Murdoch finally will be consummated.

Foreign Affairs)-by leave.,---On 16 February 1981 two construction surpervisors assigned to the Philippines Australia Development Assistance Project-PADAP-located in the province of Zamboanga del Sur on the island of Mindanao were robbed, abducted and subsequently shot by a group of bandits. One of the supervisors, Mr Gordon Murdoch, was killed and Mr Harold Hough, a New Zealander, was seriously wounded. Contrary to some reports there is no evidence t~at the attack was politically motivated or that It was directed specifically against the project or the personnel assigned to it.

Incident on an Australian Aid Project

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1557

been made in ensuring that the bulk of the population shares equitably in the economic and social benefits generated by the project. As the project comes nearer to completion those benefits will become more evident. A number of Australian priests, who are working in the area, have noted the benefits that the project has brought to the local population. For its part, the Australian Government is fully committed to the project and is doing all that it can to see that its objectives are realised.
In the light of all these circumstances, I have decided that Australia's involvement in PADAP should continue but have directed ADAB to investigate alternative means of implementing the project whereby Australian personnel will in future be less exposed to risk. For instance, I have directed that the geographical coverage of the project be reduced and that access to more distant work sites be improved. This will ensure that Australians are not required to work in either remote areas or areas where it is difficult for local authorities to provide adequate protection, As a result, the risks faced by Australian personnel will be significantly diminished.
In conclusion, the Philippines Australia Development Assistance Project is an aid activity of which all Australians-especially those who have worked on it since 1974-have every reason to be proud and one which I believe deserves the Government's continuing support.

There are a number of disturbing features of the Philippines-Australia Development Assistance Project. There are C]u,estions which I believe we must ask-questions to which the Minister has not provided answers in his statement. I acknowledge that risks involved in aid projects must be balanced against the benefits to the people being served by the projects. But we must be certain that the people are really getting the benefits. A number of claims have been made with respect to
PADAP, alleging that the primary benefit which flows from it flows not to the people of the region but to the Marcos Government in Manila. These claims have been made, not by irresponsible and uninformed groups or people, but by academics and at least one Catholic priest who has some knowledge of the area. The priest in question is
Father Soorley. He has claimed not only that the project does not benefit the residents of the area but also that it is positively detrimental to their welfare. He said, regarding the 384 kilometres of highway so far built, that it has been used only by military vehicles and large companies. He has said that this particularly expensive part of the project is of no real use to the Filipino residents in the area. Similar views have been expressed by academics. These statements do not, of course, accord with those of the Minister, who has made contrary statements today. If they are true, however, they indicate that in PADAP the Australian Government may be supplying a form of direct or indirect military assistance in the -guise of civilian aid.

Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) -by leave-The statement made by the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) deserves close attention. At the outset, on behalf of the Opposition, I extend our most sincere condolences to the family of the Australian official who was killed in the Philippines, Mr Murdoch. He served his country well on projects in Australia as well as overseas. I also extend the Opposition's good wishes to the New Zealander who was seriously wounded, and wish him a speedy recovery. I am aware from my own travel and experience in Asia that many of our aid officials work under extremely difficult conditions and, very often, in remote areas. I think that many other honourable members would be able to vouch for that. I would like to make it Clear that I alIi well aware that this project got under way under a Labor government and that a number of the comments I will be making will be made with the benefit of hindsight and in the light of developments which could not be foreseen at its inception, including the most unfortunate incident to which I have just referred.

The building of roads and bridges may bring economic benefits, although even these are often more a matter for large corporations than smallholding peasants. We areall aware that peasants in these countries do not usually possess motor vehicles. They may possess a push bike or a push cart, but that is about all. More importantly, these roads and bridges do allow penetration of motorised military vehicles into formerly remote guerrilla strongholds. The point is that PADAP is located on the island of Mindanao. Mindanao is the stronghold of the Moro National Liberation Front, a group which has, since the commencement of this project, escalated its position and become a group which is now supported by a large proportion of the population, if not an outright majority of the predominantly Moslem population in the areas in which it operates. The question which must be asked is this: Are we exposing Australian workers to unacceptable risks by placing them in such areas where they are very likely to be identifie<:l with central government ofP.cials by the local residents who are hostile to

Association ofSouth East Asian Nations

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1559

other countries which it is not desirable or intended to set aside. To harbour the belief that the ASEAN-Australia relationship is 'special' would be to invite a potentially damaging complacency in the relationship. At the same time; there should be no doubt that the Australian Government continues to place a high priority on its relations with the countries of ASEAN and the Association itself. Vietnam's invasion of Kampuchea, with support from the Soviet Union, and the resultant problems which have flowed from that invasion, have served to underline a number of important politico-strategic concerns shared by Australia and the ASEAN countries.
In 1974 Australia became the first country to establish a 'dialogue~ relationship with ASEAN. Regular consultations are now held on political, economic and development issues and a range of other matters. They have included one meeting at Heads of Government level-in 1977 the Prime Minister met with his ASEAN counterparts in Kuala Lumpur. Meetings between the Australian Foreign Mini~ter and his ASEAN counterparts are now an established practice and are a welcome and most valuable feature of the dialogue. The frequency. of consultations between other Ministers of the ASEAN and Australian Governments has also increased since 1977.
Consultations at the Ministerial level are supplemented by regular contact between Australianand ASEAN officials in Canberra, in the ASEAN countries and in other centres such as the . United Nations in New York. Consultations have been given form through the annual ASEANAustralia Forum, a meeting of senior officials, which is scheduled to meet next in Canberra in April. Between Forum meetings, the ASEANAustralia Consultative Meeting-AACMestablished in 1978, brings Australian officials from twelve departments into regular consultation with the heads of the ASEAN diplomatic missions in Canberra. Matters of detail affecting our trading relations and our development cooperation programs are dealt with by special working groups under the AACM. This consultative machinery has proved an effective and practical way of enabling a continuous process of consultations on matters of interest and concern to the six countries.
The Government agrees with the Committee's recommendation that priority be given to developing policy-making processes to coordinate effectively the interdependent elements of domestic and foreign policies. The Government considers that the political and economic, foreign and domestic elements of Australian policy should continue to be treated as parts of our total

policy towards ASEAN and not approached as entirely separate entities. As well, in formulating policy towards ASEAN, full account needs to be taken of Australia's important bilateral relations with the ASEAN countries. It is important that the practice of adequate consultation on policy matters affecting Australia's relations with ASEAN be continued. To provide a co-ordinated approach, the Government in 1977 established the Inter-departmental Committee on ASEAN. I believe this machinery is working well.
The Government accepts the Committee's recommendation that consultative procedures in policy co-ordination should be broadened to promote close liaison with the non-governmental sector when the issues involve them. Some advance has been inade in this area since evidence was presented to the Senate Committee. In practice the Government and departments maintain close contact with the business community and with. business groups, including the bilateral Business Co-operation Committees, Chambers of Com-. merce and similar bodies which link the Australian and ASEAN private sectors. In addition, officialsprovide briefings and seminars for individuals and groups in the Australian business community and these have resulted in a better understanding by Government and business alike of matters arising in dealing with ASEAN.
To meet the growing demands of AustralianASEAN relations, the Committee recommended that Australia's diplomatic missions in the region be provided with adequate staff and resources to ensure that Australia's policies are effectively implemented. Our posts in the ASEAN countries are regarded as having a high priority in claims on staffing and resources. At the same time, there is need to balance the requirements of our ASEAN posts with requirements elsewhere, such as those arising from the opening of new posts. Because of this, pressure points do exist. Staffing levels, both at our posts and in Canberra, are kept under constant review.
In respect of economic relations, the Committee recommended that emphasis be given to developing economic policies as they are a key element in the relationship and serve both ASEAN and Australian long term interests. Efforts are already underway, through consultations with the ASEAN countries and through trade and investment development initiatives, to widen regional understanding of Australian economic policies. In July 1979 a Joint Trade Com-
mission was established with the Philippines and a
Joint Trade Committee was established with Thailand. These bodies, together with the AACM, provide useful and direct means for

1560 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Association ofSouth East Asian Nations

exchanges of information on matters of trade interest between Australia and the ASEAN countries.
The Committee states that the development of economic policies should include a careful explanation of Australia's position on tariffs, including domestic implications, commitment to tariff ree ductions and responsibilities to the Australian community. The Government's approach to industries assistance, which involves a commitment to lower and more stable tariff levels, was set out in the White Paper on Manufacturing Industry and endorsed by the Study Group on Structural Adjustment. This approach recognises the need to take into account the ability of the community to absorb change and the need to deal with short term problems. The ASEAN countries have been advised of the Government's approach to these matters and this practice will continue.
The Government notes the Committee's recommendation, in respect of trading relations, that the general approach to preferences should continue 'where it is practical and mutually beneficial'. It further notes the Committee's recommendation, on p"rospects for trade development, that Australia should continue to stress its commitment to a reduction in trade barriers on a reciprocal and non-preferential basis and intensify its efforts to achieve such reductions. Tariff preferences accorded to products of developing countries have been regarded as an important concession by those countries, including ASEAN. As signatory to the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade, Australia has supported and participated in reciprocal and non-preferential reductions of trade barriers in the context of global trade negotiations such as the multilateral trade negotiations. Outside of that framework, however, reductions in tariff occur in the context of reviews of industry assistance and are not dependent on reciprocity. To tie such reductions to reciprocity would in some instances involve maintaining barriers higher than economic prudence would justify. Whilst opportunities for reciprocal reductions of trade barriers arise in the context of GATT negotiations, reciprocity is not required of developing countries in such rounds and, in the present global climate, demands for reciprocity from developing countries would not be well received.
The Government has already taken action in the direction recommended by the Committee to encourage and support links between private sector organisations in Australia and ASEAN. The ASEAN-Australia Business Council was established in June 1980 and I note that the Committee acknowledges the initiatives that the Government

has taken to support the development of industry links with ASEAN. Co-operation with the ASEAN-Australia Business Council and the various Business Co-operation Committees and Chambers will be continued where appropriate as part of these initiatives. In the end, success or failure will depend upon the independent efforts of business people but I can assure you that, as a Government, we will do all we can to create, as far as possible, the conditions for success.
In respect of data on trade flows, the" Committee recommended that the Australian Government should give further consideration to providing more detailed statistics on the balance of payments with ASEAN countries, particularly to serve industry needs. Further progress has now been made on the review of regional balance of payments statistics mentioned in the letter of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) to Senator Sim and quoted on page 38 of the report. The Australian Statistician is now able to release broad statistics on the balance of payments with each ASEAN country, although he is unable at this time to release statistics at a detailed level. '
The Committee expressed support for the proposal that the Australian Government should post a labour attache to ASEAN. It is the Government's view that such an appointment is not required at this stage but that the matter should be kept under review.
The Committee's recommendations that the relevance and significance of development assistance in Australia's relations with ASEAN and its member countries should be kept under constant review are consistent with Government policy. Bilateral development assistance to the ASEAN countries is kept continually under review through project and program reviews, and through direct consultation with recipient Governments, aid co-ordination officials and the various international consultative organisations, such as the Inter-Governmental Group on Indonesia-IGGI. The ASEAN-Australia Economic Co-operation Program-AAECP-which brings Australia and the ASEAN countries together in regional programs, is also cQntinually reviewed through regular meetings of the AACM's special working groups on development co-operation and on trade, and through the annual meetings of the ASEAN-Australia Forum.
In its conclusions and recommendations on. immigration, the Committee stated that it is cqncerned that Australia is still seen as a European outpost in an Asian region. It "recommended that the Government concentrate on projecting Australia's identity which is evolving through the

Association ofSouth East Asian Nations

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1561

changing structure of its society. The Committee also recommended the further development of immigration policy along the lines recently adopted, a humanitarian approach to the needs of refugees and the intensification of efforts to finalise the reunion in Australia of families from East Timor.
In general the Government accepts these recommendations. A substantial shift of opinion has taken place in ASEAN countries since Australia's restrictive immigration policies were changed. While Australia is still seen by some as excessively predisposed towards Europe, the view is less widely held than six or seven years ago. The understanding that many suitably qualified people are eligible to migrate to Australia is now more widespread, as is recognition that Australia, while having close cultural and other ties with Europe, shares many of the preoccupations and interests of the ASEAN countries.
Personal contacts are indispensable as a basis of undertstanding between countries. The Government agrees with the Committee that student exchanges and migration from the ASEAN countries play an important role in building this understanding and in removing any Impression that Australia remains a European outpost in an Asian region. Changes to our private overseas student policy have recently led to the introduction of new arrangements whereby a greater number of such students will be able to enter Australia for long term study.
Since the report was drafted, Australia has maintained its resettlement and humanitarian support of Indo-Chinese refugees and its efforts to complete the East Timor family reunion program. The Australian Government has contributed some $30m to Indo-Chinese refugee and related programs overseas, with further substantial private contributions from Australian citizens. We have accepted some 47,000 Indo-Chinese refugees, most of them from ASEAN countries of first asylum ,Upwards of one in every 310 persons in Australia is an Indo-Chinese refugee and community groups have backed and supported the Government's efforts. In addition to its vital humanitarian contribution, the Government's Indo-Chinese refugee program has been a p~sitive factor in Australia's co-operation with ASEAN.
In line with the Committee's recommendation, the Government is engaged in a co-operative effort with the ASEAN countries to stem the flow of illegal narcotics through the region. The Government agrees strongly with the recommendation that everything possible should be done to prevent the incalculable damage which could be

done to the fabric of our societies by the spread of narcotics, and has recently devoted increased resources to this area.
In respect of the news media, the Committee urged continuing efforts to ensure information flows between Australia and the ASEAN countries and recommended that the Government initiate further discussions with the Indonesian authorities to ensure that Radio Australia has a correspondent in Indonesia. The Government considers that the Committee's comments on these matters touch on significant aspects of the relationship between Australia and the ASEAN countries. We recognise the importance of encouraging informed and balanced coverage of developments in the region by the Australian Press and support the Committee's view that the ASEAN countries should be encouraged to establish media representation in Australia.
The Government believes that Radio Australia has an important place in expanding perceptions of Australia in the region and awaits with interest the report of the Committee of Review of the Australian Broadcasting Commission which has considered Radio Australia's operations. We are aware of objections within the Indonesian Government to aspects of Radio Australia's broadcasts. Contacts between the Australian Broadcasting Commission and the Indonesian authorities aimed at securing the return of an ABC correspondent to Jakarta have taken place. I hope that these will be successful.
The Government notes the Committee's support for the establishment of an Asian studies council, a proposal put forward in a report prepared by the Asian Studies Association of Australia. Government departments are already examining the report and the Government's views on it will be made known later in the year. In the meantime, my colleague, the Minister for Education (Mr Fife), has asked the Tertiary Education Commission to take account of the report in preparing volume I of its report for the 1982-84 triennium.
On education and cultural relations the Government accepts the Committee's recommendation that every effort be made to meet requests from the ASEAN countries for assistance in education, particularly in the teaching of English. Australia already supports a number of projects in the ASEAN countries related to education. These include bilateral aid projects, support for the South-East Asian Ministers for Education Organisation, for the activities of the Australian Vice-Chancellors Committee under the Australian-Asian Universities Co-operation

1562 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Affairs and Defence. The Opposition welcomes there is increasing militarisation of Australia's

Association ofSouth East Asian Nations

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1563

contacts with ASEAN. I want to sound a word of warning about this. The question of giving military aid does not guarantee stability. Honourable members will notice the most recent position which seems to be surfacing to some extent in Thailand. First, the records and reputations of some ASEAN governments in the fields of human rights and civil liberties are far from acceptable. I would not like a situation to arise in which Australian military and security help to ASEAN governments contributes either directly or indirectly to internal security measures taken for political reasons. We have just referred to the problems of the Philippines particularly in Mindanao. There is a problem in that country in that the Marcos regime is not readily recognised as having the support of the people. The same problems arose in South Korea, We have evidence which shows that if that happens, there is bloodshed, there is instability and there is no peace for the region.
Moreover, Australia should take all precautions possible to ensure that any military help or advice which we do give is so directed or controlled that it cannot be used by any ASEAN partner against another or against their neighbours unless for self-defence. Such precautions are only prudent. The possibility of ASEAN tending to emphasise military relations at the expense of economic co-operation is not one Australia should welcome.
The Minister stated, and the committee recommended, that the Government should give further consideration to providing more detailed statistics on ASEAN countries particularly in relation to industry and industry help. He then went on to say that in spite of the fact that the Committee expressed support for the assignment of a labour attache to ASEAN, the Government's view is that such an appointment is not necessary. We must ask: Why not? If the Government can assist industry and do its homework for it, why should not the Australian trade unions, for example, be entitled to the benefit of information on trade union developments, or the lack of them, in ASEAN countries? Not only trade unions, but also the Government may well find it instructive to receive information on the curtailment or suppression of attempts by workers in these countries to protect their interest. Let us have a bit of balance in the whole field and let us introduce balance into the information which our embassies are collecting. The Opposition believes that this is a worthwhile recommendation which should have been accepted. For example, we have recently posted a labour attache to Japan. It is no less important, and perhaps more important, for us to

have. regular detailed reporting from ASEAN capitals on the wages and conditions and union membership of workers' in the ASEAN region, particularly those employed in making products for exports to Australia because Australia is obliged to compete with ASEAN industries. Any decisions on tariffs and import quotas should be taken in the full knowledge of the wages and other costs involved in the production of goods. Also there is the question of whether there will be import quota regulations.
Another vital source of information on ASEAN manufacturing and trade is also given unsatisfactory treatment by the Government in its response to the report. Let me refer to it. The Minister states that the Australian Bureau of Statistics is not able to provide detailed figures on ASEAN balances of payments. The reason appears to be the limits on funds made available to the Bureau. Funds for the extra staff and research facilities needed to provide such detailed figures should be given at once. The cost would not be great, but the benefits could be large.
The Minister also referred to Indo-Chinese refugees. I applaud the Government's efforts and those of the thousands of Australian families who have helped in the resettlement of these unfortunate people. We must continue our efforts in this regard. But I come back to the point that it is only by fostering stability in our region that we will avoid further war and racial conflict in our areaevents which are the genesis of all refugee outflows.
The Minister stated that the Government recognises the importance of encouraging informed and balanced coverage of events in the region by the Australian Press and Radio Australia. That is all very well, but what has happened? The fact is that our correspondents have been thrown out, one after the other, in the case of one ASEAN country. The Minister states that efforts to secure the return to Jakarta of the Australian Broadcasting Commission correspondent have been made. I hope they are continuing. I hope, in fact, that the Minist~r might give us a progress report. For example, what are the Indonesian authorities saying? Are they seeking to impose conditions for the return of the correspondent?
The Minister could also give a progress report on another matter concerning Indonesia. The report of the Senate Committee mentions reunification of East Timorese with relatives in Australia. There have been Press reports, not denied, that large sums of money perhaps have had to be paid by Timorese to Indonesian bureaucrats dealing

1S64 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Association ofSouth East Asian Nations

with their cases. If bribery and bureaucratic obstacles are part of the problem the Government surely should make a protest. We would like to know more about that. When the Indonesian Foreign Minister visited Canberra he made promises about reunification. We would like to know what progress has been made towards keeping those promises.
The Minister in his statement mentioned a number of matters which I will refer to very quickly. He spoke particularly about stability in the region and the problems of Vietnam. Honourable members should note that not once did the Minister mention China. The real problem for Vietnam is China. The problems for the region is the hostility that took place between China and Vietnam. The reason for the refugee exodus is the 'surgical strike' on Vietnam by China. Everyone condemned it, as well they should have. One need only go to Hanoi, and I have, to realise the severe problems that exist there. These problems will continue.
It is important in that context that we should not create barriers between ASEAN and Vietnam. We should be getting the countries of the region closer together. We do no want to encourage or oblige Vietnam to seek Soviet assistance on the basis that there might be a further 'surgical strike' by China. Also, the problems of Kampuchea are still far from resolved.
I want to make this point very clear: We would not have had the problems of refugees if there had not been a conflict between the super-powers, with the United States perhaps encouraging China in its attack on Vietnam. If a resumption in fighting takes place, Australia's very survival could be at stake, not only in terms of military operations but in terms of mass migration of millions of people.
Do not let us minimise the problem. We are dealing with millions of people, many of whom feel they would rather leave the region. When we look at the poverty in that region and the inability of the people to get land rights or human rights, we can understand why any human being might want to leave. Yet in terms ofland prosperity, fertility and rainfall, the region is a most magnificent area. The tragedy is that it does not have any democracy. We must remember that.
Australians have worked hard over two centuries to develop a system which, while it can be criticised, is I think a long way ahead of many other systems. Our people fully appreciate democracy and individual rights. We should not adopt a mea culpa syndrome, that there is always something wrong with us in this region and that

we should be indicating to the people to the north that is us who should do better. I want to see a foreign policy in which we indicate to these people that they can do a lot better. It is important that Australia be very mindful and proud of its past, particularly the fact that we have fought for freedom in two world wars, the second of which was partly in this region. Many Australians were killed fighting for what democracy is all about. I want to see peace and stability. This cannot be encouraged, maintained or guaranteed if there is an air of hostility towards VietnaQ1. To ignore the problems of China, the influence of the United States and the Soviet Union, and the.real problems of the Association of South East Asian Nations is to ignore democracy and how it can work. It is no good having dictators or a ruling elite always pretending to help the country whereas, in fact, they are helping themselves.
As I indicated in my earlier statement today on the Philippines, when we talk about aid projects I want to see aid given to the people of the area, so that they have a chance to improve their economy and their standard of living. It is important for them to have land reform, to get the benefit of irrigation, or improved strains of rice, and to be able to sell crops at a proper price to guarantee that their standards of living will improve. When we talk about aid in these areas, let us talk about land reform, and about reform in human and civil rights, education, health, housing and all these matters which are very basic to these people.
If we talk about narcotics, we must talk about alternative cash crops which can certainly be grown. The matter should not be left on the basis that the people have to be exploited by producing the narcotics themselves. There is so much that we can do.
For example, the most important thing which concerns the people of East and West Timor is dry land farming. We are experts in that.field and it would cost us very little to show these people how they could improve their agriculture.
I come to this other element which I think has done us untold harm. I refer to the Harries report of the Committee on 'Australia's relations with the Third World' and the fact that we, in Australia, are overprotective. It is emphasised again in this report that we have to lower tariffs. Can we not get it through the heads of people in Parliament and the bureaucrats that we are the least protected of these countries that are putting up protective barriers. We allow a 60 per cent import penetration in the textile area whereas the Japanese allow only 10 per cent and the Americans allow 8 per cent.

Association ofSouth East Asian Nations

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1565

When I go to Malaysia to look at an Australian factory and I ask: 'Are you having troubles in selling the product to Australia?' The answer is: 'No, but I cannot get the product into the United States, Japan or the European Community as they will not allow it. Your market is a small one and I am happy with what I can sell there'. We look at the question whether he should be there. He is there for the reason that he is in a free trade zone to make money. He does not pay any taxes. Everything is provided for him, including cheap labour. But are the female staff members in the factories happy? I suggest that they are not. They are working for something less than one dollar a day and they are working long hours making a blouse or a skirt. Is that a real economic advantage? Would it not be better to see these young ladies back to their home towns being educated and learning some better skills; doing something worthwhile and getting some happiness out of life, because there is so much that needs to be done in these areas.
I am very critical of reports that berate Australians for being overprotective. I make the point that we do not want to see cheap labour manipulated, particularly by trans-national companies. Honourable members can bet their lives that the product those companies make is sold and exported at what the market will bear. The benefit does not go to the people who produce it. They are exploited on the basis that at some stage they will get an economic advantage. I fail to see the reason for this because it brings nothing but unhappiness, misery and hostility.
In all these regions, there is also an official hostility to trade unions or any organisations of labour, if they suggest there ought to be an improvement in housing or in wages. Such a stand has already caused a couple of revolutions in South Korea and it could bring about communist control in that country. It can happen.
I can also see the same things happening in the Philippines. If masses of people there are to be exploited for the so-called economic gain of the elite or overseas interests, we are just fostering the inevitable revolution. Because they see no alternative, people will change to a communist system, usually through the gun. Democracy never gets a chance. The problems are there.
Luckily for us, the monarchy in Thailand is a very intelligent one indeed. We owe the stability in Thailand to the King, not to the generals. Let us make it very clear that the stability is not due to the armies or the coups. Instability is the real problem.

I can see problems developing in Indonesia, which I note is entering the nuclear age, not that we would want to encourage it. Why would Indonesia want nuclear reactors? The most important thing to theSe people is energy sources. The best way Jor them to get that is by the production of synthetic fuel, or the ability to convert crops into fuel, particularly as that country has a high annual rainfall, and favourable temperatures, so there can be rapid crop production. How much better would it be if we encouraged people there to go into ethanol production.
If honourable members look at the question of food, they will notice that our main export to those people is wheat and dairy products. They need those products. Then we keep berating ourselves on the basis that there is an imbalance in trade. I have received a signal to tell me that I must conclude, and I will. There is so much more that we can do. I do not want to see any more 'Harries Reports' or any more berating of Australians because of what we have done 'wrong'. We are leading the field in the area of production and we want an independent foreign policy that guarantees that there will be no war or hostility in the whole region to jeopardise that position.
