1566 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Quarantine Amendment Bill

QUARANTINE AMENDMENT BILL 1981

Bill presented by Mr MacKellar and read a

first time.

'

Second Reading
Mr MacKELLAR (Warringah-Minister for Health) (8.1)-I move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill is an omnibus Bill by which the Government seeks to extend and up-date the Quarantine Act: To provide comprehensive quarantine and disease control arrangements for the Cocos (Keeling) Islands Territory; to give effect to outstanding recommendations for legislative change put for:vard by the Senate Standing Committee on NatlOnal Resources in its report on the ade~~acy of quarantine; and to make additional provIsions and amendments and to provide additional ~owers ~h.ich a.re required for effective quarantme admlmstratlOn and enforcement in Australia. The B~ll is a reflection of the Government's strong commitment to an efficient quarantine service which must .continue to play an effective and major role in Australia's social and economic well-being.
~onou~able members will know that a high secunty ammal quarantine station is under construction in the Territory of the Cocos (Keeling) Islands. The station will provide a safe means of achieving genetic improvement in our economic livest?ck th!ough the importation of genetically supenor ammals from disease risk areas of the world. Without the security of an off-shore station, importation of such animals from all but a very few c~untries could not be contemplated. Current estimates are that construction of the station will be completed in September 1981. Security of the station depends on appropriate quarantine and other disease control arrangements for the Territory as a whole. Because of the integrated nature of the arrangements which are required, the legislation must encompass animal, plant, general and human quarantine areas and authorise all necessary measures to prevent the introduction or spread of diseases affecting animals, plants and man. The Government has decided that these comprehensive quarantine arrangements would best be achieved by extending to the Territory with amendment where necessary for ~erritory appli~ation the relevant powers, functions and reqUirements of the Quarantine Act. The proposed legislation would differentiate betw.een Australia and the Territory to ensure that sUlta~le controls can be exercised over the movement of vessels, people, animals, plants and goods between Australia and the Territory.

The Bill also makes provision for the internal ~ontrol of ~iseases and pests of animals and plants m the terntory. These powers are exercised in All:stralia by State authorities under State legislatlOn and form an integral part of Australia's overall animal and plant protection framework. These necessary State-type powers are not currently available in the Territory. I draw the attention of the House to clause 4 of the Bill which provides for the application of the quarantine Act in the Territory. Clause 7 of the Bill amends section 13 of the Act to authorise the Governor-General to make proclamations which will prohibit or con- ~rol the impo!tation of animals, plants or goods mto the Terntory from Australia or overseas. I would like to emphasise that for the purposes of entry into Australia of persons, vessels, goods, animals or plants the Cocos Islands will continue to .be regarded as an oversea place under the amended legislation. Similarly Australia will be an oversea place for the purpose of entry into Co.cos. Clause 41 provides for the making of regulatIOns relating, inter alia, to the internal Statetype stock and plant disease control measures to which I have referred. The system for setting the fee~ which are to apply for the management and mamtenance of animals at the quarantine station will also be provided for in the regulations.
I would now like to turn to the report of the Senate Committee on National Resources. This report 'The Adequacy Of Quarantine And Other Control Measures To Protect Australia's Pastoral Indu~trie~ From The Introduction And Spread Of Exotic Livestock And Plant Diseases' was tabled in November 1979. The Committee recommended, inter alia, that the Quarantine Act be amended to eliminate problems relating to the granting of pratique and the lack of certain po~ers. for quarantine officers, and to provide legislatIve support for contemporary quarantine problems not currently covered by the Act. The Governmen~'s response to the Committee's report was tabled m another place in September 1980. The Gover~ment pointed out that, although many ~f the Important matters referred to by the Committee had been taken up by the Quarantine Amendment Act 1979, the need for further action would be closely examined in the light of the Committee's concern.
The Bill before the House proposes amendments developed in the light of the Committee's views. It follows an extensive review of relevant legislation and current quarantine requirements and problems. I would like to highlight some of the more important changes. There are new arrangements regarding imported animals, plants and goods which will ensure that they remain

Quarantine Amendment Bill

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1567

under quarantine control until specifically released. Presently the granting of pratique to the vessel releases all the goods on board. The Bill also makes provision for pratique to be granted by radio message as well as by conventional means. New powers for the examination of imported animals, plants and goods before and after release from quarantine are included in clauses 24 and 27. An examination under these provisions would include sampling and testing, The Bill provides power for quarantine officers to ask questions, require documents or samples and undertake tests in relation to goods which are, or are suspected to have been, subject to quarantine. There are also new powers under warrant to enter premises for the purposes of examining goods, including animals and plants which are subject to quarantine. Finally, the power to order imported goods, animals and plants into quarantine on disease grounds are clarified and expanded. I believe that these and other amendments contained in the Bill, taken in conjunction with the amendments effected in the Quarantine Amendment Act 1979 meet the specific concerns raised by the Senate Committee on National Resources which are appropriate to quarantine legislation.
The Bill also rectifies a number of other deficiencies identified during the Department's examination. Section 69A of the Act provides for compensation under certain circumstances for goods, including animals or plants, which are destroyed under the Act for disease reasons. These arrangements were introduced in 1979 to encourage co-operation from owners and to facilitate prompt action in exotic disease eradication operations. The appropriateness of these compensation provisions for imported livestock species and certain other classes of imported animals has been reviewed. Advice to the Government is that no other major livestock importing nation-that is, Canada, the United States of America, New Zealand and the United Kingdom-compensates where destruction of imported livestock is ordered for disease reasons at a quarantine station. Inquiries in Australia indicate that this eventuality, should it occur at an Australian mainland station or the Cocos Island station, is an insurable risk.
In the circumstances, the Government has concluded that it is reasonable for Australian importers of livestock and certain other classes of animals to carry the risk of destruction in a quarantine station under the Act as part of total importation costs. The Bill therefore provides, at clause 33, that imported horses, asses, mules, pigs and ruminants, animals imported for a zoo or circus, or animals imported for research will not

qualify for compensation if they are destroyed at a quarantine station under the Act and had been in quarantine at all times between their importation and destruction. Compensation will still be paid for these types of animals if, after release from the quarantine station, they are later ordered into quarantine and destroyed. Compensation will also be available under the Act for other imported species such as cats and dogs, lawfully imported and for all animals of Australian origin. .
I now draw the attention of honourable members to clause 38 of the Bill. During coastal surveillance operations, a number of unauthorised landings in Australia have been reported. In the main, these breaches of quarantine legislation have been by foreign fishing vessels. These quarantine breaches by small craft in remote areas of Australia pose grave threats of the introduction of major exotic diseases. Foot and mouth disease, Newcastle disease and rabies are some of the diseases from which Australia is free but which are well established in adjacent countries. Much of our northern coastline is backed by economically important livestock areas which are also abundantly populated with feral animals and insect vectors capable of transmitting any introduced exotic disease. For this reason any reports of an unauthorised landing evoke a quarantine investigation of the landing place and appropriate measures to minimise the possibility of disease introduction.
Quarantine actions of this type have highlighted serious practical deficiencies in the relevant enforcement and penalty provisions of the Act. Amongst other things, powers to apprehend or detain vessels used in connection with major contraventions of the Act are inadequate. Also there are no provisions which compel persons on such vessels to comply with directions regarding quarantine clearances. As matters now stand quarantine measures can be nullified by non-compliance. Further, present penalties have little practical relevance or deterrent value in the situations which have been encountered. Accordingly, clause 38 of the Bill inserts new section 75A, which authorises the detention of vessels of less than 45 metres in length or aircraft which are believed on reasonable grounds to have been used in or in connection with certain specified major breaches of quarantine legislation. The Minister for Health may, after consideration of the circumstances, direct that the vessel be returned to the master or owner. In other cases the vessel will be forfeited to the Crown unless the owner brings an action in a court of competent jurisdiction for the return of the vessel within a prescribed period and

1568 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Quarantine Amendment Bill

the court finds that the vessel was not used in connection with a breach of the Act.
The new section also empowers quarantine officers to destroy any goods which may present a disease risk or any animals or plants which are on board, or which be believes have been on board the detained vessel. Honourable members should note that at section 75C an approved person is also empowered to destroy overseas animals which he locates on any prescribed sea vessel in Australia waters, unless he is satisfied that arrangements exist for the security of those animals. The possible eScape of such animals presents an unacceptable disease risk. These new provisions demonstrate the Government's determination to provide the strongest possible deterrent to unauthorised landings in Australia and to the disease threat that these and other activities pose. The Government is not prepared to tolerateactivities of this kind which place our economic and social livelihood at risk.
Finally, I draw the attention of honourable members to clause 35 of the Bill. This clause inserts a new section which relates to the provision of quarantine information to travellers to Australia. Honourable members will be aware that unauthorised importations of animal and plant materials by overseas visitors or returning Australians are a major potential source of disease introduction. Large quantities of such materials are intercepted at our international ports of entry. Better co-operation from an informed travelling public is essential if we are to continue to maintain the quarantine security on which our economic well-being depends. As one step towards this objective the Government is conducting a major national quarantine publicity campaign to increase awareness and encourage individual responsibility and self-regulation in quarantine matters. The Government has now decided on additional measures to ensure that travellers to Australia are informed of our quarantine requirements.
Accordingly, proposed section 74AA requires the masters of all vessels travelling to Australia, including aircraft, to advise everyone on board of our quarantine requirements. The content and form of such advice are to be in accordance with approvals of the Director of Quarantine. The Bill before the House is a most important piece of legislation. It would provide our quarantine service with increased capacity to meet Australia's present and future needs. It is a Bill which should receive support from all sections of the House. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Morris) adjourned.

COAL LOADER CAPACITY
Motion (by Mr Fife) -by leave-agreed to:
That so much of the Standing Orders be suspended as would prevent Notice No. 53, General Business, being called on forthwith.
MrBAUME (Macarthur) (8.14)-1 move:
That this House-
(1) expresses its concern at the threat to Australian coal exports posed by continuing delays in the building of adequate coal loader capacity, particularly in New South Wales, and at the level of industrial disruption at existing coal loaders in that State, and
(2) calls upon the New South Wales Government to(a) allow private enterprise to build the additional coal loader capacity in Newcastle required to meet substantially rising demand for steaming coal; (b) ie-examine the urgent need for the construction by private enterprise of a coal loader in Botany Bay to cater for western and south western steaming and coking coals, and (c) increase the capacity of the proposed Port Kembla coal loader to receive coal transported by road in excess of the stated 2 million tonnes maximum in view of the inability. of the New South Wales Railways to provide adequate handling capacity for at least another 5 years.
My reason for moving this motion is very simple. There is a resources boom in Queensland, resulting in significant profits to the coal mining companies of that State and, of course, to all the other people who are involved in the coal industry, directly and peripherally, in that State. New South Wales is now being rewarded for the efforts in that State by large losses by coal mining companies, by the deferment of expansion programs, and by a lack of capacity to export the product which is so desperately needed by the coal consuming world.
Let us look at the reasons for this significant difference between New South Wales and Queensland. The most prominent reason for that difference appears to be in the capacity of the coal exporting facilities, the ports and the coal loaders, to handle that coal. There is no doubt that there is a significant difference between the two States. For example, at Hay Point in Queensland there is a coal loader which has a capacity at only one of its berths to cope with 6,000 tonnes an hour, The Port Kembla coal loader in New South Wales has a capacity of about 2,000 tonnes an hour. It is hoped that the new Port Kembla coal loader will have a capacity of up to 5,000 tonnes an hour. The basin coal loader in Newcastle has a capacity of about 2,000 tonnes an hour.
In every instance the capacity of those coal loaders is far behind the capacity of the coal loaders in Queensland to cope with the clearly evident demand for coal which has been signalled for many years in advance. It is bad enough that

Coal Loader Capacity

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1569

the present capacity is inadequate. In fact, the plans in New South Wales to cope with the projected capacity are such that there is no doubt whatsoever that this industry will be hamstrung by an inability to export the product which it will have the capacity to mine. Not only are unionists involved in the mining industry not receiving the reward that they should be getting for producing a lot of coal because the coal is not able to be produced but also the expansion programs in New South Wales are being held back by limitations on the capacity to export. (Quorum formed). I can understand the unwillingness of honourable members in the Australian Labor Party opposite to listen to a solid exposition of the incompetence and deliberate delays that have emerged from the way in which the New South Wales Government runs that State in relation to the development of its coal industry and the scope for workers in that industry to receive adequate awards but in particular their unwillingness to see the people of Australia benefit from the immense potential from coal exporting in this country.

The letter read as follows-
Dear Sir,
'As a result of Press speculation and widely 'different interpretations as to the Newcastle Sub-Branch of the Transport Workers' Union position re the construction and operation of a third coal loader, the union believes it pertinent to remind you of our position, which is as follows:
1. The third coal loader should be built as a matter of urgency, so as to facilitate the present export demand and to complement ongoing Hunter Valley development.
2. A decision on who should build, own and operate the third coal loader should be made on or before November 18, 1980. (A promise already reneged on by your Government).
3. The Transport Workers' Union will support any decision oftheGovernment (made on or before November 18,1980).
4. The Transport Workers' Union will oppose any bans lind or limitations placed on the construction of any third coal loader, or to the extensions of the existing loader, subject always to items 2 and 3 being complied with.
5. Of paramount importance is the welfare and job security of this union's existing workforce.

Mr Minister, there have been suggestions that your Government is in a 'no win' situation. Be that as it may, such a situation has been caused by your own department's ineptitude.

The Maritime Services Board has one of the worst industrial records of any State Government department. Threats by other unions to take industrial action in Newcastle and other ports if the loader is not built, owned and operated by the State, are easily countered by similar action being contemplated by this union. No coal need be loaded Qut of Newcastle. In fact, no coal need be transported to the Port Kembla coal loader, or to any rail siding in this State if transport workers are of a similar attitude.

The eventual construction of any new loader will require concrete, steel and vast quantities of buildingmaterials, requiring transport workers to be engaged if they are of a mind to go along with this farce. My executive do not think this will be the case.

Consider the present position:

Newcastle has two (2) coal loaders.

The first is owned and operated by the State. Vast quantities of coal are re-directed to this loader from the Port Waratah Coal Services' storage areas. Employment for Maritime Services Board workers who are paid by the S~ate is guaranteed because of the tonnage arrangements worked out between the Government and Port WaratahCoal Services. .

The second coal loader, owned by private enterprise, cost $96 million to build-not one cent of it public money. However, all the coal received, stored and blended at Port Waratah Coal Services is still loaded on to ships by MSB State Government employees. This latter proposal will still apply if the third coal loader is built by Port Waratah Coal Services and job security for all Maritime Services Board personnel will be preserved, guaranteed and, in 'fact, additional new jobs provided.

Mr Ferguson, when will you tell the people the Government will not jeopardise project roadbuilding programs, much needed school building construction and job creation programs for schoolleavers? When will you tell the political ideologists the truth: We cannot afford it and neither can they! (After all, the completed project will cost $500 million, if commenced within the next month).

On the other hand, if you succumb to industrial blackmail by political ideologists and expect this union to support your stand, you will find that the real fight is just beginning.

on behalf of our members who, in fact operate the most suc-

The Wran Government is suffering because of your posturing.

Mr Ferguson, it is time to support the majority of thousands

of hard working, dedicated supporters who are not impressed

by the extreme political ideologists you seem to want to

represent.

Mr WEST (Cunningham) (8.34)-The first comment I would like to make is that the Government must be pretty strapped for business to bring this motion before the House, to force us to listen to the diatribe from the honourable member of Macarthur (Mr Baume). Nevertheless, I will try to deal in a rational way, in contrast with the honourable member for Macarthur, with the points that he has raised. He raised three points in his General Business motion. In the first he calls Ilpon the New South Wales Government to allow private enterprise to manage the new coal loader at Newcastle. He attempts to resurrect the concept of a coal loader at Botany Bay. He also wishes to increase haulage of coal by road at Port Kembla.
Before I answer those three points in detail, I make several comments about the proposed expansion of the coal industry in both New South Wales and Queensland. Quite frankly, the reason these new loaders are required-they are required to some degree-is the very bullish expansion of the coal industry that is proposed, perhaps far more than is desirable from the national point of view. I refer to just a few facts and figures in regard to the industry. Our present coal production of washed coal is about 75 million tonnes a year.. We export about 40 million tonnes of that coal. The Joint Coal Board in New South Wales tells us that by the year 1990 we could be producing up to 250 million tonnes of coal and that by 1985 we

could be exporting between 75 million tonnes and 110 million tonnes.
Mr Hunt-That is if we can ship it.
Mr WEST-And by 1990-just listen to what I am saying-we will reach a high figure of between 115 million tonnes and 180 million tonnes. At present, in New South Wal~swe are exporting 12 million tonnes from Newcastle, 4 million tonnes from Balmain and 6t million toMes fromPort Kembhl-a tQtal of 22t million tonnes. Icite those figures to the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) to show the tremendous explosion in production that the Board is talking about and the vast sums of infrastructure expenditure that will be required. We ought to consider whether we can actually go that far. The Joint Coal Board is saying that by 1990 we might be exporting liP to 85 million tonnes of coal from New South Wales. That could raise a number of problems. For instance, private capital expenditure for the ~ctual project construction could amount to some $6 billion in the two States, the Board say's, over the next eight years. There will be massive export earnings to contend with. If the earnings go anywhere near those figures, we will be looking at approximately $2 billion extra by 1985.
Explosions of export earnings of this nature could cause-I will bevery brief because I have some points to make on what the honourable member for Macarthur said-a sque~ze on budgetary expenditure for social finance in areas such as housing, public health, public transport and they certainly will result in ri~ing interest rates. They could result in currency revaluation which in turn would result in preSSllre on other manufacturing and secondary industries. Unfortunately, we could eventually be forced to rely on profit repatriation to an undesirable level abrQad to mitigate the effect of possible revaluation and on a ~peedier repaymeot of loans than otherwise might be desirable. We could be forced to ignore the effect of capital outflow as a result of invisibles-such as the fact that we export only some 2 or 3 per cent of our raw materials on our national shipping line. We may be forced into a position where we actually are embracing these three effects, that we otherwise might have regarded as being deleterious, simply tokeep down the result of the revaluation. I put it to the House that the Opposition stands for a rationalisation of the so-called resources boom in the interests of producing a multi-tiered economy where we place just as much importance on the future of and employment in manufacturing industry and secondary industry in the public and services sector as we do on the export of raw materials.

Having said that, I deal with the points made by the honourable member for Macarthur. Under the disguise of supporting a private enterprise run new coal loader in Newcastle, he is supporting the Port Waratah Coal Services proposal to construct the new loader. That company currently runs a loader there with a capacity of 15 million tonnes. It is currently being expanded to export 20 million tons of coal. The current capacity of coal export facilities in Newcastle is between 27 million tonnes and 28 million tonnes. There is a capacity of 7 million tonnes to 8 million tonnes from the old Basin loader and when the expanded facilities being constructed at the Port Waratah Coal Services installation are finished it will export some 20 million tonnes, so there will be a 27 million tonnes to 28 million tonnes capacity. It is proposed that, by 1984, the new loader at Kooragang Island, at a cost of $240m, will add 15 million tonnes to 20 million tonnes capacity to the coal export facilities at Newcastle. Adding them all together, by 1984 there could be a total capacity of 43 million tonnes to 47 million tonnes, provided that the Kooragang Island facility could be expanded to export another 30 million tonnes perhaps by the year 1990. If that occurred, there would be a capacity to export some 70 million tonnes from the port of Newcastle.
I now mention the industrial situation to which reference has been made. I make the point that the current industrial disputation in the port of Newcastle is caused not through any argument about who should operate orconstruct the new loader; it is because of a dispute between Australian Railways Union shunters and the Maritime Services Board over a wage issue. It really has nothing to do with the future of the new loader. The history of this matter is that some five or six years ago, when Port Waratah Coal Services began opeations it gave priority of employment to members of the Transport Workers Union, against the interests of those unions traditionally employed in the coal industry, that is, the Federated Engine Drivers and Firemen's Association, the Federated Iron workers Association and the Amalgamated Metal Workers and Shipwrights Union. Understandably, the members of those unions were motivated by a fear that, once the old. Basin coal loader. is phased out, their jobs might go. Who can blame them for pressing for job security?
I understand-this information comes from the New South Wales States Government-that Port Waratah Coal Services has been most inflexible in its negotiations with the New South Wales State Government. It has insisted all along on 100 per cent ownership of the new facility and blanket

coverage to members of the Transport Workers Union. The company has not been willing to negotiate at all. The State Government requested it to be more flexible but the answer was a firm no. Then, through the Deputy Premier, Mr Ferguson, the State Government took a firm decision that the new coal loader would employ labour that traditionally has been employed in the industry. That firm decision has been taken and the position is that the new coal loader will be constructed at Kooragang Island. It will have a 15 million tonnes to 20 million tonnes capacity at a cost of some $240m. It will employ traditional labour. The new coal loader will be run by a company with a government equity of 20 per cent on the board and private enterprise equity of 80 per cent. Onethird of the capital cost will be raised by share capital. That is, of the $80m required, the State Government will have to provide only $16m and the other two-thirds will be raised by loans provided by the company in whatever form is decided upon.
Mr Hunt-What union is involved?
Mr WEST-They are the traditional unions that have been employed in the industry. That is the position. Despite the nonsense the honourable member for Macarthur put before the House, by 1984 the capacity of coal export facilities in Newcastle will be lifted considerably to between 43 million tonnes and 47 million tonnes.
I now lay to rest his second proposition that the Botany Bay proposal ought to be resuscitated in order to export between 10 million tonnes and 15 million tonnes of coal from the western coalfields and from the Burragorang Valley on the southern fields. That proposal is dead. It will not be resurrected. It is as dead as the dodo and the honourable member may as well forget about it. Even the Clutha Development company, 100 per cent owned by the British Petroleum organisation which is in turn 50 per cent owned by the British Government, has given up lobbying for that proposal, so the honourable member for Macarthur is simply wasting the time of this House in trying to resurrect that proposal for a coal loader at Botany Bay.
I now turn to Port Kembla which, of course, is in my electorate. Already the Maritime Services Board is constructing a new coal loader in the port of Kembla. Stage one will be completed in August next year. It will lift the capacity from 6.5 million tonnes a year to 15 million tonnes a year and, if stage two proceeds, it will increase to 23 million tonnes. It is just as stupid, just as time wasting and just as futile for the honourable member for Macarthur to raise the matters of Botany Bay and

If we are to achieve 2 million tonnes a year to the port of Kembla by road only, it will be necessary for Bellambi Coal Company Ltd, which operates the South Bulli colliery, to construct a conveyor system so it can put its coal on rail at the Illawarra line. It will be necessary for the State rail authority to transport some 8 million tonnes to 9 million tonnes a year from the Burragorang Valley up the southern line and down the Illawarra line. That is possible, according to that authority. It will be necessary for Kembla Coal and Coke Pty Ltd at Coalc1iff to continue to send its coal by rail. Then we may achieve the 2 million tonnes.

PARLIAMENTARY CONTRIBUTORY

SUPERANNUATION AMENDMENT BILL

1981

.

Bill-by leave-presented by Mr Howard, and read a first time.

Second Reading Mr HOWARD (Bennelong-Treasurer) (8.51)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to amend the Parliamentary Contributory Superannuation Act 1948 to rectify anomalies andinequities and to make certain other changes. Honourable members will recall that the parliamentary superannuation scheme was amended in June 1978, having regard to suggestions made by senators and members and to the provisions of the various Sate parliamentary superannuation schemes. Those amendments have been in operation for almost three years and, during that time, certain anomalies and inequities have come to notice.
The most serious anomaly concerns the formula for calculating the retiring allowance payable to Ministers and office holders. One effect of the formula is that some members' superannuation entitlements are decreasing the longer they remain in the Parliament. The Bill introduces new provisions under which contributions and benefits will be expressed as a percentage of the salary payable for each office held. This is similar in concept to the basic pension scheme and will overcome the anomalies and inequities inherent in the existing additional pension formula. Nevertheless, the entitlements of some members will be lower, at least initially, under the new provisions and special arrangements have been made in these cases. The Government decided some time ago that changes were required and the new provisions will therefore apply to all members retiring on or after 1 July 1980.
The Bill makes a number of changes in relation to commutation of retiring allowances. These include extending the period in which members may elect to commute their retiring allowances to a lump sum, permitting members retiring on or before the end of the present Parliament to commute without any restriction as to age and giving the Parliamentary Retiring Allowances Trust power to defer a member's election to commute if

Parliamentary Superannuation Bill

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1575

it considers the member is likely to re-enter the Parliament within 12 months. The Northern Territory Legislative Assembly passed legislation in September 1979 to provide superannuation arrangements for its members. This Bill provides for benefits payable under the Northern Territory scheme to be treated for the purposes of the Commonwealth Parliamentary Superannuation Scheme in the same way as benefits payable under the State parliamentary superannuation schemes.
The Government's attention has been drawn to certain inequities concerning members of this Parliament who have qualified for pensions under State parliamentary superannuation schemes. The Bill does not make any immediate change in these provisions but will enable new arrangements to o'e introduced by regulation when the Government had had the opportunity to consider the matter fully. The Australian Government Actuary has advised that in the long term the proposed changes should not significantly increase the cost of the scheme to the Commonwealth. The Bill also inc1udesa new delegations provision and a schedule of formal amendments to the Act. I commend the Bill to the House.
Leave granted for debate to continue forthwith.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition) (8.54)-The Opposition supports the Parliamentary Contributory Superannuation Amendment Bill 1981. The details which go to the constituents of whaJ is proposed have been provided to the Opposition by the Minister for Finance, Senator Dame Margaret Guilfoyle. She has provided those details comprehensively and has responded to aU requests for further information from the Opposition. In the light of that fact, the Opposition has been fully informed. There is no quibble about that. The matter came before a full Parliamentary Australian Labor Party meeting this week in which all of the ramifications were explained. In consequence, there was unanimous support for the proposal.
The proposal will remove anomalies. It is anomalous-more than that, it is an injusticethat people with the same service as an. office bearer-there is a range of office bearing positions in this Parliament-or as a Minister should have different retirement alowances because of the fact that some of them have served a greater period as a private member of parliament than others; that is, the longer the service as a private member in the Parliament the less the retirement allowance in respect of the office bearing or ministerial position which was held. That is a serious anomaly. This Bill seeks to remedy it.

There are other glaring injustices which affect some people who have given long, faithful and quite valuable service to the Parliament and who were unaware that there would be a change in the general provisions for retirement allowances, especially in this area, which would bring about a disadvantage for them at the time those arrangements were introduced. This proposal will remedy that. In those circumstances, we wholeheartedly support the Bill. I repeat that the proposals were fully discussed by the Parliamentary Labor Party at its weekly meeting. There was unanimous support for the proposals because they seek to remove these anomalies and injustices. It will be a better and fairer system as a result of the amendments.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
Bill read a second time.
Message from the Governor-General recommending appropriation announced.

COAL LOADER CAPACITY

Mr MacKENZIE (Calare) (8.58)-1 have 15 minutes in which to try to impress upon not only members of this House but also the people of Australia who are listening to the debate the situation of crisis which is presently occurring in New South Wales as far as its most important resource development industry, the coal industry, is concerned. I have happily seconded the motion which has been moved by the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume). Over recent years, my friend and colleague has untiringly attempted to bring to the notice of the people of Australia the critical situation which the New South Wales coal industry faces. Three years ago the honourable member for Macarthur and I proposed and seconded the discussion of a matter of public importance on the same issue.
I have read the remarks which were made in that debate and it seems that very little has happened in the meantime. The honourable member for Macarthur said during that debate, which was on 24 May 1978, that New South Wales was a crisis State as far as the coal industry was concerned. At that time he referred to the inadequate loading facilities in Port Kembla and the fact that the Botany Bay coal loader project had been

1576 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Coal Loader Capacity

shelved and that the Balmain site was totally inadequate as far as expansion of the coal industry in New South Wales was concerned. It seems that very little has changed in the meantime. I remember the words of my colleague, the honourable member for Macarthur, when he referred to New South Wales as being a crisis State. I refer to today's headline in the editorial of the Sydney Morning Herald. It states: 'Coal crisis'. In the Australian some days ago the heading was: 'NSW must get coal act together'. Again in the Sydney Morning Herald of yesterday there was a most extraordinary commitment of paper Space to this matter under the heading: 'The Coal Crisis'. Virtually two full pages of comment and editorial feature appear relating to the crisis in the coal industry in New South Wales.
We are in this motion discussing a most important issue and something that I believe should be brought to the notice of everybody in New South Wales, indeed everybody in Australia. This is not irresponsible reporting. This matter has been highlighted in the most responsible journals and newspapers that we have in Australia. Let us look at some of the problems behind this coal crisis. I refer again to the Sydney Morning Herald editorial of today, Thursday 9 April, which states:
The NSW Government faces a daunting problem in constructing the third coal loader in Newcastle which is urgently needed to cope with the mushrooming demand for the State's steaming coal in the export trade . . . The Wran Government-
It refers to the Wran Government's attitude on this issue-
seems to be in a state of policy disorientation, if not paralysis on the issue . . . If the loader is not built in time to expand the State's capacity to export coal to meet the fastgrowing overseas demand for it, NSW will not only suffer losses in export income which could run into hundreds of millions of dollars a year. The delay could also weaken the State's overseas credibility as a supplier and encourage buyers to go elsewhere, including Queensland.
Whilst I have many friends in Queensland my friendship does not extend to handing over the coal industry in New South Wales'to others so that they can export steaming coal from other States. What is happening? Again I must refer to Press comment. The Australian of 21 March this year pointed out that the number of ships that were tied up off Port Newcastle was an all time record, at least since the war. There were more ships tied up off Port Newcastle at that stage, more tonnage, than we had seen before in peacetime. That is hardly a reflection of which the unions and the New South Wales Labor Government can be proud. On 21 March 1981 some 38 ships were moored off Newcastle harbour as a result of a railway shunters strike which is estimated to have cost the New South Wales coal industry

some $66m to $70m in lost exports alone. That revenue loss of enormous magnitude has resulted because of a railway shunters strike that has been going on for nearly a month. On top of that, the ships were incurring demurrage charges of some $300,000 a day. My colleague, the honourable member for Macarthur pointed out that in the last six months the demurrage charges ,alone to the shipping companies have been about $50m.
I believe that is a very sad indictment of the New South Wales Labor Government and of the unions concerned. It is a dreadful loss of export income and dislocation of a most impoI'tant resource industry to New South Wales. The Australian editorial of 21 March 1981 stated that the New South Wales coal industry, with its huge reserves of steaming and coking coal, had the brightest future of virtually any State energy resource base in the country at the time. That is not a New South Wales paper. That appeared in the Australian. I refer again to today's Sydney Morning Herald. A headline states: 'Huge coal fleet waits .empty as unions feud'. The article confirms that a very large sea-borne fleet of bulk carriers has been lying off Newcastle waiting for coal to be loaded. How much longer can they wait?
I think this is a situation in which we need to ask the question: Who is winning and who is losing out in this matter? The first people who are losing out, in my opinion, are the employees of the industry; the miners themselves. The workers within the industry are earning very good salaries. They work hard under difficult conditions foI' it but their salaries are, on average, about $400 a week. Some are getting much more than that. I know of people who are getting $700 a week and, at times, even $1,000 a week. This strike, these disputes and the inadequacy of coal loading facilities and rail transport facilities are jeopardising not only the jobs of these workers but also the jobs of their children. It is interesting to note that the Miners Federation has called upon the New South Wales State Labor Council to try to resolve this dispute because those unions realise it is in their best interests to ensure that New South Wales coal exports are met and that the commitments to importers and to contractors are also met.
I refer to some of the effects that this strike, these disputes and troubles are having on mining towns. I represent some mining areas in western New South Wales. My colleague, the honourable member for Paterson (Mr O'Keefe), and I represent the entire western New South Wales coal fields. Some of the cities and towns in my area are Lithgow, Wallerawang, ,Cullen Bullen, and Portland. These areas are on the edge of a major

Coal Loader Capacity

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1577

development program. We see new residential developments, new shopping facilities, new roads and new infrastructure and we see a tremendous opportunity for employment. The work force in the area of the western coal fields could double in the next five to eight years if we can effectively get our export coal out of this country. The same applies to the area of Ulan in the electorate of my colleague, the honourble member for Paterson. New mines are being developed at risk and at great capital cost, for example undergound mines like the Clarence Colliery, which is the largest underground mine in Australia. Yet we see that this whole operation is being jeopardised by the short sightedness of the New' South Wales Government and the interactability of a handful of unions. I refer to what the Minister for Mines and Energy, Mr Pat Hills, said, I believe, in 1964. When referring to Lithgow, Mr Hills said:
Forget the coal mines; the Lithgow Valley will become a valley of small factories.
Of course, since then we have seen a major development in the mining industry. We have seen new industries and new infrastructure. We have seen new mimes but unfortunaltely the prophesy ofMr Hills in 1964 now appears to be altogther tootrue. It would seem that whereas I suggested in 1978 that he was wrong I might have to suggest now, in 1981, that he was right because he is presiding over the demise of the coal industry in New South Wales. His prophesy that the Lithgow Valley would become a valley of small factories, and that it would have little to do with the local industry tragically appears to be correct.
Who else is missing out? The coal companies are missing out. Indeed, there seems to be a view that the coal companies in New South Wales are making handsome profits. That is far from the case. Just recently one coal companay, namely Clutha Development Pty Ltd, recorded a loss of $5.4m. Furthermore, Clutha has pointed out that, together with other companies such as Austen and Butta Ltd, Coal and Allied Industries Ltd, and Bellambi Coal Co. Ltd and perhaps Oakbridge and White Industries, they have recorded either losses or very poor trading results in their coal operations. They pointed out very clearly that the reason for these losses is the chronic congestion of transport problems that are plaguing the New South Wales coal industry and also that their capacity of production which is well below their designed capacity has been largely attributable to industrial disputes and strikes. The Chairman of Directors of Clutha said recently that any increases in revenue was'more than offset by higher costs mainly caused by increased industrial action by mining unions and Transport Workers Union

members during the year both at Port Kembla and at Balmain. Who else is missing out? My friend the honourable member for Macarthur has provided some extraordinary interesting documents which, if he agrees, I seek permission to table in the Parliament.
Leave granted.
Mr MacKENZIE-These documents give detailed data on ships that have anchored and berthed at all coal ports in New South Wales. It gives a summary of the delay between berthing and loading. I should like to run through some of these figures because I think they are horrific. At the port of Balmain the average vessel delay period between arrival and commencement of loading during the six month period from October 1980 to March 1981 was 16 days. The delay at Port Kembla was 16t days; at the channel port of Newcastle it was 20 days; and at the Newcastle basin loading facaility it was 21 days. Worse still, let us see why these delays are occurring. The very comprehensive data collected by my friend, the honourable member for Macarthur, shows in detail why some of these delays occurred. In Balmain in October 1980 the reasons were a threatened pilots stoppage, train drivers' overtime bans, a train drivers' stop-work meeting and Christmas and New Year closures-perhaps we can do little about those closures. The closure due to the Maritime Services Board's picnic day was another reason why one boat was held up, not for just one day but for three days. Some bulk carriers have been held up for much longer. I think the worst delay was 58 days. The average vessel delay at Balmain in March 1981 was 25 days.
How on earth can a company endeavour to run a profitable operation carrying coal between Australia and Japan. Australia and Europe or anywhere else, knowing that its bulk carrier may well be held up for 27 days at Port Kembla in October 1980, for example, or for 30 days in November 1980? The list is endless. The best record for Port Kembla was in March 1981 when the average vessel delay was only 6.1 days. That was when the court threatened them if they didn't load. Of all the shipping movements over this period-there were literally dozens if not 100-there were only two when there was no delay between berthing and loading. That is why the industry in New South Wales is in a crisis state. They are not my words; they are the words of the leading editorial and feature writers of the media of New South Wales and Australia.
Perhaps I should conclude by asking who is to blame for this crisis situation. Is it the coal companies? Is it that demand is exceeding and

outstripping our capacity to supply? Is it that we do not have the coal? Of course it is not. We have millions of tonnes of steaming coal in New South Wales that will not get to port. It was that Government which sold out the Botany Bay coal loader. It was the Government that, despite the report of the New South Wales State Pollution Control Commission stating that the Botany Bay coal loader should go ahead---,-

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Giles)-Before I call the honourable member for Mitchell, I must say that after the honourable member for Hunter received a bit of advice from the Chair his performance was exemplary. Mr CADMAN (Mitchell) (9.28)-Coal is the only real economic base for growth that New South Wales has. I think that any New South Welshman or any Australian would recognise that many States, particularly Western Australia and Queensland, are blessed with a great capacity for growth. New South Wales has one base for economic growth that will allow it to share in the great advantages that the mineral resources boom will bring to this nation. The single base and advantage that New South Wales has is coal. Therefore, the debate tonight is relevant because the subject of the export of coal from New South Wales is crucial to that State's future not only in terms of its capacity to provide the sorts of services the people of New South Wales want but also its capacity to supply employment and the levels of infrastructure that will allow the full development of coal and the full development of important public works and facilities.
In the total world scene the emphasis on the export and development of coal has meant that Australia is now competing with the big league for markets. Our main competitors at the moment are the United States of America and South Africa. These two large producing nations are the nations that will endeavour to beat Australia in respect of markets. They will be the nations that will take up markets if Australia does not fill them. I understand that at the moment the United States has difficulties in respect of its massive exports of coal. The problems are related to the depth of ports. I understand that in the ports of Hampton Road and Mobile the maximum tonnage of ships that can take on coal is 18,000 tonnes. However, a port development program has already commenced in the United States and within two years these ports will be capable of handling 100 million tonnes of coal. In fact it is predicted that from the coalfields of America-from already developed sites-it is possible in a very short time to bring on line a total of 100 million tonnes of coal. Within two years, our major competitors can take the markets that Australia is presently fighting for.

Coal Loader Capacity

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1581

To give the House some idea of what these markets may mean, I mention that France has just completed the first of 23 1,000 megawatt coalfired electricity producing facilities; and, when the 23 come on stream, it is estimated that France will need yearly something like 40 million tonnes of coal. This year France is only importing 12 million tonnes. So we see a growth as France develops its coal-fired facilities for the production of electricity with an increase from 12 million tonnes to 40 million tonnes of coal.
Italy, a country that people consider will not advance, is in fact planning to use 60 million tonnes of coal by 1990, a short nine years away. The sums required by the French and the Italians total something like 100 million tonnes, an amount of coal that can be readily supplied by the United States within the short period of two years. That is the sort of competition that Australia faces. Those are the sorts of pressures that New South Wales faces on jobs, infrastructure and our capacity to trade. At the moment, the United States and Canada are very successfully taking part of the Australian-Japanese market. The United States already exports 15 million tonnes of coal to Japan and Canada exports 3 million tonnes. These are sensitive markets that Australians need to safeguard. In each of the last three years, New South Wales has lost $70m through lack of capacity, lack of planning and union activity. In three years, the citizens and industry of the State have been denied $210m. This is the start of a trend that is emphasised by the industrial disputes and difficulties being experienced in Newcastle.
Loaders in New South Wales and the ports of New South Wales are fully committed. They are at capacity when one considers the requirements that have been placed on them by contract& that must be fulfilled. We are looking at a difficult situation in the State where our competitors will fill markets unless we are reliable, and take jobs away unless we can meet our commitments. We are looking at circumstances where, within two years, unless New South Wales gets its act together, its major capacity for economic expansion will be gone. I think that these difficulties are accentuated by the stupidity that we see going on between men seeking to get some small share of this development. I think that a proper consideration of this matter would indicate that the prospects for everyone are substantial.
To give the House some idea of those people who are involved in the dispute at Newcastle, which has tied up 38 ships and which is costing perhaps something like $12,000 a day, I mention that among the unions involved are the Federated

Engine Drivers and Firemen's Association of Australasia, the Federated Iron workers Association of Australia, the Electrical Trades Union of Australia, the Amalgamated Metal Workers and Shipwrights Union, the Maritime Services Board Officers Association, the Foremen Stevedores Association of Australia, the Association of Professional Engineers, Australia, the Plumbers and Gasfitters Employees Union of Australia, and some others. What a list of organisations involved on this site, all stopping the people of New South Wales having some advantage from the only source of economic development that that State possesses.
I think that it is interesting also to pursue the attitude of those who are involved. We must look at something of that port's present capacity. Quoting from the Sydney M orning H erald of yesterday's date, the port's present coal loading capacity is now 16 million tonnes a year. Port Waratah's facilities are being extended to expand total capacity to 25 million tonnes by December next year. But exports through the port, now 15 million tonnes a year, are expected to rise to 18 million this year, 23 million tonnes next year and 26 million tonnes in 1983, if the loading capaCity exists. The demand for 1984 could run as high as 30 million tonnes. At a price of $50 a tonne-it could be more-that represents a figure of $1 ,500m a year. That capacity is a threat. That capacity will be lost and, for the citizens of New South Wales, it will be wasted. We can lay the blame fairly at the feet of the New South Wales Government. Twelve months ago it was offered the prospect of a private enterprise consortium constructing a coal loader for $290m. Within recent weeks the Government has said that it can construct something similar for the small sum of $365m, which is a massive increase. That is typical New South Wales Government activity. It involves itself and does the job more slowly but more expensively. The actual cost is somewhere near $500m. The cost predicted by the Secretary of the Newcastle branch of the Transport Workers Union of Australia, Mr Barry Joy, is something like $500m. So we see the job at least one year late and costing somewhere near twice as much.
If honourable members look at the situation in New South Wales, they will see that wherever port facilities are being developed for the export of coal, they have either been cancelled or they are running late, by as much as two years. For instance, the Balmain facility is running two years late. The tragedy of this problem is that the New South Wales Government has decided that it will develop through its own offices a consortium that

1582 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Coal Loader Capacity

will include the Maritime Services Board. The Government has indicated that this will allow a capacity for those unions involved to buy shares and to involve themselves in the management of the site. The State Government's proposal is to purchase 20 per cent of the shares for the facility which will develop the coal loader. However, I understand that the proposal put forward by the New South Wales Government is that it wishes to have 40 per cent membership of the Board. For a 20 per cent equity, that seems quite substantial. The threat to the operators will be-it was expressed in the State House today by the Premier-that, unless the coal proprietors agree, they will lose their capacity to operate. They will lose their capacity to produce coal. That threat was clearly spelt out in the State House today by Mr Wran. That is the way in which this system will work-a 40 per cent union control of the Board for a 20 per cent equity capital. Apart from the 40 per cent control of the Board, the State Government will have the capacity to threaten that Board with the loss of its lease for coal development if it does not co-operate with the needs of the State Government. All of this is public activity at its worst.
We shall see within the next week Mr Wran, the Premier and Treasurer of New South Wales, come to this capital city begging for money, asking for more funds for infrastructure and loans and at the same time saying that he needs these funds for schools and hospitals. 'How any free-enterprise operation can exist if controlled by a 20 per cent interest in addition to being told who they will employ and on what terms is beyond comprehension'.
But simply, the Government has taken the first steps in nationalising the coal industry of this State and has put at risk the continuance of overseas export markets and long-term development of Hunter Valley coal interests and associated infrastructure.
It is a sad day when a State of this country has a capacity to advance but will not take it up and when, through inaction and ineptitude, it brings itself to the same level as some of the people that it so rightly criticises for holding the world to ransom over oil supplies. It would seem that Australia is in a marvellous position today with regard to energy. I am most concerned that Australia, particularly the Premier of New South Wales and his Government, may be compared with some of those sheiks in Iran who have sought to hold the world to ransom for energy. In fact, I put it to the House that Mr Wran may well be the new Ayatollah of Australia.

This motion was cooked up by the honourable member for Macarthur. I have always been very kind to him and he to me,but the man who sold shares that did not exist when he was with Patrick Partners now wants to sell us some arguments that do not exist. The electorate of the honourable member for Macarthur borders mine and, to an extent, I am aware of his statements in print. Macarthur is an electorate I once represented and Werriwa also shares the problems he indentifies with his electorate inasmuch as miners and coal truck drivers from Werriwa are engaged in coal mining in the Burragorang Valley and the mines between the Valley and the Illawarra escarpment. I mention the mines of Appin and Westcliff.
The honourable member's motion is partially a gimmick to push the interests of one small segment of the industry. I am quite sure that after

Coal Loader Capacity

this debate the Press will be full of it. The honourable member spends a lot of his time on public relations ex.cercises and attacking the New South Wales Government. This is another variation of the theme. I am rather disgusted about this. I want to make some general points on the matter. The whole coal strategy of New South Wales cannot be premised in terms of anyone small group. The New South Wales Government is a sovereign government and has every right to make up its own mind as to the best way to handle New South Wales coal without advice from this Parliament. The sovereign Government of New South Wales announced in 1978 that, with the completion of the coal loader at Port Kembla in 1982, only 2 million tonnes of coal will be transported to the loader by road. If it is all right for the sovereign Government of Queensland to transport coal by rail and if it is all right for the West Australian Government to transport iron ore by rail, why is it not all right for the New South Wales Government basically to follow a rail strategy? After all, we heard a lot from the Liberal-National Country parties in opposition about sovereignty and State rights. They spoke about how the States were being forced to do this and do that.
All this Government is doing is fighting the States at every turn on funds for infrastructure. The Fraser Government is trying to get the public relations benefit of the resources boom; yet' it and the Treasury are trying to strangle it at birth by denying funds to the States. The Fraser Government has chopped down on funds for transport. This Government is trying to force the States to impose an income tax and it is harassing and hectoring the States at every opportunity to cut down on their public services. Then it is being critical of the States if any of the services can be alleged to have declined. The motion before the House raises three specific questions relating to Newcastle, Botany Bay and Port Kembla. Honourable members opposite have concentrated on industrial disruption. We all agree that there is far too much of that. The first part of the motion before the House states:
(a) allow private enterprise to build the additional coal loader capacity in Newcastle required to meet substantially rising demand for steaming coal;
The third coal loader will be a private enterprise operation to the degree of 80 per cent. The New South Wales Government will have 20 per cent ownership. It will be built and run on the basis of 80 per cent and 20 per cent ownership. The Government is inviting responses from the industry now. There are plans also for a fourth coal loader. Already intensive studies are going on. As

people well know the Coal Export Strategy Committee-which reported to the New South Wales Parliament in March 1979-led to the setting up of another task force and a bigger committee which is known as the Coal Resources Development Committee on which there are representatives of industry, unions, local government and various departments. I will not go through them all. The second part of the motion before the House states:
(b) re-examine the urgent need for the construction by private enterprise of a coal loader in Botany Bay to cater for western and south western steaming and coking coals.
The Botany Bay issue is a dead letter. It has not been stirred up for years. I am quite amazed. There is no sense in this Parliament making some fatuous declaration about Botany Bay. Everyone in the industry to whom I speak knows that this is not an option any more. If we go back to that issue, the opponents ignore the independent Australian National University study-the one under Professor Noel Butlin-which was sponsored by the Botany Bay Project Management Committee and which included the Australian Academy of Science, the Australian Academy of the Social Sciences and the Australian Academy of the Humanities. It was a totally independent report as those academies do not have vested interests. The report stated:
The loading of coal at Port Botany appears to be undesirable for both economic and environmental reasons. The main case for expanded coal facilities is at Newcastle. The main case for improving the environmental impact of coal loading appears to be in Port Kembla where there is also a case for some expansion of capacity.
The proposal for export of coal from Port Botany should, we believe, be abandoned in its entirety and the port area currently reserved for this use reallocated.
We could go on quoting from reports. After that report we have the Simblist report which stated that the coal loader project should not proceed at this stage. These were not shoddy small reports put our by the State Pollution Control Commission under the New South Wales Liberal Government. These were comprehensive, wide, and properly based reprorts. The environmentalists were not even happy with that. They said that a lot of things still had not been taken into account. The Botany Bay region has been wiped by every responsible authority that has ever looked at it. I said earlier that the Sydney Press also believed, when the Government went along with the Simblist report, that it was dong the right thing. Let us not have selective quotes. Simblist QC. demolished the report of the former State Pollution Control Commission very effectively. He said that he was distressed by what has happened in Botany Bay. He was distressed by the

1586 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Coal Loader Capacity

government in the areas in which this is taking place.
It is absolutely essential that a third coal loader be built at the port of Newcastle immediately to assist in the export of coal from the Hunter Valley-the area I have just mentioned-where great progress has been made. Coal sales and export receipts of more than $300m could be lost if there are any further delays in plans for the construction of the third coal loader at Kooragang Island. Delays in the construction program for the coal loader could cause a shortfall of seven million tonnes to 10 million tonnes in the shipment capacity needed at Newcastle by the mid-1980s.
Such a shortfall in capacity could well result in the permanent loss of export opportunities for Hunter Valley coal. The colliery proprietors have called on the State Government to draw up plans for a loader of conventional size and design, rather than the massive loader that is proposed by the Maritime Services Board. They have also called on the Government to reach prompt agreement on equity structuring and financing in respect of the loader. The deadline for applications by groups wishing to take equity in the third Newcastle coal loader closed on Monday 6 April. Despite the New South Wales Government's inability to provide detailed financial information on the $230m project, and although the Government's long-promised prospectus has not materialised, New South Wales coal producers and shippers have agreed to apply for stakes in the controversial project. As has been mentioned, the New South Wales Government will take a 20 per cent share in the company operating the loader. The remaining equity will be open to the private sector.
The largest seaborne fleet to assemble off an Australian port-35 bulk carriers totalling 1.5 million dead weight tonnes-has been lying for weeks off Newcastle as a result of industrial disputes. The disputes have halted loading at the port, and delayed the export from Newcastle, of some $60m worth of steaming coal. They have added millions to the cost of shipping that coal.
When one travels overseas and has the good fortune to visit Japan, Korea and other countries, the first thing one is asked by men in the coal industry is: 'Why can we not rely on supplies of coal from Australia? Why can we not depend on, and have provided, reliable shipping?' For instance, Colonel Kim of Pohang Steel in Korea-one of our biggest buyers of coal-has said that because of the shipping delays at Australian ports it is necessary to stockpile far more coal than is needed.

The main cause of the hold-up at Newcastle has been the month-long New South Wales railways shunters' strike, which halted rail deliveries of coal to the port. These shunters put a darg of 500 tonnes per train on the coal that could be brought by rail to the port of Newcastle. What a tragedy it is to go up the New England Highway and pass trains with only eight, nine or 10 loaded trucks, instead of trains carrying some 2,000 or 3,000 tonnes of coal.
Another dispute which is building up could have a far more devastating effect upon the booming New South Wales coal export trade. Two groups of unions manning the present coal loaders are locked in a bitter dispute over who should control the proposed third coal loader-the Government or private enterprise-and whose members should man it. The maritime unions installed at the Government-owned Basin loader, and the group dominated by the Transport Workers Union manning the privately-owned Port Waratah loader, are threatening to stop construction of the urgently needed third loader unless they get their way. The maritime unions have already staged a series of rolling strikes which have aggravated shipping hold-ups at the port and a TWU ban has stopped dredging work in preparation for the third loader at Kooragang Island. What is wrong with the unions? Why do they not see that they are destroying the wonderfuUnclustry that this country is endeavouring to buildupr with great benefit to the nation's export income. Some 366 men are involved. The Miners Federation has called in the New South Wales Labour Council to settle the dispute. I say, good on the Miners Federation for taking this stand and endeavouring to bring some sanity to the two unions that are at loggerheads.
The Government is racing against time to expand the coal loading capacity at the port to cope with the rising demand, which could exceed the current loading capacity by 1983. One has only to travel in the Paterson ele'ctorate up the New England Highway to see the tremendous amount of coal that is coming down to the port. With more mines being operated there, the load will be far greater. The Government turned down an offer by the consortium which owns the Port Waratah loader to finance, build imd operate the third loader by December 1983 at a first statge cost of $290m. Some 18 months after receiving that offer the Government proposed instead a consortium in which the Maritime Services Board-that is, the Government-would have a 20 per cent share, but the Government did not spell out who would control it. The Government proposed that the maritime unions would staff it.

( Coal Loader Capacity
i

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1587

The TWU members have denounced the proposal. Colliery proprietors and industry associates have held back from joining it pending the receipt of firmer assurances from the Government. Last Monday was the deadline for replies to the Government's invitation to invest in the project. It is understood that the Government is pleased with the response, but coal mining industry authorities are sceptical that the response has been adequate.
Why cannot this coal loader be built by private enterprise? The coal industry and the colliery owners are prepared to finance it, yet the New South Wales Government wants to delay it as long as possible. That is the policy of the New South Wales Government, which says that it has not the finance to build the loader itself. However, it adds that if it does get sufficient private backing the feuding between the two unions will have to be resolved. Delays must be eliminated or the loss to New South Wales, in terms of coal exports, will be all the greater. It could run into millions and millions of dollars. If it is a loss to New South Wales it also represents a great loss to the Commonwealth, because we are interested in seeing that the mining industries are profitable. However, profit seems to be a dirty word on the other side of the chamber.
The port's present coal loading capacity is now 16 million tonnes a year. Port Watatah's facilities are being extended to give it a capacity, by December of next year, of 25 million tonnes. Exports now total 15 million tonnes, but are expected to rise to 18 million tonnes this year, to 23 million tonnes next year and to 26 million tonnes in 1983-if the loading capacity exists. The provision of the loading facilities at the Port of Newcastle is the important aspect. In 1984 demand could run as high as 30 million tonnes. At a price of $50 a tonne-and it could be higherthat represents an income of $1,500m a year. If New South Wales cannot load the coal it will lose $50m for everyone million tonnes by which its capacity falls short. Delays in the turnround time of ships, which currently stands at 20 days, will erode the competitiveness of New South Wales coal in the export market. While the darg by the shunters was in effect, the time taken to load a coal truck was up to 10 days instead of perhaps two.
It is essential that a coal loader be established in Botany Bay to take south western and western steaming and coking coal. With increased output to take care of export trade, it is essential that the necessary facilities be provided. There is a rail link to Botany Bay and port facilities also are present.

It is disastrous that a coal loader has not been constructed in that area. In view of the inability for at least five years of the New South Wales Railways to provide adequate handling capacity, it is essential to increase the capacity of the proposed Port Kembla loader. At that port, south of Sydney, the Government has prevented Clutha Development Company from building a system of conveyor belts to ship coal to the colliers that are banked up off-shore. Instead, the coal is moved by road, with a disastrous safety record, and transport bottlenecks prevent mines from being developed and brought into production. Coal hauled from the south and west to Sydney, within sight of Botany Bay, is then-after choking out the main southern rail line-dragged back to congest Port Kembla. This also has dumbfounded the industry. According to the coal industry, cancelling the proposed coal loader at Botany Bay was a disaster.
We support the motion that has been brought forward by my colleague, the honourable member for Macarthur. We want to see that our great coal mining industry is assisted and given protection. We have a great opportunity to increase our export trade in other markets around the world but we must have the facilities to load the ships. If we do not do this we will be beaten to this overseas trade by the United States of America, Canada and other countries that are prepared to work around the clock and to give service to the customers that they supply.
Mr LES JOHNSON (Hughes) (lO.lO)-The House is treated tonight to a very unusual debate. We are probably fighting out of our class in that we are trying to tell a sovereign State government which has a very great record how to run its affairs. It seems ironic that such an attitude comes from a Government which is so incapable of running its own affairs that it has to pull on this kind of discussion to keep the Parliament in progress. There is no legislation, no objectivity of any kind at all. We have this verbose and garrulous honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) engaging in pre-State election political opportunism which, of course, is of the variety that we can expect from here on to election day in New South Wales. The people of Australia ought not to be fooled into believing that there is any sincere intention on the part of this Government to help any State that might be lagging or flagging in respect of the development of any of its industries.
The fact of the matter is that this Government has virtually abdicated responsibility in almost every area. That is why we are without legislation. This is a laissez-faire government which is prepared to turn the whole development of Australia over to the multinationals and interests of people

1588 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Coal Loader Capacity

overseas who dominate it. I think some impertinence was expressed in this motion that is before the House at the present time. The first paragraph of the motion alleges that there are delays in building coal loaders in New South Wales. The fact of the matter is that we have had a most unprecedented rate of development in the coal industry in that State. It is a fact also that private industry does not find New South Wales unattractive by any means. If there is any problem at all it is because coal mining is developing at such a rapid and successful rate that there is a problem in getting the coal out of the ports.
The honourable member for Macarthur went on to contend that we should have private enterprise build the coal loader at Port Waratah, heaven knowns what for. Obviously a great philosophical consideration is involved in this. The honourable gentleman's attitude and that of many of his colleagues have always been that one turns whatever one can over to people who have no interest in anything other than the exploitation of other people and in making a quid at anybody's expense. But there is an opposite philosophical point of view. It is to this effect: There are resources in the ground that belong to the Australian people. That is the first factor. The second factor is that the prospects are that those resources can be exploited by the Australian people who labour in the mines, on the waterfront and on the loaders. There is a third factor and it happens to be the entrepreneurial factor. This Government takes the view that that should be the dominating factor. Because some smart people come along-sometimes they are extremely competent people-and use the money of ordinary people which they derive from the great financial institutions of Australia or the world, they believe that they are the only ones who should have a say in anything, that they should run the whole concern and that they should reap the massive proceeds rather than the people who should derive considerable welfare and well-being from such activities. It so happens that there are people in this country, typified by members of the Labor Opposition here, who have a different point of view.
Of course, the honourable member went on to make the ungainly and outrageous suggestion that we should stick a coal loader in the middle of Botany Bay. We should take into account just one of the facts, that this is the very birthplace of the nation. Can one ever imagine the Americans putting a coal loader at-what is the place?

When the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) comes into this place and moves a motion such as he did, he should look at why this Parliament, or this Government, has not taken action.
