This Government has an excellent record of handling industrial disputes! I ask honourable members to consider the recent Professional Radio Employees Institute dispute in airports i throughout Australia. It is an excellent record! I suggest to the honourable member for Macarthur that the Government did very well in that dispute. What did the Government do about the recent . Qantas Airways Ltd dispute and about the Telecom Australia and Australia Post disputes? I Do honourable members want me to go on and to refer to the rest of them? This' Government has just as bad a reputation as anyone in its handling of industrial disputes. This Government has never i shone in the field of industrial disputes. The workers involved in the PREI and Telecom disputes got practically everything that they went on strike for, but this Government set out to provoke a dispute and to cause inconvenience to the public. As far as this present dispute in Newcastle is concerned, I would love to have time to show honourable members what is the position as far as planning is concerned.
Reference has been made to the ability of the present coal loaders to handle demand. At present the port has a capacity of 18 million tonnes. Yet, in the last full year it handled 11.76 million tonnes. Why is the honourable member for Macarthur complaining about capacity? It is there. Already a coal loader is planned and the only factor that is holding it up is a demarcation dispute among unions. They are the plain facts. It is a demarcation dispute between Maritime Services Board unions and the Transport Workers Union group. It is most regrettable that they have not been able to resolve it.
Possibly we could look at an industry union. There is already an industry union at the Port Waratah Coal Services complex and there is no reason why that union could not be extended from the coal face to the port. The unions will talk about it but, when this Government talks about the amalgamation of unions, it makes it so

damned difficult that it is well nigh impossible for an amalgamation to be brought about. The amalgamation of unions to form the Amalgamated Metal Workers and Shipwrights Union was most difficult to achieve because of this Government's legislation. There is only one way to eliminate demarcation disputes-I am talking as one who has been involved in many a demarcation disputeand that is to bring about amalgamations. There are practically no demarcation disputes today among the unions involvved in the AMSWU; the disputes are settled internally, and there is no body snatching. This Government should make it much easier to bring about amalgamations.
In regard to the dispute involving the Australian Railways Union shunters, as a citizen of Newcastle I regret seeing those 37 ships-I think that was the maximum number-lying off the port of Newcastle, costing something like $2m a week. At the same time, when we find that an industry gives a certain number. of people a salary or wage above everyone else, we cannot complain if the bloke in the middle, who is on a base rate or on a rate much lower than anyone else, receiving a couple of hundred dollars a fortnight less than other people in the industry, puts on a blue. That is what the shunters' strike is about. If the coal industry as a whole had. ailocated or made availabe to all of the people in the industry and industry allowance such as some of them get that shunters' strike would.not have taken place because ,the fellows would have been getting a wage comparable with the wages of other people in the industry. That is what brought it about.
I have been trying to find out all night the result of the court case on this matter heard today. It will be terribly humorous if we pick up the newspapers tomorrow and find that the fellows involved have received a decent increase as a result of that case. There will be egg on the faces of members of the Government. I regret that I do not have the time to raise a number of other issues, such as the Federal Government setting up the coal loader, and setting up an authority to control, operate and manage these things. I gave an assurance that I would sit down in time to permit the bringing forward of other business. I thank the honourable member for Hughes for curtailing his speech to enable me to make this contribution.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

Mr CHARLES (Isaacs) (10.29)-1 thank you, Mr Deputy Speaker, for allowing me to continue the few words I began last night. Before I was interrupted last night, I was talking about tourism and how tourism is important to the southern bayside region of Melbourne, especially in and around the Frankston-Mornington Pensinsula area. The Frankston Council, seeing the need to promote tourism in the area; has been vigorously promoting and encouraging many tourist attractions, such as world class sporting events, to Frankston as one way of spreading the tourist dollar to everyone throughout the region. Frankston Council has asked the Victoriim Premier, Mr Hamer, to consider establishing a separate tourist authority for the Frankston and Mornington Peninsula area. In this move that council has my full support as, historically and geographically, there has always been a connection between these areas.- People in the bayside area are also in a different promotional area to the rest of Melbourne, especially the Healsville authority to which Frankston is presently attached. I cohgratulate the Frankston Council for this progressivemove.
The Council spends thousands of dollars each year on beach cleaning and maintenance and ree ceives Ii dollar for dollar subsidy from the State Government. The Council has worked hard at cleaning and beautifying the Frankston beaches and surrounding areas. As I mentioned earlier, several world class sporting events have been held in Frankston and several are to be held over the next few months. For example, the World Bowls Championships were held at the City of Frankston Bowling Club in January of last year. A total of 130 bowlers participated, representing 20 countries. The International Grand Prix Tennis Tournament was held in the Frankston indoor Tennis stadium in October of last year. This tournament was won by Vitas Gerulaitis. It is to be held again this year from 19 to 25 October. In March of next year the International Fireball World Yachting Series will be held off Frankston. This series will cOVer two distinct but major events,over 22 days, and it is anticipated that up to 170 boats from.20 countries will be competing.

Whilst these events have been and, I am sure, will be very popular and have generated a lot of interest and brought tourists and their money to the area-money which is then generated throughtout small business, shops; indeed, the whole community-one thing is missing and it is common to all these promotions; that is, Federal Government funds. Not one cent of Federal funds has been put into any of these projects. The Frankston Council, through the ratepayers, assisted and aided by private enterprise, has had to put up most of the money. It is a fact that local authorities must fund the majority of big tourist projects. The Federal Government, instead of continuing its hot air rhetoric on the subject and words of encouragement, should do something positive. Here we have a council which is willing to promote recreation and tourism in many ways tQ generate money throughout the community and to help with Frankston's shockingly high unemployment rate. Tourism, being a labour intensive industry, must be looked at seriously by the Government-in fact, any government-in an attempt to relieve the unemployment situation by generating many jobs, as it would. No area needs it more at present than Frankston and the surrounding districts. However, what is the Government doing? It is doing nothing.
Another area that needs a drastic boost is transport. Transport is vital to the tourist industry. Yet the Federal Government has reduced funds, in real terms, for rail projects that the people of the bayside area desperately need. They need a fast and reliable service, not one that is slowly dying because of the choking of Federal funds. Frankston, being some 40 kilometres from Melbourne, relies heavily on having lifast and reliable public transport service to get people to the area. This is especially important in the summer months. The Federal Government should consider direct negotiation and direct funding of local municipalities such as Frankston. The Federal Government should assist the Frankston Council in its efforts to make Frankston and the Mornington Peninsula a really wonderful and attractive tourist area. I commend the Frankston Council for its work and efforts and offer my assistance in any way I can. I sincerely hope that the Federal Government will look at the situation, heed my advice and assist local authorities in their efforts to promote tourism and recreation in their areas.
Mr TAMBLING (Northern Territory) (10.34)-TQnight I wish to address the House on the problems of isolation. I am pleased to note that the Treasurer (Mr Howard) is in the House. I preface my remarks by reminding honourable

1592 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Adjournment

members that 30 per cent of Australia's population live in the vast area of land outside the boundaries of the' south-east coastal fdnge. To define isolation as merely having to travel long distances to the nearest major population centre is totally inadequate. More correctly, isolation is the social condition brought about by these long distances. This seems to be an obvious statement, but let us consider some of the conditions brought about by isolation.
Communication is often a problem in the Northern Territory. Only people in the major population centres currently receive television coverage. Under the Government's remote area television program many more communities will receive television coverage in the near future. Nonetheless, the program excludes many thousands of Territorians. A similar situation exists with regard to radio. It is hard for many city dwellers to appreCiate that a large number of people in remote Australia in 1981 still rely on Radio Australia as an important communication link.
When we talk of isolation we talk of areas with little or no public transport, few roads and inevitable high costs of air travel. The report of the Holcroft Independent Public Inquiry into Domestic Air Fares has correctly indicated injustices in the air fare pricing system. The Government has since moved to redress them. In the Northern Territory people are forced to travel interstate if they wish to pursue tertiary or specialised studies. Isolated Australians do not have access to a full range of health services. Psychiatric and geriatric care, services for the handicapped, radiotherapy, neurosurgery and alcoholism services are all underdeveloped in the Northern Territory.
I have used these examples to emphasise that isolation is more than just being a long way from a major population centre. To illustrate the significance of these remote areas in Northern Australia, suffice is to say that they contain 85 per cent of Australia's iron ore reserves, 85 per cent of our uranium reserves, 80 per cent of our bauxite reserves, 65 per cent of our copper reserves and 60 per cent of our natural gas. To deny the importance and to prevent the development of these areas is clearly nothing short of lunacy.
It is essential that some incentive be given to attract people to live in these areas. The taxation zone allowance rebate could be an effective way of doing this. It is a meagre price to pay for Australia's development. I put it to the House that the current rebate of $216 per annum is insufficient to encourage people to move to these areas. The level of concessions should be

increased. A rebate in the order of $2,000 per annum would prove far more attractive than the present level. It can hardly be argued that $216 per annum adequately compensates for the disparities between living in remote areas and living in southern cities. Raising the rebate to a more realistic level will not overcome the disabilities associated with remoteness for those on pensions or other social security benefits. Accordingly, supplementary relief should be considered for people in this category. The zone allowance tax rebate is currently under review. I believe the forthcoming Budget must provide the appropriate means of adopting any recommendations and redressing the iniquitous financial disadvantages of people living in the Northern Territory and other isolated areas of Australia.
In the brief time left to me, I wish to draw your attention, Mr Deputy Speaker, to the fact that people who are new to this chamber have not yet had their photographs displayed in the exhibition area below Kings Hall. I think that is an irresponsible act on the part of the Joint House Department. Members of our electorates would certainly like to know, after five months, that.we are here.
Mr BARRY JONES (Lalor) (10.39)-1 wish to bring the House up to date on the search for the elusive nitinol. Nitinol is an acronym for Nickel Titanium Naval Ordnance Laboratory. On 26 February I asked the Minister for Science and Technology (Mr Thomson) a question on notice about nitinol. It is an alloy of nickel and titanium with shape memory characteristics. I followed this up with a question without notice of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) on 5 March asking about its development potential for Australia. When the Prime Minister asked me for additional information, I presented his office with a threemetre long computer printout with 59 citations about nitinol, none of them Australian. I want to explain something which appeared in the Scientific American in November' 1975 about shape- , memory. The article stated:
In order for an alloy to exhibit the shape-memory effect it must have a crystal structure that can shift into the configuration known as martensite when it is subjected to a certain temperature or stress and then shift out of it. In a simple example a wire of shape-memory alloy might be bent at room temperature into the form of a four-leaf clover. Then the wire is heated until its crystal structure assumes a high-temperature configuration called the beta or parent phrase. Next the wire is rapidly cooled, so that the atoms in the metal rearrange themselves into the crystal form of martensite. One can now bend or twist the wire into any other form. If the wire is later heated to a temperature above that at which the martensite reverts to the parent phase, there is an orderly shift of large groups of atoms that restores the original cloverleaf form. Since the martensite transformation is essential for the shapememory effect, alloys that exhibit memory are also known as marmem alloys.

Adjournment

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1593

In the United States, West Germany and Japan nitinol heat engines have been developed. They appear to be the most efficient form of converting low grade heat to motion with up to 80 per cent efficiency. Nitinol engines can be powered, in effect, by exciting the rotor of the engine by contact with cold and hot water. This is an example of a high technology industry where Australia could get in first if we had the enterprise to do it. Nitinol engines could have significant export potential in Third World countries lacking primary energy resources where pumping, electricity generation, air conditioning and some manufacturing activity could be carried out, powered by waste water and solar heating. In addition nitinol has vital uses in space technology, optical fibres, fasteners, surgery and precision construction techniques. What is the Australian Government doing about nitinol and other shape-memory alloys? It is doing almost nothing. I have still had no answers to the questions to the Minister for Science and Technology and the Prime Minister. In a letter dated 26 March 1981 Associate Professor N. Kennon and Dr D. Dunne from the Department of Metallurgy at the University of Wollongong wrote that they were working on shapememory alloys, not only on nitinol but also on others using aluminium and copper. I should like to refer to the amounts provided by the Commonwealth through the Australian Research Grants Committee. In 1977, $3,334 was provided; in 1978, $7,399; in 1979, $3,370; and in 1980, $2,940. Grants approved for 1981 indicate that $9,000 has been allocated. Together with support from the University of Wollongong, Professor Kennon and Dr Dunne have received a total of $35,000 over five years. They wrote to me stating:
Australia is now so far behind overseas expertise in applying these alloys that it would be a monumental task requiring huge funding to even begin this kind of endeavour. We cannot compete with massively funded overseas research. Our resources are totally inadequate.
I appeal to the Treasurer (Mr Howard), who is sitting at the table, to ensure that this is seen as a matter of very high national priority. Its scientific significance in high technology industries is vital. This Government has done absolutely nothing about it. We supply the raw materials and ignore the sophisticated applied technology. It is in the Treasurer's hands to do something about it and to make sure that it is given the adequate grant that it needs to carry us through the high technological industry throughout the 1980s,
Mr TUCKEY (O'Connor) (10.44)-Media reports raise questions of increases in excise and sales tax on the products of the liquor industry. I also am grateful that the Treasurer (Mr Howard) is with us tonight. Before coming to this House my

principal business was that of the hotel industry. I believe that this gave me the experience necessary to comment on the interests of both the public and the industry. I oppose any increase in the excise duty and/or the application of sales tax on the liquor industry. After massive increases in excise in recent years, twice by a Labor Government and once by a Liberal Government, the 1980-81 Budget predicts collections from excise on beer of $1,OlOm and a further $lOOm from spirits. That is approximately 3 per cent of total government revenue. Sales tax of 15 per cent is also collected on spirits.
State governments levy licensing fees on all alcoholic beverages. Let us consider a 750 millilitres bottle of 43 per cent proof scotch which is in a bonded warehouse. It has a value there of approximately $2. Excise is $18.75 a litre of alcohol or $6.46 on that bottle. This brings the value to $8.46. Before selling it the wholesaler must add his margin which brings the value of the bottle of scotch to $10.15. The wholesaler then must also add 15 per cent sales tax which is $1.52. The price is now $11.67. This is the price, for instance, on which the Western Australian Government adds its liquor tax of 8 per cent or 83c. By this stage that $2 bottle of scotch, having attracted an additional $1.65 of commercial mark-up, is suddenly costing the retailer $12.50. The tax component is $8.81 or approximately 420 per cent of the bonded cost. It is achieved by placing a tax on a tax on a tax. In fact, we might coin a new phrase, compound tax.
Some honourable members present would say on moralistic grounds that alcohol is bad and that higher prices will guarantee less consumption. Statistics prove that to be so. The moral myth of that argument is that the price increases do not stop consumption in the area where it is needed. When the price increases, the large percentage of the market-the moderate drinkers-decide that they can do with less. Liquor sales fall with tragic consequent effects on the liquor industry. A small percentage of drinkers-the heavy drinkers, or ' alcoholics-continue the habit. The extra money they 'need is found by reducing expenditure elsewhere, possibly at the expense of their families.
State Government policy has had another major effect on the hotel industry. State Governments, by their reasonable requirements of the district clauses included in licensing legislation, have created circumstances where hotels constructed in recent years have been large and relatively widely spaced. Large car parking areas were demanded by the licensing authorities. These

1594 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Adjournment

same governments have now discovered the problems of drinking and driving and have taken punitive steps to try to stop it. Yet the licensing legislation precludes the development of small economically viable liquor outlets spaced at approximately walking distance. Strangely, the Western Australian Government liquor tax was instituted originally to compensate hoteliers whose premises were closed in rationalisation processes. I ask whether that Government would again surrender its revenue to compensate owners of these large premises if it granted numerous licences to more closely sited, smaller venues or is it simpler to collect the tax and count the bodies.
The hotel industry is one of few industries in this day and age which remains labour intensive. It also fills a very necessary role in our social environment. It offers the opportunity for people to meet without previous arrangement and in an informal manner. This is particularly important in country areas where other forms of tax-free public entertainment are severely limited. In fact, I believe it can be said that excise discriminates against country people due to their lack of entertainment choice. The liquor industry and the Australian public believe that they have paid their share. No extra burden should be added.
Mr ROBERT BROWN (Hunter) (10.49) -One of the things that I have become very tired of hearing not only since I have been a member of the Federal Parliament but also prior to that is the constant claim that has been made by spokesmen for the Government that the States have to. share some of the burden, that the Commonwealth is doing all it can to cut expenditure and that the States will be expected to do the same. I want to give the lie to those claims that have been made. That claim is one of the greatest frauds that has been perpetrated on the States and on the Australian community by any government at any time. The most recent repetition of that claim was made by the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) at the Liberal Party's New South Wales council meeting at Maitland on , Saturday, 28 March 1981. Sir Phillip Lynch was reported to have said, among other things, that the States cannot and will not escape playing their part in reducing the public sector blight on the national wealth. I would like to indicate the reason why that claim and the claim which has been repeated on so many occasions by this Government is demonstrably false and can be demonstrated to be false.
During the period that this Commonwealth Government has been in office-from 1975-76 until 1980-81-total Commonwealth revenue increased from $18,276m to $34,471m. That is an

increase of 88.6 per cent. Of course, in terms of Commonwealth Government expenditure, the percentage increase has been comparable. Let us assume that for this current year 1980-81 the inflation rate is 10 per cent. That assumption is not necessary for the purpose of the argument. The inflation rate could be 9 per cent or 9.5 per cent. Whatever the inflation rate is, it will not make any substantial difference. But if we assume for this year an inflation rate of 10 per cent, it means that during the period of this Government the consumer price index or costs have increased by 63.4 per cent. To keep pace with inflation Commonwealth Government revenue should have increased during that time by $11 ,587m. But in fact during that time it increased by $16,195m. This means that during the period of this Government, in real terms Commonwealth Government revenue has increased by $4,608m.
Let us have a look at the effect of this Government's policies on the States. I am referring here to the total composition of the States' grants because I do not have time to deal with the breakup of those grants. If I were to do so-I will later on-the. picture would be even worse. But in terms of total grants going to the States in the form of general revenue grants, general purpose capital grants and specific purpose grants this is the position during that period. In 1975-76 the States received total grants of $8,555m. In 1980-81 they received $12.561m, which is .an increase during that time of 46.8 per cent. Bear in mind that the increase in the CPI during the period that I indicated was 63.4 per cent. In order to keep pace with inflation, the increase in Commonwealth grants to the State during that period should have been $5,424m. In fact, it is $4,006m which means that in.this year alone the States in real terms are $1 ,418m worse off than they were in the first year that the Government took office.
I am getting sick and tired of the Treasurer (Mr Howard), the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) and all of these other spokesmen on the front bench of the Government parading themselves around Australia talking about the States taking a greater share of the burden. The situation at the present time is that the States are carrying the entire burden. In addition to that, the States are being forced to enter into all of the areas from which this Government is retreating. This Government has the financial capacity to fill all of the gaps it is leaving. It isforcing the responsibility on to the States which it is increasingly disadvantaging in terms of their financial capacity to do the things that this Government expects them to do. I think it is about time we had a bit of truth from

Mr JULL (Bowman) (10.54)-Last night following the euphoria of the debate on the Airlines Equipment (Loan Guarantee) Bill most members on this side of the House went home feeling quite satisfied that at last the Australian travelling public were on the way to receiving some sort of justice in their air fares. They woke this morning to read a story in most of the major morning dailies. I quote from one story in the Brisbane Courier Mail under the headline: 'Air fare loophole closed'. The article in the newspaper under the dateline Sydney stated:
Budget airfares between Hong Kong and London, used by many Ausralian travellers as a cheap way of flying to Britain, have been banned from sale in Australia by the Federal Transport Department.
The only way that people now can legally buy a budget Hong Kong to London air fare to Australia is if they intend to travel by ship to Hong Kong.
The article also stated:
Three airlines-Cathay Pacific, British Airways and British Caledonian Airways-have been selling the cheap Hong Kong to London fares. Cathay Pacific is most affected by the Transport Department ban, as it is the only one flying direct to Hong Kong from Australia.
I wonder really exactly where we are heading in terms of what the Department is now doing to our international aviation policy in denying the Australian travelling public access to cheaper air fares overseas. This is not the first case in the last few weeks where we have seen this sort of bungling, this sort of bureaucratic interference that makes an absolute laughing stock of Australia in world aviation circles.
Another classic case has been going on in the Department of Transport at the moment concerning Australian travellers who are flying to Europe via Japan. In its wisdom, but for no stated reason, the Department of Transport decided to impose a 20 per cent surcharge on all fares sold to Europe via Japan on Japan Air Lines-JAL. This was rather interesting because Japan Air Lines tried to establish just why this 20 per cent surcharge was imposed. It would seem that the main reason was that the costs of flying via Japan were somewhat higher despite the fact that there is only 30 miles difference in the route mileage flying to London via Japan than flying on the conventional kangaroo route. It was then claimed that the landing charges in Japan were higher and the ground handling charges were higher. In actual fact they

are not. To make a farce of this situation, Japan Air Lines immediately went to the British civil aviation authority and applied for a fare to fly from London to Tokyo to Australia at the same rate as the standard fare that exists on the Kangaroo route. Despite the fact that the Australian Government had imposed a 20 per cent surcharge, the British Government gave immediate approval for the fare to operate. So we now have a situation where people in Britain can buy a fare to Australia via Japan at exactly the same price as they can buy a fare on the conventional route. But Australians cannot travel on that route. This is interesting because, after all, Japan is our major trading partner. But there we see this situation. I would doubt very much-
Mr Armitage-Are you a travel agent or a lobby for a travel agent?
Mr JULL-The only thing I am really interested in is getting a fair deal for the Australian fare travelling public. I repeat that the thing that worries me about the Australian Labor Party is that it did not have one word to say about aviation in the final report of its Adelaide conference. It was indicated at a meeting I had with the former Minister for Transport some two years ago that people buying a point to point air fare certainly had to comply with the rules to Australia but once that point to point air fare was established outside the country, it was then possible for Australians to take advantage of and add-on excursion fare that may be available on any airline to any part of the world. That was the advice that I was given. Now we have a situation where once again the Australian travelling public is being denied access to that fare.
I mentioned last night that Britons who want to come to Australia had the facility to buy a one way fare for  198. That is gross discrimination against the Australian travelling public. I think that the House should be fully aware of the differences in the setting of international air fares and the setting of domestic air fares.
Mr Barry Jones-Did you see Qantas's Pacific ad?
Mr JULL-Qantas Airways Ltd is saying at the moment that all it wants to do is to be able to get in there and compete, and I could not agree more. But why are domestic air fares approved by the Minister and international air fares set by the secretary of the Department of Transport? The sooner we get an independent aviation licencing and fare setting authority, the better. Then we may get some sense into the situation.

Due to the expansion of housing developments, many gun clubs which were originally in out of the way places are now in close proximity to residential areas. In recent months the noise on several occasions has been so bad that residents

have been forced to leave their homes and go out for the day.

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1597 ANSWERS TO QUESTIONS

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Foreign Investment in Real Estate
(Question No. 260)
Mr Humphreys asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 3 December 1980:
(1) Are follow-up measures taken by the Foreign Investment Review Board to monitor real estate acquisitions by foreign interests, to ensure that projects approved for specified purposes do not subsequently lapse into mere speculative ventures; if so, what are they.
(2) Has his attention been drawn to reports that the 50,531 hectares in north-west Queensland purchased by Brazilian millionaire, Sebastiao Maia, carries no stock and the commitment to the building of a meat works in the area has not materialised.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Follow-up measures are taken by the Foreign Investment Review Board in appropriate cases to monitor compliance with the terms of the Government's approval. The methods employed vary according to particular circumstances but primarily involve correspondence and discussion with the parties. In a significant number of cases, foreign investors are required to report to the Board annually or by a specified time on the state of progress with their proposal. For example, where approval is given for the purchase of real estate by an intending migrant, the approval would normally require that the Board be advised when residence in Australia is taken up or of any significant change in migration plans. It would also normally be conditional on the property being sold to Australian or other eligible interests if the migration plans do not proceed.
(2) I am informed that Mr Sebastiao Maia and two members of his family were granted permanent resident status in Australia in 1976. The purchase of properties subsequent to his acquiring that status would not have been subject to examination under the Government's foreign investment policy.
Cape Class Vessels: Oil Dispersant Facilities on Queensland Coastline
(Question No. 548)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:

The 'Cape Pillar' was in service on the Queensland coastline on 20 January 1981.
(2) The Government recently decided to maintain the Cape Moreton in service.
No decision has yet been taken on unmanning of the lighthouse on Lady Elliott Island. The policy on automation and modernisation of lighthouses is a long standing one. The Government's current program is based on recommendations set out in the Summers Report and reflects operational, technical and economic considerations. Decisions to unman particular lighthouses are made after evaluation of all relevant factors.
The capacity of my Department to respond to incidents in the Great Barrier Reef area has not been affected.
Poverty Line Figures
(Question No. 554)
Dr Klugman asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
When will poverty line figures based on recent Australian household expenditure figures be available.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The Social Welfare Policy Secretariat's report on poverty is expected to be completed during the first half of 1981, and will incorporate information on income relativities.
Discounting of International Air Fares
(Question No. 611)
Mr Jull asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 25 February 1981:
(1) How many (a) prosecutions have been launched and (b) convictions recorded in respect to (i) Qantas, (ii) overseas airlines operating in Australia and (iii) travel agents in Austalia, for illegal discounting of international air fares in each year since 1972.
(2) Which (a) overseas airlines and (b) travel agents have been involved in these cases.

(I) How many 'Cape' Class vessels fitted with bulk oil dispersant facilities were in-service on the Queensland coastline as at 20 January 1981.
(2) In the light of his Department's decision that the M.V. Cape Moreton is now surplus to requirements and of proposals to de-man and automate some lighthouses (e.g. that on Lady Elliott Island) what will be the capability of his Department to respond to either shipping collision or grounding of oil tankers in the event of these incidents occurring along shipping lanes traversing the Great Barrier Reef.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) There are three 'Cape' Class vessels in my Department's fleet-'Cape Don', 'Cape Moreton' and 'Cape Pillar'. All three vessels are fitted with oil dispersant equipment, as is a smaller vessel, the 'M.V. Lumen'.

Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
In responding to the honourable member's question may I firstly point out that although he requested information dating back to 1972, Air Navigation Regulation 106A did not come into effect until 1 April 1976-1 shall therefore confine my response to the period commencing I April 1976.
(1) (i) No prosecutions have been launched or conviction recorded against Qantas for the illegal discounting of international air fares in Australia.

Italian Earthquake Disaster
(Question No. 637)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 26 February 1981:
What financial arrangements are being provided for refugees from the Italian earthquake disaster who wish to settle in Australia.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Australia's response to the Italian earthquake disaster was immediate. Significant migration concessions were introduced from 25 November 1980.
Sponsorships were accepted from any relative in Australia and the minimum residential requirements for sponsorship (normally three years for non-immediate family members) were waived. Priority was given to applications from the earthquake zone, and normal selection criteria were eased.
Extra immigration officers were stationed in Italy and a temporary office was established in Naples on 26 November to handle visa applications from earthquake victims. In some cases of hardship, visas were issued 'on the spot' and processing was left until the recipients arrived in Australia.
Financial assistance for Italian earthquake victims wishing to settle in Australia has been available both in Italy and in Australia. This assistance has been provided to meet travel costs to Australia and to help with initial settlement on arrival.
The transport costs of those earthquake victims wishing to settle in Australia have largely been met by the Italian government. For some, however, travel costs have been met by sponsoring families in Australia or the Australian Government.
On arrival in Australia all earthquake victims have available to them the full range of assistance and services available to migrants who enter under the sponsorship of relatives. This includes post arrival services provided through the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs. It also includes, for those who require it and are eligible, financial assistance through the income maintenance programs of the Department of Social Security.
Although maintenance guarantees were obtained for elderly people above retiring age, partly for their own protection as they would not qualify residentially for pensions, it has been decided that these should not be enforced and

approaches to the Commonwealth for assistance should be dealt with on the basis that maintenance guarantees do not exist.
Additional assistance to help meet immediate financial and emotional needs has been provided by the Italian Government through its Consulates and Consulates-General and by the Australian community. The Italian community in Australia has, in particular, been most generous in its support.
Morwell By-pass Freeway
(Question No. 639)
Mr Cunningham asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 26 February 1981:
(I) Has the Latrobe Valley Ministerial Council applied to the Commonwealth Government for funds to be made available to construct the Morwell By-pass Freeway, Victoria.
(2) Does the Government intend to increase the funds available to the development road.
(3) When will construction of the Morwell By-pass Freeway commence.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No.

(3) At this stage, the construction of the Morwell By-pass is not expected to commence for another five years, however, if the State Government should decide it is necessary to speed up the construction of the by-pass then it is at liberty to allocate State funds to this project towards the cost if it so wished.
Australian Taxation Office: Internal Security
(Question No. 655)
Mr Willis asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 3 March 1981:
(I) Was the Sydney office of the Australian Taxation Office broken into at the end of 1980; if so, what section of the Office was involved.
(2) Has it been established if any documents were copied or stolen during the alleged break-in; if so, to what subject did these documents relate.
(3) Have any investigations been made into the internal security of the section involved following the incident; if so, what did the investigations conclude.
(4) Is he able to say whether police officers investigating the break-in failed to search for fingerprints after the break-in.
(5) Has he received a report from the Australian Security Intelligence Organization or the Australian Federal Police concerning the break-in; if so (a) does he intend to take any action over the incident and (b) is he satisfied that a thorough investigation has been undertaken into aspects of the affair.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Commissioner of Taxation has advised that on 29 September 1980 signs were found of unauthorised entry into some unlocked drawers and an unlocked cupboard in the Investigation Branch of the Australian Taxation Office in Sydney. No evidence of breaking and entering of the premises was found.

Answers to Questions

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1599

(2) The Commissioner has' advised that it is not known whether any documents were copied but he has no reason to suspect they were. Whilst at the time nothing appeared to have been stolen, it has been subsequently established that a folder of papers and one personal file appear to be missing. It has not been established that these papers are missing because of the unauthorised entry and their disappearance has not been reported to the Australian Federal Police. The folder contains anonymous letters concerning the affairs of various persons. These letters would have been examined before being placed in the folder. The file,which may have been mislaid in another section of the office, is thought to have been sent to the Investigation Branch for a routine purpose. The matter requiring attention thore is the value for tax purposes of a house supplied by an employer to the particular taxpayer.
(3) The Commissioner has informed me that, following the incident, all internal security arrangements in the area have been tightened.
(4) The Commissioner has advised that the Australian Federal Police made an initial inquiry whether there had been a break and enter. They reported that there was no evidence of forced entry into the particular office and, based on the facts as then known, that nothing appeared to have been stolen or damaged. Because of the secrecy provisions of the Income Tax Assessment Act, documents could not be taken away for fingerprint examination by the police.
(5) The Commissioner has informed me that the Australian Federal Police gave an oral report to Taxation Office officials on the result of ~heir inquiries. No report was sought or received from the Australian Security Intelligence Organization concerning the incident. The Commissioner has advised me that he is satisfied that a thorough investigation was conducted into the affair. I do not propose to take any action over the incident.

Tax Exemptions for Donations
(Question No. 710)
Dr Everingham asked the Treasurer, upon notice,on4March 1981:
What is the estimated value of tax exemptions for donations to private organisations and relief projects (a) overseas and (b) generally.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

1600 REPRESENTATIVES 9 April 1981

Answers to Questions

Gifts to private organisations and relief projects are not recorded separately from other gifts that are deductible under section 78 (1) (it) of the Income Tax Assessment Act. The only relevant statistics available relate to gifts allowed in assessments of individuals to school building funds which would almost invariably be private organisations. For the 1978-79 income year all gifts allowed under section 78 (1) (a) to taxable individuals in the main statistics amounted to $62m of which $25m related to gifts to school building funds. The cost to revenue of allowing the latter gifts is put at about $9m.
Gifts to funds with overseas l5eneficiaries were not allowable as deductions for income tax purposes in the 1978-79 income year. However, donations to the IDEC Kampuchean Relief Appeal and the Australian Red Cross East Timor Appeal were allowed in 1979-80 income tax returns if the gifts were made on or after 1 July 1979 and on or before 30 June 1980. Deductions for other donations to funds established by certain overseas aid agencies, including the International Disaster Emergency Committee which operates the Kampuchean Relief Appeal, were announced on 18 September 1980 and will be allowed in 1980-81 income tax returns. The period in which gifts in respect of the African Relief Appeal conducted by IDEC had been deductible was also extended beyond 30 June 1980 up to the close of the Appeal. A further announcement was made on 4 March 1980 that donations to the IDEC Kampuchean Relief Appeal made during the period 1 July 1980 to 18 September 1980 will qualify for deduction. Information on the amounts allowed as deductions in respect of gifts to these funds is not available.
The statistics relating to gifts do not include amounts of donations by business for charitable purposes which can be shown to be in the nature of business outgoings and may be allowed under section 51 of the Income Tax Assessment Act. The extent to which such gifts may be allowed as business expenses is not known.

Coastal Surveillance
(Question No. 812)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 12 March 1981:
(1) What action is being taken to select an appropriate aircraft for carrying out surveillance, health, immigration, customs, illegal fishing and anti-drug operations in off-shore Australian waters.
(2) Has his attention been drawn to the reported shortcomings of the Nomad aircraft which is currently in use on a contract basis for surveillance in northern Australia.
(3) Is it a fact that the present model of the Nomad aircraft cannot operate further than 3 miles off-shore; if so, is he satisfied that Australia's territorial integrity is being adequately protected by the continuing use of this aircraft.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) .Assessment of aircraft types in the performance of varying coastal surveillance tasks has been made progressively since the current program was introduced in 1978. Currently RAAF Long Range Maritime aircraft patrol off-shore Australian waters. Light twin-engined civil aircraft are used inshore.
(2) No shortcomings for the tasks specified have been reported with the Nomad aircraft during the relatively short period of evaluation in the coastal surveillance role. Performance is being carefully monitored to assess suitability over a range of surveillance tasks.
(3) No.

Grants for Australian Inventors
(Question No. 771)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
(1) What grants or incentives have been provided for Australian inventors and manufacturers to develop improved medical aids and equipment, such as wheelchairs, for disabled people.
(2) Are such grants or incentives of a special nature because 1981 is the International Year of Disabled Persons or will these programs continue in future years.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Answers to Questions

9 April 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1601

(I) Grants have been provided under the Industrial Research and Development Incentives Act 1976 for Australian manufacturers to develop improved medical aids and equipment, such as wheelchairs, for disabled people. The secrecy provisions of the Act preclude the disclosure of information provided by companies concerning grant-assisted projects. Examination of annual reports of the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives Board shows that in 1978-79 and 1979-80 grant commitments amounting in aggregate to almost $4m were entered into in respect of industrial research and development projects in the general field of medical and veterinary science and hygiene.
Support has also been provided under the public interest provisions of the Industrial Research and Development Incentives Act 1976 to enable the carrying out of industrial research projects for the development of improved medical aids and equipment for disabled people. Public interest projects involve major government industry co-operative technology developments aimed at securing the benefits of Australian innovations for Australia. The arrangements for carrying out such projects include industrial research contracts for selected Australian manufacturers to undertake specific development programs. Particular examples of approved projects of relevance to disabled people include:
(a) A project of industrial research for the development of a micro-electronic device to be surgically implanted in patients as a cure for profound or complete deafness which cannot be assisted by conventional hearing aids. The 'bionic ear' is based on pioneering basic research carried out at the University of Melbourne and Australia is a world leader in the field. The project has substantial potential public health benefits and will consolidate Australian bio-engineering and medical implant capability established through the cardiac pacemaker. An amount of $995,000 has been approved to date for the industrial research necessary for commercial development by Australian industry; and
(b) A project of industrial research for the development of a micro-processor based training aid for the education and rehabilitation of handicapped people. The aid was developed to the laboratory prototype stage at the University of Melbourne and comprises a portable low-cost unit that is simply connected to a conventional TV set to generate displays which a handicapped person controls through a 'joystick'. A range of programs tailored to an individual's particular disability will provide training in the use of motor and manipUlative skills, co-ordination of limb movements as well as intellectual learning. An amount of $200,000 has been approved for this project.
Inventors grants have been provided under the Assistance to Inventors Scheme for Australian inventors to develop improved medical aids and equipment for disabled people.' Particular examples of approved grants include:
(a) An inventors grant of $5,000 to Mr G. Ward and Dr K. Forward of Cheltenham, Victoria, for the development of a pulse communicator to enable disabled people to communicate by morse. code through a television screen, typewriter or teletype equipment. The communicator will have application to many severe accident and stroke victims, paraplegics, quadraplegics and spastics who have suffered voice as well as muscle damage;'
(b) An inventors grant of $8,250 to Mr D. B. Nelson of Canberra for the development of an electricallypowered bathing aid for disabled and aged people. The aid allows one person to transfer a patient in and out of a bath and provides benefits in terms of greater privacy and independence;

(c) An inventors grant of$I,400 to Mr K. Caldow of Dandenong, Victoria, for the development of an ambulance trolley-stretcher which can be adjusted into a Hat or covered stretcher or a wheelchair. The trolleystretcher can be adapted to virtually any patient requirement and will provide for greater handling convenience;
(d) Inventors grants of $7,765 to Mr R. E. McDonnell of Canberra for developments associated with improved methods. of collection for Australian colostomy patients. The developments are considered to have significant potential to alleviate suffering amongst such patients.
(2) No. The Industrial Research and Development Incentives Scheme is to continue in future years. The Assistance to Inventors Scheme is to be reviewed in the light of experience gained from pilot innovation programs which have been implemented to evaluate longer-term policy options for the promotion of Australian invention and innovation.

Innovative Housing Projects
(Question No. 931)
Mr Mildren asked the Minister for Housing and Construction, upon notice, on 26 March 1981:
(1) What was the amount allocated in the last Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement for Innovative Housing Projects.
(2) Will the 1981 Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement also include an allocation towards Innovative Housing Projects; if not, why not.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) No allocation of funds was specifically earmarked for innovative housing projects under the 1978 Commonwealth State Housing Agreement. The 1978 Agreement provides the States with a considerable flexibility to determine the uses to which rental and home purchase assistance funds can be put. These purposes included a full range of innovative approaches to home ownership and rental housing including interest subsidy schemes, spot buying and leasing of rental accommodation, joint ventures with the private sector and the involvement of local government and voluntary organisations..
(2) After careful consideration of State views the Commonwealth has decided that the same approach will be followed in the 1981 Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement.

There will be no specific earmarked allocation of funds for innovative projects. Instead the proposed Agreement will continue to allow each State maximum flexibility and autonomy in working out its own programs. Purposes for which funds may be used will continue the flexibility of the 1978 Agreement and States will be able to fund the full range of innovative projects if they so wish.
